# Keeper League

# Chapter 1

Nate Griggs heard the house before he saw it. The driveway was a quarter-mile of gravel through pines, and somewhere up ahead Tommy Renaldi was laughing, big and open-throated. Nate had heard that laugh carry across practice fields and parking lots; apparently it carried through rural Minnesota woods too, because he could hear it over his engine and the podcast ad for a mattress company he hadn't bothered to pause.

Nate parked behind Cliff's Audi. The Audi was new, or new-looking. Cliff's cars were always new-looking even when they weren't. Nate grabbed his duffel, his mic bag, and the thirty-rack of Hamm's that was his annual contribution to the weekend. Martin stocked the house with Lagavulin and craft beer, but Nate brought the Hamm's because he'd brought the Hamm's since 2001, and some bits were sacred even when the beer wasn't.

The lake house was an A-frame on Elbow Lake, pine-paneled, with a deck wrapping around the lake side. Martin's parents had bought it in the eighties, and Martin got it in the divorce five years back. Linda had never contested the house because she'd always hated the drive from the Cities. Twenty-five Septembers they'd been coming here. The same nail held the draft board on the wall, the same third stair creaked, the same smell of wood smoke and lake damp lived in the pine. The league was the excuse. The house was the thing.

Nate let himself in and walked into noise. Tommy was in the great room with a beer in one hand and Doug Whitfield's shoulder in the other, telling a story about a cornerback who'd transferred to his school. Paul Decker was at the kitchen table with a drink, overnight bag squared against the wall like it was awaiting inspection. Martin was at the counter unwrapping deli paper from a stack of sandwiches he'd driven to Dent to get, because Martin always drove to Dent, because Martin could not allow a person to be hungry in his house.

"Griggsy," Martin said, pointing with a mustard-smeared knife. "Thirty-rack goes on the counter, your room's the same, towels are on the bed."

"I didn't even get to put my bag down."

"Towels on the bed, room's the same, beer on the counter. Different order, same information."

Nate dropped the Hamm's on the counter. Martin was already shifting bottles aside to make room, lining the necks up as he went as if a cluttered counter reflected on him personally. Martin was six-one, stocky, with a ruddy face and a receding hairline he'd stopped fighting about three years back. The man wore a flannel that cost a hundred and fifty dollars and looked like it cost twelve, which was Martin's whole approach to possessions: expensive things made to seem careless, because caring visibly about what you owned was a vulnerability Martin didn't permit himself. He had big hands and a gap between his front teeth when he smiled, which he was doing now as he reached for the Hamm's.

"Tommy," Nate said, nodding toward the great room. "What inning are we in?"

"The cornerback. The one who can't catch." Paul took a sip. "I think we're approaching the father who calls the superintendent."

"Classic."

Tommy hadn't noticed Nate yet, or had noticed and didn't care. The shoulder he was gripping belonged to Doug, who stood in the kitchen doorway letting Tommy hang on him and waiting for the story to burn itself out. Doug had lost weight. More than the usual. His face was thinner, the hoodie hung wrong on him, and the bags under his eyes looked permanent. But he was leaning into the room instead of away from it, smiling at Tommy's story with his whole face. He'd driven three hours for this, and Nate could see it.

Nate caught his eye and raised a hand. Doug waved back.

"You need anything?" Doug asked.

"A beer and a time machine."

"I can do one of those."

"He needs both," Tommy said, finally breaking off. Tommy clapped Nate on the back hard enough to move him. "Griggs. Holy shit, that hat. You're still wearing that hat?"

The podcast-branded cap. GRIGGS ON THE GRID in white block letters. His listeners had collectively paid for a hundred of them, which meant he'd sold twenty-three.

"Content is content."

"It's a garbage fucking hat is what it is. You look like you're sponsoring yourself." Tommy grinned. Six-two and still built like something functional, crew cut graying at the temples, hands that were always gripping something. A beer, a shoulder, the back of his own neck. "I'm kidding. Sort of. Still a bad hat."

Cliff Haywood came downstairs in pressed chinos and a collared polo, like he'd confused the lake house with a client dinner. His handshake was firm enough that Nate felt it in his elbow. Silver hair, permanent tan, shoulders set so square Nate had to resist straightening his own.

"Nate. Good drive up?"

"Cliff. Are those new pants?"

"These are pants."

"Those are pants the way your car out there is just a car."

Cliff smiled, the automatic one, the sales-floor one, the one that activated before his face decided anything, and moved to the counter. "Kess, tell me you went to Lindemann's. Tell me you got the turkey club."

"Two turkey clubs. You ate one and a half last year."

"I ate one. Paul had the other half."

"To be fair," Paul said, "that's accurate."

"You had one and a half." Martin tapped his temple. "I remember everything that happens in this kitchen."

"Including our annual act of financial degeneracy," Nate said.

"Especially that. Around one in the morning you're doing the loser bet with me in here, same as always, so don't pass out in the basement and claim confusion."

"I've honored the ritual every year."

"Last year you tried to shake on it at breakfast."

"That was a provisional handshake."

"That was fraud."

Joel Beckman was the last one Nate registered, and only because Joel wasn't doing anything registrable. Joel was leaning against the wall near the hallway, arms crossed, beer untouched in his hand. At first glance he looked the same: lean, wiry, sharp features, those quick eyes that had made him the best poker player in the group for a decade. But something was wrong. His jaw was set. When someone looked at him, the smile came late by half a beat, like it was being translated from a language Joel wasn't currently speaking.

Nate couldn't place it. Joel was always a little wound up. The public defender in him ran cross-examination in the background even when nobody had asked for it. This was different. It looked like Joel had already had a fight nobody else had been invited to.

"Beck," Nate said. "You good?"

"Fine." Joel pushed off the wall. "You want a beer?"

Joel was already headed for the beer before Nate could answer, and the question was dead.

Martin caught Nate's eye across the kitchen. A small shrug. Don't push it.

The house filled up the way it always did over the next hour. Duffel bags dropped in rooms, the third stair creaked under everyone who went up, Martin circulated making sure drinks were topped off and food stayed accessible. Nate clipped his podcast mic to his phone and propped it against the chip bowl on the kitchen counter. Not recording yet, he was only testing levels. He'd grab audio during the draft later: the arguments, the trash talk, the ambient sound of seven guys treating a fantasy football league like it mattered more than their mortgages. His listeners expected the draft-night episode. All hundred and twelve of them.

"You bringing that fucking thing again?" Tommy said, eyeing the mic.

"Content is content."

"Last year you put my voice on there and some shithead kid in Ohio said my zone defense was garbage."

"He wasn't wrong."

"He was twelve, Griggs. Twelve."

"Wise beyond his years."

Tommy threw a chip at him, and the evening had started.

---

The draft board went up at six. Martin hung it on the same nail in the great room wall he'd been using since 2004, when the nail had replaced the thumbtack that had replaced the masking tape that had replaced the year they just wrote picks on the back of a pizza box. The board was laminated whiteboard now, with columns for each team and rows for rounds. Martin had pre-printed the player rankings on a separate sheet and taped it beside the board. Color-coded markers waited on the table. The sight of all that order settled Nate a little. Once the board was up, the weekend always knew what it was.

Since the incident of 2016, when Tommy and Cliff had both used blue and the third round became unreadable and the argument about it lasted, intermittently, until April, Martin brought color-coded markers every year.

"Red's mine," Martin said, uncapping one and writing KESSLER at the top of the first column. "Pick order's in the bowl."

He set a ceramic bowl on the table with seven folded slips of paper inside. Martin had cut the paper, written the numbers, folded them identically, because Martin folded things identically. The draft order was random, but the randomness belonged to Martin. Martin produced the system, ran it, and never submitted it to review. Nobody had ever accused him of rigging it. Nobody had ever verified it either. Nate had never decided whether that was funny or reassuring.

Tommy drew first pick and bellowed. Cliff drew second and said nothing, which meant he was doing math. Doug drew third and said, "Sure, that works." Paul drew fourth and nodded. Nate drew fifth and made a sound that Tommy later described on the podcast clip as "a walrus being told bad news." Joel drew sixth without comment. Seventh was Martin's by default, the last slot, the host's slot, the one that looked like sacrifice, and nobody pointed out that the man who controlled the paper and the bowl had landed there again.

Nate hit record on his phone. The red light steadied him. If he was capturing the room, he had something to do besides sit there and feel whatever the room was doing back to him.

"All right," Martin said. "Tommy's on the clock."

"Don't need to look." Tommy grabbed his green marker and wrote LAMAR JACKSON on the board. "Done. Let's fucking go."

"You're keeping Lamar again," Joel said.

"Damn right I'm keeping Lamar."

"Four years in a row, Tommy."

Nate heard the old edges settling into place: Tommy dug in on loyalty, Joel on value. On a normal year it was funny. Tonight Joel sounded like he wanted blood over a quarterback.

"Because he's a goddamn cheat code. You just said he's great."

"I said he's great. I'm saying at a certain point you're paying a premium for loyalty over value."

"What premium? He's a top-five quarterback. What the fuck premium are you talking about?"

"Moving on," Martin said. "Cliff."

Cliff studied the ranking sheet for ten seconds. Nate watched the level bars jump on his phone and felt a small content-brain thrill. This was exactly the shit his listeners liked: Cliff turning a second-round pick into a financing discussion. Cliff uncapped his black marker, then turned to Paul. "Quick question. What are you thinking at four?"

"I'm thinking about my pick at four."

"Right, but directionally. Running back? Tight end? Because if you're going running back at four, I don't need to reach at two, and we can..."

"Cliff. I'm not telling you my strategy."

"I'm not asking your strategy. I'm asking your direction. Those are different things."

"The difference is the same information in a nicer package," Joel said.

Nate grinned because Joel had finally said the quiet part out loud, but he also checked Martin. Cliff's little arrangements were a house ritual too, funny right up to the second they started sounding like need.

"Here's what I'm proposing," Cliff said, ignoring Joel entirely. "An understanding. Not a trade. An understanding where, if you're going running back at four, I take the tight end at two, the running backs fall to you, everybody wins. It's an arrangement that benefits both..."

"A trade," said five people.

Cliff held up his hands and wrote CeeDee Lamb on the board.

The draft went the way it always went: slowly, then all at once, then Tommy calling someone an idiot. By the fourth round the table was louder than the house could hold. Paul was making a measured case for drafting a defense early, citing bye weeks and strength of schedule, and Nate could hear how happy the structure made him, that little insurance-adjuster hum under the argument. Tommy kept breaking in to tell him it was the most chickenshit strategy he'd heard in twenty-five years of a league that had seen some profoundly chickenshit strategies.

"A defense in the fourth?" Tommy said. "Jesus Christ, Deck. Draft a defense that early and you ought to have to piss sitting down."

"I don't think the league constitution addresses that."

"It should. Put it in writing."

Meanwhile Cliff was quietly negotiating a sixth-round swap with Doug, who clearly didn't follow it but had agreed to it because Cliff asked nicely and Doug agreed to things. Nate felt the old urge to save Doug with a joke before the whole table noticed what was happening, which was also a way of not having to look at it too hard himself.

"You're getting fleeced," Joel told Doug.

"Probably," Doug said.

"You understand what you just gave up?"

"Not entirely."

"Then why'd you agree to it?"

Doug blinked at the board like it might explain the contract he'd just signed. Nate knew that look. Doug agreed to things the way other people apologized.

Doug shrugged. "He was very persuasive."

Cliff pointed at Doug. "See? A reasonable man. This is what a reasonable man looks like."

"A reasonable man who doesn't know what he agreed to," Joel said. But he was almost smiling, the closest to relaxed he'd been since arriving.

Nate narrated pieces of the draft in his broadcaster voice, the one that made Tommy groan and Martin shake his head and that Nate couldn't turn off because the bit was how he processed the evening. It also kept him from sitting there like a civilian and noticing Joel's jaw or Doug folding smaller every time Cliff made an offer. Under the bit was the real observation: these guys, this table, this board, this lake going copper through the window in the late September light. Twenty-five years of exactly this. He'd keep recording. At least one of them would say something usable.

Martin overrode a disputed pick in the seventh round. Joel had selected a running back Tommy claimed was injured, and the table split. Tommy was pulling up stats on his phone, Joel was citing the player's own Instagram post from that morning, Cliff was proposing a compromise that was transparently another trade, Paul was asking whether the league had ever actually established an injury-report protocol, and Doug was saying he thought the guy played last week but wasn't sure.

Martin listened to all of it. Then he said, "The pick stands."

There was no vote, no debate, no appeal. Joel's pick stood because Martin said it did, and nobody pushed back because Martin's authority in this league came from twenty-five years of driving to Dent for sandwiches and hanging the board on the nail and bringing the markers and opening his house every September and never once asking anyone to acknowledge any of it. Nate felt the table give way around that authority every year. It was comforting. It was also a bill nobody ever stopped paying. You couldn't argue with that kind of authority. The argument would require naming the debt, and the debt was too large and too old and too deeply woven into the weekend to bear naming.

"Thank you," Joel said, and the word came out flat.

"Don't thank me. I just read the rules."

"You're the fucking rules," Tommy said.

"Somebody's gotta be." Martin reached across the table and topped off Tommy's beer from the pitcher without being asked.

The draft ended at nine-thirty. Martin's team was, by consensus, terrible. Seventh pick every round, the scraps nobody else wanted. Martin circled his picks on the board in red marker and shrugged.

"Worked out last year."

"Last year you finished fourth," Cliff said.

"Top half."

"Fourth isn't top half. Fourth out of seven is exactly the middle."

"Middle's the new top half. Nate, tell your listeners."

"I'm sure all twelve of them agree," Nate said, and Paul actually laughed, which was generous.

The evening opened up after the draft. The board stayed on the wall, Martin's red circles already looking like the saddest column, and the talk shifted from picks to the upcoming season, from the season to coaching, from coaching to old stories polished by repetition: the wedding where the DJ played "Dust in the Wind" during the first dance and nobody stopped him. The Super Bowl party where Doug fell asleep on the couch and nobody noticed until the fourth quarter. The year Cliff brought Megan to the draft and the group became so aggressively polite that Martin threatened to start talking about bowel health if she came back.

Tommy hooked a thumb at Martin. "Tomorrow night I'm giving you one blue pill, old man."

Martin looked up from his glass. "Why are you saying that like you're unveiling a statue?"

"Because your sex life needs leadership."

"Please stop talking about my sex life."

"Can't. It's tragic. One pill, Kess. That's all I'm saying. One. Coach's decision."

"I don't want you coaching my dick."

"That's why it got here."

The table broke up laughing, and even Martin had to bend forward for a second before he could point at Tommy and say, "You're a bad person."

"Yeah," Tommy said. "But I'm a practical bad person."

Nate and Doug went out to smoke. Doug had started again since the divorce, and Nate smoked when Doug smoked, which was the closest either of them got to a buddy system.

"Steph still not talking to you?" Nate asked.

"She texts about the furniture. That counts, right?"

"Barely."

"Yeah." Doug exhaled into the dark. The lake was flat and black beyond the deck railing, and a loon called from somewhere past the dock. "I'm glad I came up."

"You say that every year."

"I mean it every year." Doug looked at Nate with an expression too open for the joke Nate had been building toward. "I needed this one. You know?"

Nate let the joke go. "Yeah, man. I know."

For a minute they just stood there, smoking, listening to Tommy's voice through the window arguing about something that didn't matter with someone who didn't care.

Martin had gone to the far end of the deck. Through the glass door Nate could see him standing at the railing with Tommy, the two of them in what looked like a private conversation. Martin's hand rested on Tommy's shoulder for a moment. Tommy nodded at something. It lasted thirty seconds, maybe less. Martin came back inside first. Tommy followed a beat later, running the back of his hand across his face in a way that could've been the lake air or could've been something else entirely. Nobody mentioned it.

In the kitchen, Cliff had cornered Paul. Not about the draft. "I've been trying to get Martin to unwind the Roseville duplex and get my forty thousand out of it for two years. He keeps deferring."

Paul took a sip and offered nothing.

"I'm not talking about paper gains. That's forty grand of mine sitting in a dead property while one note's already tipping into default and two more come due by October." Cliff gave the room a small, practiced sweep of his hand. "He's generous as hell when it's his terms, the sandwiches, the house, all of this. Try to collect on the other end and the guy's a wall."

"Cliff." Paul set his glass down. "It's draft night."

"I know it's draft night."

"Then let it be draft night."

"Sure. Yeah, absolutely." Cliff looked at his drink, then back at Paul, and produced the same automatic smile from the afternoon. "Great night."

The evening got late. Nate lost track of the time around eleven, three drinks past good judgment and one past the ability to notice. In the basement he was setting up his cot when he heard raised voices through the floor. One was Martin's. The other was Joel's. This wasn't fantasy-football yelling. Something older and meaner was in it, and the tone cut through thirty years of uninsulated floorboards without any trouble at all.

Nate went upstairs. Tommy was in the hallway, leaning against the wall outside Martin's study.

"What's going on?"

"Don't know. Don't want to know."

"Is that Joel?"

"I said I don't want to know, Griggs."

The voices were coming from behind the study door. Nate couldn't make out words, just tone. Martin's was low and controlled. Joel's was climbing toward a register Nate had never heard from him in twenty-five years.

Paul appeared at the top of the stairs. "What the hell is that?"

"Joel and Martin," Tommy said. "Been going at it for ten fucking minutes."

The study door opened. Joel came out like he'd been launched, face white, jaw locked, eyes registering the three men in the hallway without any change in expression whatsoever.

"What was that about?" Paul asked.

"Nothing."

"Joel. That wasn't nothing."

"I said it's handled." Joel went straight for the front door and out onto the porch. The screen door slapped shut behind him.

Paul looked into the study. The door was still open. Martin was standing behind his desk with both hands flat on the surface, breathing through his nose.

"Martin?"

"Go to bed, Paul."

"You need anything?"

"I need you to go to bed."

Paul went. Tommy went. Nate went back down to the basement. The house settled into the specific quiet of men who had decided to stop talking to each other. The refrigerator cycled on somewhere above him. Pine branches scraped once against the siding and quit.

Nate lay on his cot and stared at the ceiling and thought about Tommy promising to show up tomorrow with one blue pill and a whole coaching speech, and about whether Joel was okay, and about whether any of it was his business.

Sleep finally got him with the recorder still running on his phone, the mic propped against the chip bowl on the kitchen counter one floor up, picking up nothing but the house settling and the click of the front door opening and closing at twelve-fifteen.

---

Friday was the lake day. They took Martin's pontoon out, fished badly, drank adequately, came back sunburned and quiet the way a full day of water and sun made people quiet. Joel and Martin didn't speak to each other, or didn't speak where anyone else could hear, and nobody tested the silence. Doug sat in the back of the boat and trailed his hand in the water and looked more at peace than Nate had seen him in a year.

The evening was supposed to be the easy one. Nate wanted it to be easy badly enough to believe the hot tub counted as a plan. The draft was done, the league had structure, and the drinking could be pointless instead of competitive. Martin fired up the hot tub at eight. By nine-thirty all seven of them were in it, two bodies past the tub's comfortable capacity and packed in close enough that dignity had already left the deck and Nate had one knee jammed against a jet.

"I'm touching someone's foot," Cliff said.

"That's my foot," Doug said.

"Your foot is on my thigh."

"There isn't room."

"Whose heel is on my balls?" Tommy said.

"That's the jet," Nate said.

"The jet doesn't have toenails, asshole."

"Kess, you said this seats six."

"It seats six people who aren't Tommy."

"I'm a large man," Tommy said.

"You're a large man in a medium tub," Martin said, "and you're crowding everybody's genitals."

"That's leadership pressure."

"That's a claim," Paul said.

Tommy shifted anyway. Water slopped over the edge onto the deck and ran cold over Nate's elbow where he'd braced himself on the rim. Wedged between Paul and the jet controls, he felt something give in his lower back and made a private decision to address it never.

"This is disgusting," Joel said. He was still perched on the rim with only his calves in the water, holding out longer than Nate would've bet on. "This is seven guys making soup."

"Don't say soup," Paul said.

"Man soup, then. Ball stew. Take your pick."

"Don't say ball stew either."

"Get in the fucking water, Beck," Tommy said. "Your calves don't count."

"I'm not marinating with you people."

"You've known us since freshman year. If we were going to kill you with bacteria, we'd have done it in 1998."

"That is not reassuring."

"Get in."

Joel lowered himself in a few irritated inches at a time, like every new inch of water required separate approval. Water rose. Cliff swore. Doug pulled his legs in. Nate watched Martin at the far end with his arms spread along the rim, looking pleased in that quiet Kessler way that meant the evening was finally running where he wanted it.

"Now we're in business," Tommy said.

"Now we're in a petri dish," Joel said, but he settled in.

"Look at Kess," Tommy said. "Look how happy he is. Guy hasn't had this much thigh on him in five years."

"That is not true," Martin said.

"Less?" Cliff asked.

"Cliff, shut up."

"I'm just asking for data."

"Data says you're all going home tomorrow and I'm bleaching this thing."

"Bleach won't fix what's happening to your dick," Tommy said.

"You promised to stop talking about that."

"I promised nothing. I said one blue pill and a prayer."

"That's not a prayer anybody wants answered," Paul said.

They told stories, the same ones mostly, refined by twenty-five annual retellings into something between history and myth. Nate could've called the order before they came out. The year Paul got food poisoning and spent the entire draft in the bathroom, calling his picks through the closed door. The year Nate accidentally included thirty seconds of Tommy screaming about a referee in a podcast episode and Tommy's AD called him on Monday morning. The year someone, nobody could agree who, pissed off the end of the dock at three in the morning and the neighbor across the lake turned on a spotlight.

"That was Cliff," Tommy said.

"That was absolutely not me."

"It was Cliff. I saw the whole thing. You were standing on the dock with your dick out talking to the moon."

"I wasn't on the dock. I was asleep."

"You were awake enough to piss into the lake and explain compound interest to the moon. I remember because it was the most Cliff Haywood thing I've ever seen in my life."

"None of that happened."

"Some of it happened," Doug said quietly, and the tub erupted.

Doug almost never contributed stories, and Nate felt the whole tub turn toward him when he did. But when the laughter hit, Doug's whole body went into it, shoulders shaking, sinking deeper into the water, eyes squeezed shut. Sunburn crossed his nose, his hair stuck to his forehead, and for the first time that weekend he looked like somebody with no problems at all.

"You know what I love about this?" Doug said.

"The foot contact," Cliff said.

"Shut up." Doug moved his hand across the surface of the water, trying to hold something the words couldn't. "I love that this hasn't changed. Twenty-five years and this part is still the same. It's good. This is good."

Nobody argued with that.

They stayed in the tub until the sky was full dark and the water had started to cool and moths were throwing themselves against the porch light. Martin climbed out first, wrapped a towel around his waist, and went inside. The extra room he left behind felt so good on Nate's side that it almost made him suspicious. Somewhere across the lake a motorboat was coming in late, its running light a white pinprick moving slow against the trees.

Half an hour later Martin came back to the doorway in sweats and a thermal, last drink of the night in hand. "I'm done. Lock up when you're done. Not you, Beckman. You don't know where anything locks."

"I know where the door is."

"That's a start. Good night."

Before Martin could turn away, Tommy pawed through the pile of clothes beside the tub, came up with a little silver blister pack with four blue pills under the foil, and held it out like an offering.

"Kess. Friday-night care package."

Martin squinted at it. "Is that Viagra?"

"Sample pack," Tommy said. "Four of the little bastards. I'm not wasting a whole prescription on your sad bachelor dick."

The tub went up all at once. Nate, suddenly irritated he hadn't dragged the mic outside with him, memorized the little silver blister pack anyway.

"I'm not taking boner advice from a high school football coach," Martin said.

"Good. Don't take advice. Take one. Lose three. Tape the whole thing over your bed. I'm saying options matter."

"Jesus Christ," Paul said, laughing into his hand.

"There it is," Cliff said. "Targeted intervention."

Tommy leaned over and slapped the blister pack into Martin's palm. "Use it, don't use it, lose the whole thing in the sheets, I don't give a shit."

Martin closed his fist around the little foil strip and laughed hard enough to fold a little at the waist. "I'm keeping this as evidence."

"Evidence of what?"

"Evidence that you're an asshole."

"That's already in the record, buddy."

Martin went upstairs. The third step creaked under him like it always did.

The rest of them stayed up another hour. Tommy and Cliff had a conversation on the deck that Nate caught the edge of, Cliff leaning in with a beer, that particular pitch in his voice that meant he was making an ask, Tommy shaking his head and going inside. Joel sat in an Adirondack chair staring at the lake, his beer going warm in his hand. Nate thought about asking him what the fight with Martin had been about and decided against it. Whatever it was lived between Joel's silence and Martin's closed study door, and it wasn't Nate's.

Paul was the last one to head up. Paul stood in the kitchen rinsing glasses, the way he always did, the clean-up man, the one who made sure Martin's house looked the same in the morning as it had in the evening.

"You good down there?" he asked Nate.

"Fine. Gonna stay up a bit."

"You still doing that stupid loser-bet handshake with him?"

"Yeah. House asleep or it doesn't count."

"Don't record me sleeping."

"No promises."

Paul went upstairs. The house emptied out.

Nate went down to the basement, brushed his teeth, and lay on his cot. Sleep wouldn't come. The day had been too long, or the booze was too much, or his brain was doing what it always did after a full day of performing, running the highlight reel, cutting the bits, deciding what was content and what stayed in the room. Tommy handing Martin that little silver blister pack was a cold open. The hot tub was a full segment. Martin's "evidence that you're an asshole" was a closer, if Nate had caught it on mic, which he probably hadn't.

Nate checked his phone. One-thirty.

Sleep should've come.

It didn't.

He kept hearing the voicemail he'd left hours earlier, back before the hot tub, when Martin had gone upstairs to change and Nate, already half in the bag, had decided the annual post-draft autopsy needed an official record.

"Kess, it's Griggs. Official post-draft autopsy. Your team is dead. Not bad. Dead dead. Toe-tag, sheet-over-the-face, tell the league office the family wants privacy. You took half the injury report and whatever that bench was supposed to be. I'm leaving this for the record because when I bury you by October I need proof I tried to help. Call me tomorrow if you want the full film breakdown."

He'd thought it was funny at the time. Down here, alone with it, it sounded meaner than he'd meant.

The loser bet went back to the league's second season: five bucks on who'd finish last, shaken on in Martin's kitchen at some ungodly hour while the house slept. It was a nothing bet, a ceremony, and Martin treated ceremonies like contracts.

At quarter to two, wanting to get the handshake done and stop hearing his own voice in his head, Nate went upstairs, taking the basement stairs, then the main stairs, and the third step creaked under his weight.

The upper hallway was dark. Light from the nightstand lamp showed under Martin's door.

"Kess?"

Nothing.

He knocked once with the backs of his fingers, then tried the handle. The door wasn't latched. It swung inward under his hand, and the light cut a thin stripe across the floorboards.

The light caught Martin's shoulder first, the slope of his back under the sheet. He was turned onto his left side, facing the window. The sheet had twisted down around his hips. His left arm was trapped under him. His right hand hung off the mattress, fingers open toward the floor. His mouth was open. Nothing in him moved.

Nate couldn't have said what told him the room was wrong, not in words he could've put on a podcast or in a conversation or anywhere else. Maybe it was the hand. Maybe it was the stillness. Maybe it was that sleeping people still did little things under the sheet, and Martin wasn't doing any of them.

Closer in, the smell hit first, booze and sweat and something under both that Nate couldn't name but that his body understood before his brain caught up, because his hands went cold and his legs stopped wanting to hold him.

"Martin."

He crossed the room and put a hand on Martin's shoulder through the sheet. Warm. Absolutely still.

"Martin. Hey. Martin."

How long he stood there, he couldn't tell. The Lagavulin bottle sat on the nightstand with a finger left in it. The glass beside it had dried to a ring. The refrigerator hummed somewhere below him. Nate was not narrating this. This wasn't the bit. None of his usual tricks were firing. He was standing next to his friend and his friend was not breathing and the room was wrong.

Nate backed out, left the door open, and walked down the hall to the guest room Paul always took. Paul opened the door in a T-shirt and boxers, blinking, and Nate couldn't make his voice work.


# Chapter 2

Nate was standing in the doorway of the guest room in his boxers and a podcast-branded T-shirt, and Paul knew before Nate said anything because Nate wasn't performing. Nate was always performing, at the draft table, at the grill, on the phone to his eleven listeners, in his sleep for all Paul knew. But the man in the doorway was small and still and his mouth was working without sound, and that was how Paul Decker learned that his best friend was dead.

"Something's wrong with Martin." Nate's voice came out at a register Paul had never heard from him — flat, airless, like the words had been squeezed through a slit. "I went up to — I was gonna ask him about the side bet, and he's — Paul, I think he's —"

Paul was already moving. Bare feet on the cold hallway floor, the bathroom nightlight throwing that sick yellow glow Martin always left on for guests who drank too much and needed a beacon to piss by. Martin's bedroom door was open at the end of the hall. Nate must have left it open. The light from the nightstand lamp spilled into the hallway in a thin stripe.

Martin was in bed, turned onto his left side, facing the window. The sheet had twisted down around his hips. His left arm was trapped under him. His right hand hung off the mattress, fingers open toward the floor. His mouth was open. His eyes were closed. Paul would be grateful for that later, grateful he didn't have to see whatever the eyes looked like, but right now he wasn't thinking about later. The color of Martin's face was wrong. Martin was always ruddy. Flushed from lake sun, from booze, from yelling about someone's draft pick, from the blood pressure he joked about and never treated. Now his skin was a grayish yellow that Paul had never seen on a living person, and the seeing of it was what told his body before his brain caught up.

Paul put his hand on Martin's neck and felt nothing. Warm skin, slack muscle, no pulse. The warmth was the cruelest part: it meant recent, it meant close, it meant that somewhere in the last hour Martin Kessler's heart had stopped and the heat was still leaving his body and Paul had been asleep twenty feet down the hall.

"Call 911," Paul said.

"I don't have — my phone's downstairs, I left it on the —"

"Go get it. Go."

Nate didn't move. His face was coming apart in a way Paul had never seen from him, the muscles pulling in directions that had nothing to do with comedy or performance or any expression Nate had practiced. His eyes were wet and he was gripping the doorframe with one hand like it was the only thing keeping him upright.

"Nate. Phone. Now."

Nate turned and went. Paul heard him on the stairs, heard the third step creak, the one everyone knew to skip, because Nate was not thinking about stairs right now, and then Paul was alone with Martin.

CPR came next because you start CPR. That was what you did. You didn't check for a pulse a second time or stand there weighing odds the way Paul did at a desk all day. First he got one hand under Martin's shoulder and one at his hip and rolled him off his left side onto his back. The body came over heavy and slack, the pillow bunching under one shoulder, the sheet snarling around Martin's legs. Then you put your hands on your best friend's chest and you pushed. Paul's training was a weekend course twelve years ago, mandated by his company for field adjusters who might encounter a medical emergency at a claim site, and what he remembered was: thirty compressions, two breaths, don't stop, don't second-guess the depth. The practice mannequin had smelled like new rubber sneakers, and the instructor had said "you can't make it worse" with the cheerful certainty of a man who had never done this on someone he loved.

Paul laced his fingers and set the heel of his right hand on Martin's sternum and pushed. The first compression produced a sound that was not the cinematic crack of breaking ribs but a soft wet yielding, a thud that Paul felt travel through his palms and up his forearms and into a place behind his eyes where it would live for months. Again, he pushed. The counting was supposed to help: one, two, three, four. What it actually did was give his brain a count to hold while the rest of him processed the fact that the body moving under his hands was the person who had driven four hundred miles to pick him up from a motel in Fargo when Paul's rental car died during his father's funeral trip. Who had shown up at Paul's house the night after Anne's miscarriage with a bottle of Maker's Mark and a roll of trash bags and had cleaned the kitchen without being asked and without saying a single thing about why the kitchen needed cleaning. Who had been Paul's best man and given a toast that made Paul's mother cry, and Paul's mother did not cry, and Martin had known exactly which story would do it, the one about Paul falling asleep in the library junior year and Martin covering him with his own coat and leaving a note that said "you looked cold, you looked stupid, I'll be at the bar."

Fifteen, sixteen, seventeen. Paul tilted Martin's head back and sealed his mouth over Martin's and breathed. The air went in and Martin's chest rose and the air came back tasting like stale whiskey and metal. Air went in again, and then Paul went back to compressions. Twenty-five years of friendship being expressed as a rhythm Paul could not maintain because his arms were already burning and his breath was already ragged and the counting was already losing itself in the gap between what he was doing and what it was accomplishing.

Because he knew. He knew the ugly small percentages, knew how quickly a room turned from emergency to aftermath, had once had to explain that difference to twelve jurors staring back at him. By the third set he knew he was just putting force into a closed room, a forty-seven-year-old man in flannel pajama pants driving his weight into another forty-seven-year-old man's chest after the real chance was gone.

Still he pushed. The count dissolved into meaningless numbers. His shoulders felt like they were separating. Soon the effort became its own reason, not to save Martin but to avoid becoming the person who stopped trying.

Then he stopped.

On his heels, Paul looked at Martin's face, which had not changed, which would never change again, and said, "Fuck."

Then he leaned forward and put his forehead on the edge of the mattress. The sheets smelled like Martin's laundry detergent, that cheap blue bottle he'd been buying since freshman year at the University of Minnesota. Paul had seen it under every sink Martin had ever owned: apartments, the first house, Linda's house, this lake house. A stupid detail, and Paul pressed his face into it until his eyes burned and his throat closed and his nose ran onto the cotton. He stayed there with his forehead on the bed and his hands on the mattress three inches from his dead best friend and did not move for what felt like a very long time.

---

Nate came back with his phone and Tommy and Joel behind him, all of them half-dressed and blinking in the hallway light. Tommy in coaching shorts and nothing else, the scar on his left forearm visible, one side of his head mashed flat from the pillow. Joel in boxers and a University of Minnesota T-shirt that had to be twenty years old, its collar stretched and the maroon faded toward brown. They filled the doorway and looked past Paul to the bed, and Paul, who was standing now, who had wiped his face with the back of his hand and straightened up because that was what you did, shook his head.

"Jesus Christ," Tommy said. It came out the way Tommy said everything, loud, definitive, but his face was wrong for the volume. His face was the face of a man who had just been told the news he already feared.

"Is he —" Joel started.

"Yeah."

Tommy stepped into the room and put his hand on Martin's chest, his enormous hand flat on the sternum Paul had just been compressing, and held it there like he was checking for himself, like the determination required a second opinion from a man who coached high school football for a living. The hand stayed there a beat and then came back, and as Tommy looked at his own palm the fingers curled slightly and Paul watched recognition move behind Tommy's eyes that was not grief. Not yet. It was closer to the look of a man hearing a sound he'd been listening for.

"Did you call?" Paul asked Nate.

"I tried — there's no signal in the basement. I came back up and got these guys first."

"There's signal on the deck," Paul said. "Or by the road."

Nobody moved toward the deck.

Doug appeared in the hallway behind them, blinking, his sweatshirt on inside out, the tag sticking up from his collar like a small white flag. Doug didn't ask. His gaze went past the bodies in the doorway to the bed and to their faces and back to the bed, and his mouth made a small shape that didn't become a word. Then he stepped back against the wall to make himself smaller, which was what Doug did when rooms got too full.

Cliff was last, arriving in pressed pajama pants and a T-shirt that still had a crease from the fold, because Cliff packed his T-shirts in tissue paper, because Cliff was Cliff, and he stood at the back of the group and looked at Martin's body on the bed and then his eyes moved to the nightstand — the Lagavulin, the glass, the room's layout — and Paul recognized the expression. Paul had seen it on men who walked into a bad room and went straight to angles instead of grief. Cliff was calculating. Someone was dead in the room and Cliff Haywood was already doing math.

"We need to call," Paul said. The word came back to him, and the repetition made it sound less like a plan and more like the line a man throws at a room that isn't listening.

"He's gone, Deck." Tommy's coaching voice, the one for halftime when a kid was hurt and the injury was real and what mattered was keeping everyone else from falling apart. Flat, declarative. "You can see he's gone."

"That's not the point. We're supposed to —"

"What's the ambulance gonna do?" Tommy scrubbed a hand over his face. "It's a thirty-minute drive from Detroit Lakes and he's — Paul, he's gone. You know he's gone."

"We still have to call."

"He's not wrong." Joel, from the far side of the doorway. His voice had shifted into that careful, mortared register — the legal architecture engaging. "We call now, we get a county deputy in forty minutes taking statements till dawn. Six guys in their underwear who've been drinking, trying to —"

"Yeah, and if we don't call, it looks like —"

"We call at seven, it's the same deputy." Joel's hands came up, the narrowing gesture. "Business hours. Same result. The only difference is —"

"The only difference is five hours where it looks like we sat on it."

"It looks like six men who were woken up at two in the morning and handled it badly." Joel held Paul's look. "Which is what this is."

The room was quiet. The refrigerator hummed downstairs in its forty-minute cycle. A loon called from the lake, that sliding, deranged sound that tourists recorded on their phones and locals tuned out, a bird screaming into the dark for no reason a human could use.

Paul looked around the room. At Tommy, who was staring at the nightstand now with an intensity that didn't match the moment. At Joel, whose legal reasoning was sound and whose reasons for wanting it sound were his own. At Cliff, who had stopped calculating and was now looking at the doorway like he was measuring how long it would take to get through it. At Doug, pressed against the hallway wall, arms crossed over the inside-out sweatshirt, saying nothing. At Nate, who was holding his phone with both hands like it had been taken away from him and given back wrong.

Paul wanted to argue. The impulse was to pick up the phone and call himself and let the county handle it. But Joel was making enough sense to sound like permission, and Paul's chest and forearms were still wet from the compressions. His saliva was on Martin's mouth. His tears were on Martin's sheets. Some part of his brain, traitorous and trained, started laying the pieces out anyway: Nate gone for the phone. Paul alone with the body. Martin's arm knocked out of place. Wet marks on the nightstand. His own spit on Martin's mouth.

Martin's body was nowhere near where Paul had found it. He had rolled Martin off his left side for CPR and left him canted across the mattress, one arm trapped under his ribs, the sheets twisted, the pillow half off the bed. Paul looked at all of it and saw interruption. What he saw was a room he had touched too hard.

"OK," Paul said. "Morning."

The room held its shape for another minute, six men and a dead man in a bedroom at 2 AM, none of them knowing what to do with their hands. Then Doug said, quietly, that he'd make coffee, and that was enough. The group drifted to the kitchen in a slow, useless shuffle.

Paul stayed and told them he wanted a minute. They gave it to him.

The minute lasted twenty-five.

What Paul did in Martin's bedroom in those twenty-five minutes was methodical and sickening. First he moved the body again. He got his hands under Martin's shoulder and hip and hauled him out of the diagonal CPR sprawl and into the middle of the mattress on his back. He pulled the trapped arm free, laid one arm by Martin's side, folded the other across the chest, then hated himself for how much that looked like intention. He straightened the pillow under Martin's head and smoothed the sheet back over Martin's waist. Every adjustment was a guess, and each guess felt like dragging the room another inch away from what Paul had actually found at 2 AM: Martin on his left side, facing the window, as if sleep had simply tipped over into death.

Then came the pillow and the glass, tipped sideways against the lamp base after CPR. The glass went upright. At the wet shine around Martin's mouth, he hesitated and wiped it with the corner of the top sheet. The cloth caught on Martin's lower lip. Paul stopped with the sheet still in his fist, seeing what the gesture had become, then finished wiping because half-done felt worse.

Next the surfaces: nightstand, bedframe, doorknob, the hem of his flannel shirt wrapped around one finger. The motions were small and practiced-looking, which made them worse. A man on television did this after murder. Paul did it after trying to save his friend.

By the time he reached the hall bathroom, the tap was already on. At the sink, he scrubbed his hands with Martin's Ivory soap until the smell changed, sweat and old whiskey replaced by that flat clean bar smell Martin had carried from apartment to apartment for twenty-five years. Water ran over his wrists. Foam slid into the drain. When he dried his hands on the towel and turned off the light, the room upstairs was tidier and less true.

The kitchen was lit by the fluorescent tube over the sink that nobody liked and nobody replaced because it was Martin's kitchen and Martin got to decide what was harsh. Paul sat at the table with both hands around a mug of Doug's punishing coffee and looked through the sliding glass door at the deck.

Tommy was outside, pacing. Even from behind glass, even in the dark, Tommy was unmistakable, the size, the athletic restlessness, the way he kept reaching the edge of the deck and turning hard as if somebody had given him a direction he couldn't follow. Back and forth across the deck. Back and forth.

Nate was in the great room, visible through the kitchen doorway, sitting on the couch with his phone on his lap and his hands on his knees. He wasn't looking at the phone. The performance was gone. He just sat there.

Joel had taken the chair by the dead fireplace and was already asleep, or pretending to be, his legs stretched out, his chin on his chest, his breathing even. Joel's ability to shut down under pressure was either a lawyer's skill or a dissociative gift, and Paul had never been able to tell which.

Cliff was somewhere. Paul could hear him in the hallway for a moment, and then he couldn't.

Doug sat across from Paul with his own mug of terrible coffee, not drinking it, turning the handle back and forth with two fingers as if that small motion was all he could tolerate. They looked at each other over the table, and neither of them said anything for a long time.

"You OK?" Paul asked.

Doug looked at him. "No." A beat. "Are you?"

"No."

"OK."

The coffee maker gurgled through its cycle. Paul sat there and he listened.

That was when it started.

The first sound came from the stairs, a creak from higher up than the usual third step. Everyone knew that third step. Everybody skipped it without thinking, same as avoiding the hot burner or lifting the bathroom door to latch it. Whoever was climbing had remembered. Their weight landed on the fourth or fifth instead and made the wood complain.

Paul checked. Tommy was still on the deck. Joel was in his chair. Doug was across the table. Nate had been on the couch. Paul leaned back to look through the kitchen doorway, and Nate was still there, hunched forward now, phone in his hands, the screen casting a blue wash across his face.

Either someone else was on the stairs or the house had chosen that moment to settle.

Then footsteps crossed the upstairs hallway, heavy and deliberate, the stride of a big man trying to be quiet and failing because big men still walked heavy when they tried not to. A pause, then Martin's bedroom door swung wider. The top hinge gave its faint metallic moan. Martin had been meaning to oil it for years.

Someone was in Martin's room.

Paul's first thought was practical: someone's checking on him. His second came sour and fast: someone's doing what he'd just done.

Paul almost got up. Instead he stayed in his chair and kept his mug.

Over the next two hours, the house kept giving people away. Its ordinary night noises were still there, pine beams adjusting to the temperature drop, the A-frame taking wind off the lake, the sounds you stopped hearing after the first night. Over that came another layer: people trying to move through the place unheard and missing by inches.

A little after three, the front hall toilet flushed. Somebody held the handle down hard and let the tank empty all the way, the kind of full-tank rush you used when you needed everything gone on the first try. The plumbing rattled in the walls afterward, Martin's old copper pipes objecting the way they always did.

Later the study announced itself with the thumb latch. Martin's parents had put that wrought-iron thing on the door in the seventies, and when it snapped back it made a hard iron-on-iron click no other room in the house could make. Paul heard it and stayed in his chair. Getting up would've meant he'd heard it, and hearing it would've meant he cared, and caring would've meant there was any reason to think about the study at that hour.

Later still, footsteps crossed above him again, lighter now. Whoever it was had shortened their stride the way people did when they were carrying something they didn't want to drop. Then came the soft rattle of the shared bathroom's medicine cabinet. The mirror hinge squeaked. Martin's mother had probably complained about that exact squeak before Paul was born. Something small and hard hit the tile, then got scooped up fast. The footsteps retreated in short careful beats.

A little later, after his sense of time had started to fray, blue-white light washed across the hallway outside the kitchen. A phone screen, held close to someone's body. It moved, paused, moved again, then went dark. Whoever was out there needed to work a phone before daylight.

Paul kept the sounds unsorted: the heavy steps on the stairs, the hard flush, the study latch, the bathroom mirror, plastic on tile, the blue-white hallway glow. By the end of it the whole house felt handled. Doug sat across from him on his third cup of terrible coffee, sweatshirt right-side-out now, which meant Doug had changed something too while Paul was listening and not looking.

By five the house had gone still again, or still enough that Paul could hear the lake at the dock and the pines outside the kitchen window. First gray light was coming through.

When he finally stood, his knees cracked. His lower back had locked into the shape of the chair and protested the new configuration. Then he walked down the hallway.

The study door stood two inches open. On his way to the kitchen hours earlier, Paul had passed it closed. Now the gap showed Martin's desk and the lake print on the wall, tipped a quarter inch to the right. Paul noticed because that print had hung straight for as long as he'd known Martin.

From there he continued to Martin's room. The door was as he'd left it. Paul pushed it open.

Martin was on his back in the middle of the bed, exactly where Paul had put him: arm across the chest, sheets smoothed, pillow centered. But the nightstand had changed. The Lagavulin bottle remained. So did Martin's phone, the glass, and the lamp. Beside Martin's small brown supplement case, though, a clean patch of wood the size of a matchbook showed where the foil-backed sildenafil sample pack had been. Tommy had passed it around on the deck like a dirty joke; by the time Martin set it beside the vitamins, one bubble was already punched flat. Paul had registered it during CPR without knowing he'd registered it.

Backing out of the room, he went to the shared bathroom at the other end of the hall. The medicine cabinet stood just unlatched, the mirror-door floating a quarter inch forward. Paul didn't open it further. He could see his own toiletries kit on the second shelf, a tube of toothpaste, Nate's mouthwash. The third shelf looked wrong. Too bare.

Doug was still at the table when Paul came back. His mug sat empty. The coffee level in the pot had dropped. He'd turned the sweatshirt right-side-out. Doug looked at Paul. Paul looked back. Neither of them said it.

Tommy was no longer on the deck. Paul could hear the shower running upstairs, the pipes shuddering when the hot water went past halfway and sending a hollow bang through the walls. Tommy was showering at 5:15 in the morning.

Joel hadn't moved from the chair by the fireplace.

Cliff was in the great room now, sitting where Nate had been, scrolling through his phone with an expression of focused calm that Paul recognized from twenty years of watching Cliff work rooms and deals and dinners: the face Cliff put on when the numbers were bad and the other party hadn't figured it out yet. Cliff looked up when Paul entered the kitchen and gave him the sealed-off nod he used in bad meetings, polite on the surface and impossible to read.

Nate was gone from the couch. Paul looked around and didn't see him on the deck, in the hallway, or in the kitchen. Gone somewhere at 5 in the morning, busy with whatever had pulled him off the couch.

Paul poured himself another cup of Doug's devastating coffee and stood at the kitchen window. The lake showed through the pines, iron-gray in the predawn light. The Adirondack chairs on the deck were wet with dew. The propane grill sat where Martin had made burgers eighteen hours ago, scraping the grates clean, scolding Tommy for leaving grease on the drip tray, everybody laughing. Eighteen hours ago.

"Deck."

Tommy was in the kitchen doorway. Showered, dressed in jeans and a polo shirt that was wrong for Tommy, too neat, too deliberate. His face was scrubbed raw. His hands were jammed in his pockets so deep the denim pulled tight, and he stood too still for a man that size.

"We should call," Tommy said. "Should've called hours ago."

"Yeah."

"I'll do it. I'll call from the deck, there's better signal."

"OK."

Tommy stayed there another beat, gave Paul a look Paul would spend weeks failing to name, then went out the sliding door. Paul heard his voice on the deck, his coaching voice, reporting the death of Martin Kessler to the Becker County 911 dispatcher with the clipped factual cadence of a man who had called in injuries before: name, location, situation, time discovered. He'd stripped the emotion out of it. Through the glass Paul could see Tommy's knuckles whitening on the railing.

Paul looked around the kitchen. The fluorescent tube buzzed. Morning came through the pines in thin gold lines and laid them across the lake.

The sun cleared the tree line. Paul poured out his cold coffee, rinsed the mug, and set it on the wooden drying rack Martin had once hauled back from Fargo like it mattered. Then he stood at the sink with water running over his hands and cried soundlessly while Tommy's voice carried through the glass door, giving directions to a house none of them would ever walk into the same way again.


# Chapter 3

Tommy couldn't sit down.

For forty minutes, maybe an hour, he'd been on his feet. The sequence had gone missing somewhere between Nate coming up from the basement saying Martin wasn't breathing and Paul coming back down from the master bedroom with his glasses crooked and his shirt wet with whatever had come out of Martin. The microwave said 3:14. Tommy stood in Martin's kitchen with both hands on the back of a chair, squeezing until the wood complained. Sitting down meant the situation was settled. It wasn't fucking settled.

Nate was on the couch in the great room, still in the shorts and T-shirt he'd slept in, with his face gone hollow. Paul sat at the kitchen table with both hands around a mug of coffee he hadn't touched. Joel had taken himself out to the deck, which was either smart or shady. Tommy couldn't decide. Doug had disappeared back downstairs. Cliff was somewhere in the house, doing whatever Cliff did when things went bad.

Martin was upstairs in the master bedroom, dead.

Tommy crossed to the refrigerator and opened it. Tommy didn't want anything. Instead, he shut it again, went to the sink, and stared out at the lake. The water was black, the pines were blacker, and the sky was maybe one shade lighter than either. A loon let out that idiot laugh from somewhere across the cove, and Tommy pressed his forehead to the glass and thought, even the goddamn birds.

Since Nate told them, he'd been opening doors, closing them, lifting things, setting them down, pacing from table to counter and back. His body wanted a job. His brain kept handing it static.

"We should call somebody," Tommy said.

He'd already said it once, maybe twenty minutes ago. Paul had told him to wait until morning, wait until they could think straight, and Tommy had said fine because the word had come out before the argument did. Now his mouth had looped back around.

"In the morning," Paul said.

His voice was flat, flat in that insurance way of his, the voice of a man who spent his life looking at wreckage and assigning numbers to it.

"We should call now."

"It's three in the morning, Tommy. Who are we calling? The guy's dead." Paul pinched the bridge of his nose and held it there. "We'll call in the morning."

Tommy turned from the window. What he wanted was a useful sentence. What he wanted was for somebody to move, for the room to stop sitting there like this was already a story they would tell later. This was what he did. He pointed, barked, made people pick a job, and the next thing happened.

"All right, listen," Tommy said. He pointed at Paul. "Decker, when the time comes, you handle the calls. Family, police, whoever. Griggs, drink some water. Somebody eat something. Somebody do anything except sit there like a dead fucking goldfish."

Nate looked at him from the couch with an expression Tommy read as confusion and would later understand as pity.

"Tommy," Nate said.

"I'm serious. We can't just stand around with our dicks in our hands."

"He's dead, man." Nate's voice cracked. Then it dropped to the floor with the rest of him. "He's dead."

Both of Tommy's hands went to the back of his neck. He squeezed hard enough to feel the tendons jump in his forearms. Nate was still on the couch. Martin was still upstairs dead. Nothing in the room changed.

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

At the counter, he planted both palms on the laminate and leaned in until his shoulders hurt. The coaching version of this had lasted maybe ninety seconds. Long enough to bark assignments and act like volume could put order back in the room. There was no fixing this. Martin was dead. The halftime-talk bullshit had hit the walls and dropped.

Then the thought came back.

It had been in him from the moment Nate said breathing. It hadn't even been a whole thought at first, just pressure under the ribs, cold and ugly and not the same as grief. Now it pushed all the way through: the pills.

Friday night's joke had been old-man dick medicine. Actually, the joke had started years earlier. Every draft weekend Tommy brought Martin something stupid. He'd brought an inflatable doll when they were thirty, Rogaine when Martin's hairline started backing out, and once a novelty jockstrap that said FANTASY STUD and got worn over jeans for twenty loud minutes because Martin never knew when to leave a bit alone. This year Tommy had bought a sample pack of Viagra off a guy from the gym who sold them out of his locker like contraband protein powder.

At dinner he'd winged the blister pack across the table and told Martin it was so Karen didn't have to spend the whole weekend pretending to be patient. That line had started ten minutes of the usual garbage. Better stretch first, old man. Hope she signed a waiver. Somebody asked if Martin needed a Gatorade and a whistle. Martin called Tommy a goddamn asshole, laughed so hard he had to wipe his eyes, and set the pills beside his water glass. Everybody at the table had gotten one crude shot in. That was the point of a gift like that.

Tommy had seen him take one.

Late Friday, after the hot tub, after Cliff and Joel had gone to bed, after the Lagavulin bottle on the deck had dropped low enough for people to start making sentimental noises, Martin went upstairs. Tommy came out of the shared bathroom a minute later and passed the open master door on his way back down to the green room off the first-floor hall. He caught Martin at the nightstand, pushing one blue pill through the foil with his thumb. Karen was in Minneapolis, or somewhere not here. There was no audience except Tommy. That almost made it worse. Martin saw him looking, gave him that watch-this grin, tossed the pill dry into his mouth, and winked like a teenager about to sneak off and jerk off on principle. Tommy had told him he was out of his goddamn mind. Martin said he'd been told. That was the last funny thing he ever said.

Viagra and bad hearts did not belong together. Tommy wasn't a doctor. Tommy was a high school football coach with a PE degree and enough first-aid classes to know how to count compressions and get actual help on the way. But he knew the one medical fact every man in America knew about Viagra, because the commercials never shut up about it. Old guys with bad hearts weren't supposed to take boner pills, not if they were on blood pressure meds, not if they'd had too much booze, and probably not after a long soak in a hot tub either. Martin had been drinking since dinner. Martin had spent an hour stewing in hot water. Martin had looked red as hell before Tommy ever tossed him the pack. Now Martin was upstairs dead at forty-seven, and Tommy couldn't stop doing the ugly math.

From the counter, Tommy looked at Paul, who was still staring into untouched coffee.

"I'm going upstairs," Tommy said.

"What for?"

"Bathroom."

Paul nodded. Men accepted bathroom lies on instinct. Tommy went.

The stairs were dark. On the way up Tommy skipped the third step because everybody skipped the third step. Martin had been promising to fix that creak since Clinton. Upstairs, a shell-shaped night-light glowed by the shared bathroom and turned the hallway amber. The master bedroom door stood open.

Nothing in Tommy wanted to go through it. But the alternative was worse. The three remaining pills were still on Martin's nightstand in a foil pack anybody with a badge or a clipboard could pick up and start asking questions about.

Tommy went in.

Martin lay on his back on the bed. Paul had done something to him. Tommy could tell that much right away. The body had been straightened. The hands were crossed on the chest. Martin's face had gone a gray-purple color that had nothing to do with the ruddy flush Tommy had spent twenty-five years drinking across from.

The room smelled like Martin: Lagavulin, cedar closet, old lake-house air, wet skin, and chlorine still clinging to the place. The smell caught Tommy in the throat so fast he had to stop in the doorway and breathe once before moving.

The lamp on the nightstand was off. In the hall light Tommy saw a half glass of water, Martin's reading glasses, a thick Civil War history lying facedown, and the blister pack. Three pills were still sealed. The fourth bubble was empty.

Tommy picked it up. His hand was shaking hard now, not a little tired tremor but a whole-arm shake that started in his shoulder and ran clear into his fingers. The foil crackled. The pack went into his sweatpants pocket, and Tommy left the room without looking back at Martin because he knew if he looked back he was going to make a sound.

In the hallway his elbow clipped the doorframe. The blister pack crinkled again, loud as a starter pistol in the quiet house, and Tommy flattened both palms against the wall and waited. Footsteps moved on the main floor. A door opened and shut. One of the other guys was awake down there, doing whatever dumb panicked thing he thought he needed to do. Fine. Let him. Tommy had his own problem.

He went back down the stairs with the pack in his pocket, skipping the third step on instinct. The green room sat off the first-floor hall by the slider, the room Martin always put him in. Tommy shut himself inside and sat on the edge of the bed. He felt the pills through the thin fabric of his sweats, three hard bumps against his thigh. Right now he should flush them. The half bath was ten feet away. Punch them out, flush, throw the empty foil in his duffel. Three steps were still three steps.

But the half bath opened right off the hall and the kitchen beyond it, and somebody was still moving around out there. In this house the downstairs toilet didn't flush so much as announce itself. Tommy could not make himself stand in there with that roar going off while Martin cooled upstairs and half the house listened for what happened next.

So he sat with both hands locked around the edge of the mattress and stared at the dark little room, at the charging cord Martin always left snaked beside the bed, at the roll of athletic tape on the nightstand, at the green walls gone muddy in the dark. His mind kept landing on Denise, the kids, the school-board parents, the booster-club assholes, his job. All of it sat on the idea that Tommy Renaldi was a simple, decent man who did simple, decent things and had never been anywhere near the kind of trouble that got written into reports. In his head he pictured the words anyway: pills, cardiac arrest, hot tub, joke gift. Maybe there was a law word for it, manslaughter or negligent homicide or some other word Joel would know. Tommy didn't. Tommy knew only that he'd handed a friend a box of boner pills and that friend was dead upstairs.

Somewhere above him, boards ticked. Then the shared bathroom door eased shut and the medicine-cabinet mirror tapped the wall. Tommy went still. Another cabinet door clicked, plastic rattled, and then a zipper ran.

Of course. Jesus Christ. He wasn't the only asshole in the house doing arithmetic at four in the morning.

He opened the green-room door an inch. From the first-floor hall he could see the foot of the stairs. Doug came down from the second floor in a gray hoodie with his duffel hugged high against his ribs, head down, face washed clean of color. He didn't look toward Tommy. He crossed the hall in socks and disappeared toward the basement door, a man trying to weigh nothing.

Tommy almost said something. What the fuck are you doing, Whitfield, was up first, and under it was the worse question, which was what do you think you're hiding. He didn't want either answer. Everybody had a thing. Everybody had some little private grenade they were trying to get out of sight before daylight. Fine. Great. Beautiful. Tommy shut the door before Doug reached the basement stairs.

He could have used the bathroom then. The hall was empty again. Instead he sat back down harder than before. If Whitfield had just come down out of that upstairs bathroom with his duffel hugged to his chest and somebody heard Tommy set off the downstairs toilet a beat later, that stopped being bad luck and started looking like a committee.

Tommy waited for the sun.

What the sun bought him was witnesses.

Pipes knocked in the walls. Coffee started downstairs. Somebody opened the slider and shut it too hard. Tommy came out of the green room with the blister pack in his sweatpants pocket and found the kitchen lit ugly and yellow, all six of them back inside a morning they clearly did not want. Paul was measuring coffee with the concentration of a bomb technician. Nate sat at the table in yesterday's T-shirt with both hands around an empty mug. Joel stood on the deck in a hoodie, looking at the lake like he was preparing an argument for it. Cliff had somehow shaved. That felt obscene.

"We're calling now," Tommy said.

Paul nodded once. "Yes."

"No, I mean now now. Nobody wanders off. Nobody starts saying weird shit before we know who's coming out here."

Joel came in off the deck and slid the door shut behind him. "What does weird shit mean?"

"It means don't freelance."

Cliff let out a breath that wasn't a laugh. "That's a hell of a thing to say before anybody's even called the police."

Tommy turned on him. "You know what I mean."

"Yeah, we do," Joel said. "And — it's not the time, Tommy. You're coaching."

Nate scrubbed both hands over his face. "Can we maybe not do rules right now? Jesus Christ."

Tommy felt himself getting louder and hated it while it was happening. "Somebody's got to do something. We got six guys walking around like they forgot how a phone works."

Paul set the coffee scoop down. The metal click against the counter was small. It shut Tommy up faster than shouting would've.

"I'll make the call," Paul said. "You go outside where you can get a signal and talk to dispatch if they kick us over to county. That's useful. The rest of us can stay out of your way for thirty seconds."

Paul had called it useful, and that was enough. There it was: a job.

Tommy took the fastest shower of his life, scrubbed his face raw, and pulled on jeans and a polo. Then he grabbed Martin's zip fleece off the hook by the mudroom without asking and moved the blister pack out of his sweatpants pocket. It still smelled like cedar and Scotch and a little chlorine. At seven he stood on the deck with the fleece open over the polo, cell phone lifted toward the lake because that was where the signal held, one hand white on the railing while he repeated directions to the dispatcher every time the wind chewed half the sentence away. Twice he gave the address. Three times he described the turn off 59. By the time he came back inside his hands were numb and Paul was in the study on the landline answering the callback questions Tommy couldn't stand there for, spelling names, giving Martin's date of birth, explaining that yes, the man was cold and no, they had not moved him. That last part made Tommy look away. The deputies from Becker County showed up around eight-thirty. By then the blister pack was in the right pocket of Tommy's zip jacket.

The morning was cold enough to justify keeping the jacket on, and the jacket had deeper pockets than jeans. Every time Tommy shifted on the couch, the empty foil scratched lightly against his thigh.

The deputies were young. One had a mustache that looked borrowed. The other looked like he'd started shaving during the Obama years. They asked their questions in the careful voice people used around fresh bodies: when did you last see him, what time did you go to bed, did he have any medical conditions you knew about. Tommy answered in short hard sentences. He'd last seen Martin around one. No, he didn't know. No, he wasn't aware of any medical conditions. They wrote it down, went upstairs, and came back down. One of them took a call on the deck where the signal was better. The other stood in the kitchen drinking Nate's coffee, which tasted like punishment.

The worst part was how long Tommy had waited. At 3:14 he could have flushed the pills in peace. Now it was daylight, there were deputies in the house, and the three blue diamonds in his pocket had become three tiny sirens.

They stayed, made more trips upstairs, took more notes, and did another round through the bedroom. By the time the deputies finally stepped outside to wait for the county detective, they'd been in the house long enough to empty half the coffee pot and learn Martin's middle name. The wall clock over the stove said 9:52.

When the screen door slapped shut behind them, the kitchen breathed once and tightened right back up.

"County detective's ten minutes out," Nate said, staring at his phone like it might turn into a different morning if he stared hard enough.

Paul nodded. "That's what they said."

Joel had the study door half shut. "If anybody needs the bathroom, go now. Once county gets here, nobody's moving without it meaning something."

Paul dried his hands on a dish towel. "He's right about that."

That hit Tommy like a whistle blast. No more deputies drifting in and out. No more little errands. Just a county detective walking in and deciding where men belonged, what doors stayed open, who got watched.

Tommy looked at the front hall, then the half bath, then the five men in the kitchen with him. Paul was at the sink with the coffee pot in his hands. Nate sat at the table in his podcast cap, staring at his phone without seeing it. Cliff leaned against the counter with his arms crossed and his concern dialed in just a notch too carefully. Doug had surfaced from somewhere and stood near the mudroom with his hoodie zipped to the throat. Joel was already halfway back into the study.

Tommy stood.

"I'm going to the bathroom," Tommy said to the room.

Down the front hall, old pine glowed yellow in the morning light. Tommy kept his right hand buried in his jacket pocket around the blister pack and felt the three smooth bumps under the foil.

Walking, Tommy pulled it out. His fingers were worse now than they'd been in the dark. He'd had no sleep, too much adrenaline, too much coffee smell, too much dead body in the air. The first pill punched through clean and dropped into his left palm. So did the second.

The third one tore sideways through the foil and shot out.

It hit the floor, bounced once, and rolled, a small blue diamond in that cable-commercial sky blue.

The pill wobbled toward the baseboard and Tommy lunged too late. His hand slapped empty floor. The pill kept going and came to rest against the pine three feet ahead of him.

Tommy Renaldi went down on all fours in the hallway of his dead friend's lake house and crawled after a Viagra pill.

His knees cracked against the hardwood. The empty blister pack got crushed in one fist while the other hand reached for the baseboard. There it was, bright against the wood, like the house itself had spit the thing back at him. Tommy pinched it between his fingers and pushed himself up. The left knee popped, then the right. Tommy checked the floor. There was no powder, no blue streak, no witness mark. The baseboard was clean. The kitchen around the corner stayed loud with ordinary kitchen noise. Nobody had seen him.

Four more feet and he was inside the half bath with the door shut.

The room was small: toilet, pedestal sink, framed loon print, frosted window turning the light a flat September gold. Tommy opened his fist over the bowl. Three blue pills sat in his palm, damp from sweat. Tommy dropped them in and flushed.

The toilet answered with the full old-house roar, pipes shuddering in the walls, the kind of flush you felt in your teeth. Tommy watched the pills spin, flash blue once, and vanish down the throat of the bowl. Clear water climbed back into place.

The empty blister pack stayed in his other hand. Tommy folded it twice, jammed it deep into his jacket pocket, and turned on the sink because a man in a bathroom needed to be washing his hands if anybody asked.

Then tires hit the gravel.

The sound came through the frosted glass as a car climbed the driveway, cut its engine, and shut its door. Light quick steps came up the front porch. Then the doorbell rang, the stupid two-note chime Martin had once described as a dentist's office in hell.

A woman's voice came through the hall.

"Morning. County detective. Am I at Mr. Kessner's house, or have I dragged this casserole to the wrong place?"

The front door opened.

"Kessler," Joel said. He was precise even now. "His name was Kessler."

"Right. Kessler. That's what I wrote down. My mouth took a detour." Her voice stayed warm, easy, busy. "I'm sorry. And yes, this is a green bean casserole. It seemed rude to show up with only a notebook. Could I set it somewhere before my arms give out?"

More voices followed, Paul being polite and Nate not saying a thing, which was wrong enough to make Tommy's skin tighten.

"And before I start bothering people, do you have a bathroom on this floor? I made a bad decision with gas-station coffee between Detroit Lakes and here."

Footsteps turned down the hall toward him.

Tommy looked at the toilet. The bowl was clear. His wet hands caught his eye. Then the door. Twenty-five years coming to this house and he'd never clocked the fact that the half-bath door had no lock. Now his hand clamped the knob like it was the only hardware in Minnesota that mattered.

"I think that one's occupied," Paul said from the hall. He sounded calm and careful, doing his adult voice. "There's one upstairs. Second door on the left."

"Oh goodness. Of course it is." A small laugh. "Upstairs is perfect. Thank you."
