The footsteps turned away and went to the stairs. The third step barked under her weight.

"Lord, that's a loud stair," she said, still cheerful, voice drifting upward. "Pretty banister, though. Your friend had a nice house."

Her talking moved to the second floor and blurred behind the closing bathroom door. Tommy stayed where he was, hand on the knob, wet fingerprints drying on brass, heart kicking at his ribs.

Upstairs, the toilet ran, then cut off. A cabinet door clicked. Water hit porcelain. Tommy kept his hand on the knob and stared at the damp crescents his fingers had left in the brass until he heard the bathroom door open again.

Then the stairs, quicker this time and skipping the creak because somebody had warned her. Tommy dried his hands on his jeans, opened the door, and went back to the great room.

The draft board still hung on the wall. Martin's picks were in red. Tommy's were in blue. Friday it had been the whole weekend. Now it was a bright grid behind a county detective setting a casserole dish on the kitchen counter and setting her leather notebook beside it.

Nate looked over from the table.

"You all right?" he asked. There was no bit in it, no setup, just the question.

"Fine," Tommy said. "I'm fine."

His right hand stayed in his jacket pocket, thumb worrying the folded foil until the corners pressed into his skin. In the kitchen Mallick was asking Paul where she'd be least in the way, whether the fridge had room, whether that shelf would work or if another one made more sense. Her voice was warm and neighborly and relentless.

Tommy sat on the couch and kept still. The pills were in the plumbing. The pack in his pocket was folded twice, warm from his hand, sharp at the corners.

Then Mallick turned from the refrigerator and looked at him. She didn't look long, just long enough for Tommy to stop breathing. She gave the room a quick polite smile and turned back to the casserole. Tommy stayed on the couch with both hands buried in his pockets and waited for his lungs to start again.


# Chapter 4

The Becker County deputies had made seven trips up and down the stairs. From the kitchen table, Joel counted every one. He'd been planted there since quarter to eight with a mug Paul kept refilling, and on each pass the deputies skipped the third step near the top. Paul had warned them about the creak before Joel even thought to mention it.

Cup four sat half-finished in front of him. Too much coffee on top of no sleep and no food had his hands shaking in short fast bursts. His stomach had gone sour an hour ago. Every time a deputy passed behind him, the muscles between his shoulders locked. He'd seen clients go that still in arraignment. He had never thought about what it cost them. Now he knew. It felt like being made small in public.

The great room still smelled like Martin's scotch, the Lagavulin he'd poured too generously last night, because Martin never knew how to pour lightly, host reflex still firing even when people told him to sit down. Empty glasses still crowded the stone hearth where people had set them after the draft, nobody willing to decide yet what counted as trash and what counted as evidence. Morning light came through the A-frame windows in thick gold slabs, postcard light, the wrong light for this.

Tommy stood on the deck with both hands around a coffee mug like it was the only solid thing left. A gray clammy cast had settled over his face, the look of a man bracing for numbers he already knew were bad. His right hand kept drifting to the pocket of his zip jacket and coming back out empty. In the kitchen doorway, Nate was explaining the lake house's history to a deputy who had not asked and did not care. Doug sat on the couch in the corner with his hood up and his knees pulled in, looking at his phone with the blank concentration of a man reading absolutely nothing.

Nobody held still for long. Nate shifted from one foot to the other and filled every silence the deputy left him. Tommy came in from the deck, went back out, came in again. Doug's thumb moved over a black screen without ever waking it. Even Cliff's calm had a showroom shine to it, too polished to trust. The whole room felt arranged instead of inhabited, like six men had been told to wait in a place they recognized and now couldn't figure out how to sit in it.

By the fireplace, Cliff occupied an Adirondack chair with his legs crossed and his coffee balanced on the armrest. He looked delayed-flight calm. Everyone else looked like they'd been in a wreck.

Paul came back from the hallway and sat across from Joel. His wire-rimmed glasses were smudged and still smudged, which was how you knew Paul Decker was not all right. Under normal conditions the man cleaned them every forty minutes, same as the refrigerator cycle.

"Medical examiner's been called," Paul said. "They said a couple hours."

"From Detroit Lakes?"

"Yeah." Paul rubbed the bridge of his nose where the glasses sat. "The deputies have been professional. Thorough. I told them Martin had some health concerns."

"Did he?"

Paul looked at him. "He was forty-seven and he drank like he was twenty-two. He didn't exercise. You saw what he ate last night."

"That's not a health concern, that's a lifestyle."

"Joel."

"That's not me lawyering this."

"You're always being a lawyer." Paul said it without malice. He'd been saying it for twenty years.

Joel let that one go because it was true. The mug came up and went back down untouched. "Has anyone called Linda?"

"I've got her number. I've been waiting until I know what to say. I still don't. There's no protocol for this."

"No."

"Yeah." Paul's eyes went to the draft board. Martin's first-round pick sat there in red, with the neat projected-points note Martin always added, the numbers that didn't matter anymore. "I know."

Joel nearly asked what the deputies had found upstairs, then stopped himself. Paul would answer if he could. If he couldn't, the question would just thicken the room.

Gravel popped under tires out front. Both deputies turned toward the windows. A maroon Ford Explorer rolled in behind the sheriff's cruiser, and the woman behind the wheel leaned across the passenger seat for a Pyrex dish with foil stretched tight across the top.

So that was what she'd reached for.

She came through the front door carrying it with both hands, already talking. "Oh, good morning. I'm Detective Mallick with the Becker County Sheriff's Office. I'm so sorry about your friend. Is this the Kessner residence?"

Silence took the room by the throat. Six men had heard the same wrong name and were waiting for somebody else to fix it.

"Kessler," Paul said. "Martin Kessler."

"Oh, Kessler. I'm sorry, I had it wrong in my notes somewhere." She shifted the casserole to one arm and offered her free hand toward the room in general. She was stocky, maybe five-six, with gray in her low bun and reading glasses on a beaded chain. She looked like the neighbor who'd come over after seeing too many cars in the driveway. "Now, where can I set this down? It's green bean, my mother's recipe, and I know everybody says that, but she really did make it better than anybody. Needs about twenty minutes at three-fifty if you want it later."

Nate moved first. He took the casserole like you took a dish from an aunt at a funeral, automatically, before your brain had time to register how insane the gesture was. "Yeah, the fridge is right here."

"Thank you so much. And before anything else, I'm sorry, this is embarrassing, but could someone point me toward the restroom? The drive from Detroit Lakes, two cups of coffee, and I just, well." Her smile came out warm and faintly sheepish. Joel felt the skin on the back of his neck tighten. "Anyway."

"Half bath's by the front door," Paul said. "Just past the hall closet."

"I'll find it. Thank you." She set her leather notebook on the counter and disappeared down the hallway with the unhurried ease of a woman looking for the bathroom at a friend's house, not at a death scene.

Joel looked at Paul. Paul looked back at him.

"What the fuck," Tommy said from the deck doorway. He had come inside without Joel noticing and was standing there with his mug aimed at the hallway. "Did she just bring us a casserole?"

"A detective," Nate said. You could hear the podcast gears starting up, already sorting this into a story he could tell later if later still existed. "A county detective brought a green bean casserole to a death investigation. I mean, is that standard? Is that a Minnesota thing?"

"It's a casserole thing," Doug said from the couch without looking up.

"Right, thanks, Doug. Great distinction. A casserole thing. At a death investigation. From a woman who called Martin the wrong name."

"Keep your voice down," Joel said.

"I'm just saying."

"I know what you're doing. Keep it down."

Mallick came back from the bathroom drying her hands on the sides of her blazer. She picked up the notebook, clicked open a pen, and got a quick nod from the younger deputy with the crew cut, the one who'd been asking about the draft board earlier. Then he stepped back and let her take the room.

"Now," she said, pulling up a chair at the kitchen table as if she'd been invited. "I'm sorry about getting here late. Road work on 59 had everything backed up, if you can believe that on a Saturday. Deputy Sievert and Deputy Kohn have been taking good care of things." She opened the notebook to a fresh page. "So I understand Mr. Kessler was found earlier this morning?"

"A little after two," Paul said. "Nate found him."

"Nate." Her eyes moved around the room. "That's you, then?"

"Me. Yeah. Nathan Griggs." Nate stepped forward like he'd been called on in class. "It was after two. Two-oh-three maybe. I looked at my phone right when I saw him."

"Just after two. OK." She wrote it down. "And he was in the downstairs bedroom? One of the guest rooms?"

"Upstairs," Paul said. "Master bedroom. Front of the hall."

"Upstairs. Of course. I'm sorry." More writing. "And you were all here this weekend, how many of you? I had seven in my notes, but maybe I'm counting wrong."

"Seven." It came out of Joel before he could stop it. "Six now."

The room flinched. Even Joel heard how it landed, too clean and too exact, like he'd trimmed the soft parts out before it left his mouth. Mallick looked at him for a beat with an expression that could have been sympathy or simple attention.

"Six. Of course. I'm sorry." Her pen moved again. "And you're Joel?"

"Joel Beckman."

"Joel." She wrote the name down. "And you all come up here every year for this? For fishing, or is it football?"

"Fantasy football," Nate said. "It's a keeper league. Twenty-five years running. Well, twenty-five drafts. We started in college."

"Oh, that's wonderful. My nephew does that. He's on his phone every Thanksgiving arguing about waiver wire nonsense and it drives his mother crazy." She peered through the screen door at the deck. "And is that a hot tub I saw coming in? Nice one. Seats what, six? Eight?"

"Six," Tommy said. His voice sounded scraped raw.

"Six. And you all used it last night, or was that the night before?"

"Last night," Paul said. Joel watched the calculation happen in real time, that insurance-adjuster precision kicking in while he decided what to disclose and what to leave alone. "Some of us. It was a late night."

"Oh, sure. Draft night. Like New Year's Eve for football." She smiled down at her notebook. "And Mr. Kessler was out there with the rest of you till close to midnight?"

"No," Tommy said, too fast. "Not that long."

Paul turned his head.

Tommy kept going anyway. "He got in, he got out. Ten minutes maybe. He said it was too hot."

Mallick looked up. "Too hot."

"That's what he said," Tommy replied. He squeezed the mug once, hard enough to flex the lid. "Something like that."

Paul cleared his throat. "It was late. Everybody was moving in and out."

"Of course." She wrote again. "I swear those things are always either lukewarm or boiling. No middle on them. And that deck door still catches unless you lift it first, or did he finally fix that?"

Nate answered first. "You just give it a shove with your hip and it goes."

From the chair by the fireplace, Cliff said, "Lift and slide. If you shove it, it jumps the track."

Mallick turned to him as if grateful for the help. "Lift and slide. Thank you."

Cliff gave her a small smile meant to say he was the kind of man who noticed houses.

Joel took another swallow of coffee. Tommy volunteering the hot-tub timing. Cliff volunteering the door. Maybe both meant nothing. Maybe both were only the way men sounded when a stranger sat down and started writing.

"Is there coffee?" Mallick asked. "I saw the pot going and I'm still trying to wake up after that drive."

Paul got up and poured her a cup. Of course he did. When things went bad, Paul started taking care of whatever sat nearest his hands. Coffee. Plates. Somebody's coat over the back of a chair. A sentence calm enough to lay over whatever ugly thing had just been said. Joel had seen him do it at Martin's divorce dinner, at Nate's birthday when Nate drank himself into a fight with a bouncer, at a dozen weekends when somebody got sloppy and needed handling. It was how Paul kept the room from coming apart while he avoided looking straight at the worst thing in it.

Mallick held the mug under her nose and breathed in. "Oh, that's good. Nice dark roast. Martin, Mr. Kessler, had good taste." Her attention drifted to the draft board. "Is that your fantasy football thing? Oh my goodness. That's elaborate."

"It's a keeper league," Nate started, and Joel could see the whole explanation loading behind his eyes, league history, rules, scoring quirks, twenty-five years of context ready to pour out whether anyone wanted it or not.

"Nate," Joel said. "She didn't ask how it works."

Mallick smiled over the rim of the mug and said nothing, which shut Nate down faster than another question would have.

She kept them at the table another fifteen minutes, not really interviewing anybody so much as asking the sort of house questions that sounded too dumb to matter. When was the addition put on. Was the upstairs bathroom the one with the slow drain. Did the dock light work if you hit the switch twice or was that the old place on Pelican Lake she was thinking of. Every answer got a thank-you and a note. Every correction sat somewhere behind her reading glasses and stayed there.

When she asked whether the master had its own bathroom or if everybody used the hall one, Paul said the master had its own. Tommy said, "Hall bath's ours." Then Doug, still looking at his dead phone, said, "Nate and I are downstairs." Mallick nodded like she'd just learned who took cream with their coffee.

"That has to be a traffic jam after midnight, then," she said. "Six men, one hall bath, scotch and hot-tub heat on board."

"Three men," Tommy said.

Paul said, "Four if you count Martin's bathroom as off-limits to the rest of us."

"Right. Of course." She smiled and wrote it down. "I only ask because old houses like this keep a little record of people in the night. Toilet lid, bad floorboard, somebody clomping around for aspirin."

Nobody answered right away.

Nate tried first. "I mean, sure, people get up. We're not nineteen."

"Did you?" Mallick asked.

"Me?" Nate pointed at his chest like she might mean another Nate. "I was downstairs. I don't think so. Maybe once. For water. No, that was earlier."

Mallick wrote something down.

Joel watched Paul's face go flat and Tommy's right hand disappear back into his jacket pocket. He had not seen Tommy pull anything out of it all morning.

When she asked whether Martin had always taken the front bedroom, Doug said, "Always," without looking up from his dead phone screen.

When she wondered if the deck faced east, Nate said west, no, southwest, actually straight at sunset this time of year, and Joel watched him hear himself talking too much without being able to stop.

When she said the stairs were steeper than she'd expected, Paul said Martin's father had built the place in the eighties and left more room for windows than common sense. Paul smiled when he said it, and for half a second it was the right smile, the old one, the one you got before Martin followed it with something filthy. Then it was gone.

When she asked whether people used the front door or the deck more after midnight, three men answered. Nate said deck. Cliff said whichever was closer. Paul said it depended on weather. Mallick thanked them all and wrote another line. Joel had the sour little thought that if she asked what color the fucking moon had been, somebody in the room would correct somebody else about it.

The younger deputy watched all this from the hallway without pretending not to. He never wrote anything down in front of them. He just leaned a shoulder to the wall and looked from speaker to speaker as if the whole room might sort itself into categories if he stayed patient long enough. Joel kept looking for some soft place in it, the point where this turned back into a weekend with one terrible thing at the center instead of a whole house gone wrong. He could not find one.

---

For another forty minutes she worked the room before she got to Joel. From his chair, he watched her make the slow circle and felt each stop bring her closer. By the time she turned away from Paul, the back of his neck was damp.

Out on the deck she talked to Tommy while he sat hunched forward in an Adirondack with his hands laced behind his neck. Joel couldn't hear every word through the screen door, only pieces. "After midnight," Mallick said at one point, mild as ever, and Tommy answered too quickly with, "No. Before. I was done before that." He rubbed at the back of his neck until the skin there went red. When she asked something else Joel missed, Tommy glanced toward the hallway instead of at her and said, "I didn't go back in there."

She did not lean forward at that. She didn't pounce or sharpen or change tone. She only said something Joel could not make out and let Tommy fill the silence for her. Tommy looked over his shoulder at the lake as if the answer might be written there, then said, louder, "I went back to my room. I shut the door. That was it." Mallick nodded and wrote, and Tommy's mouth stayed open a second after the words were finished, like he'd realized too late that every extra word was making it worse.

Next came Cliff in the kitchen. Cliff being Cliff, shoulders back, good eye contact, selling composure even here. Mallick asked about the house layout. Cliff corrected her on which guest room sat where, then corrected her again when she called Martin's study the little office downstairs. "Upstairs," he said. "Left side off the hall. Pocket door." She thanked him, wrote it down, and moved on while Cliff drank his coffee like he'd closed something.

Then she asked whether Martin always kept the study shut. Cliff said usually. She asked whether the room had its own window AC or only the central air. Cliff said only central, unless Martin still hauled the portable unit up in July. He caught himself halfway through that answer, maybe hearing how far into the room he'd just wandered, and added, "I mean, I don't know if he did this year." Mallick thanked him again. Cliff smiled, but it came slower that time.

Doug got only a few minutes. He gave her nods, monosyllables, that agreeable blankness he gave anyone who pushed too close. Mallick didn't lean on him. She just wrote. Once, when she asked if he heard people moving overnight, Doug said, "Heard stairs," then seemed to wish he hadn't said even that.

"More than once?" Mallick asked.

Doug stared at the floorboards. "I wasn't counting."

"Sure," she said. "But more than once?"

Doug dragged a hand over his mouth. "Maybe. I don't know. House made noise."

"Old houses do." She stood without another word, which seemed to bother Doug more than if she'd kept after him.

She took Nate next, maybe because he had become impossible to ignore even while trying to disappear. He stayed standing until she asked him twice to sit, then perched on the edge of a chair as if full contact with it might count as commitment. Joel could hear most of that one because Nate forgot to lower his voice.

"I didn't go up there because something was wrong," Nate said. "I went up because I was awake. I mean, I was already awake. Basement couch, weird angle, my back was cooked, so I went looking for, not looking. I was just up."

"For the bathroom?" Mallick asked.

"Maybe water. Maybe the bathroom. Maybe both. That's the glamorous answer."

"And your phone was in your hand because?"

Nate laughed once. "Because I'm forty-six and I don't go anywhere in the dark without that stupid flashlight. Also service is better by the stairs."

"Mm-hm."

"That sounds rehearsed. It isn't rehearsed. I just know where the bars are in this place."

She wrote that down. Nate saw her do it and talked faster, explaining which corner of the basement ate calls, how Martin always complained the carrier map was bullshit, how nobody ever fixed lake-house internet because half the point of coming up was pretending the world couldn't reach you. By the end of it he sounded like a man testifying against a cell tower. Mallick thanked him with the same little nod she used on everybody else, and Nate sat back like he'd somehow talked himself into a hole he could not locate.

She circled back to him two minutes later from the doorway and asked, easy as anything, "When you found him, was the hall light on already or did you switch it on?"

Nate blinked. "Hall light."

"Already on."

"No. I turned it on. I think. Wait." He looked at the ceiling as if the answer might still be there. "Maybe the bathroom light was on and I hit the hall switch after. I don't know."

Mallick nodded. "Those old switches are never where you expect them."

Nate let out a breath like she'd just spared him something.

On her way back through the kitchen, she paused beside Paul and asked if the oven ran hot and whether he'd mind putting the casserole in around two-fifty. Paul said yes. Then she asked whether Nate had been downstairs with Doug all night.

"Mostly," Paul said.

Nate let out a laugh that died halfway. "Mostly is such a guilty word, man."

Paul didn't look at him. "It means mostly."

Mallick wrote that down too, like it was as useful as the deck door or the oven temperature. Maybe it was.

She did not let Paul go after that.

"Did you hear anyone up after you went to bed?" she asked.

Paul took off his glasses, wiped them on the hem of his shirt, and put them back on. "Not clearly."

"Not clearly."

"It's an old house. Things carry. Pipes, steps, people settling in."

"Sure. And you yourself stayed put?"

"As far as I recall."

That one made Joel look up. Not because it was damning. Because it was Paul, and Paul did not usually leave himself three exits in one sentence unless he needed them.

"Did Martin mention chest pain, indigestion, anything like that before bed?" Mallick asked.

"No," Paul said.

Then, after half a beat, "Not to me."

Mallick thanked him. She did not change expression. She only wrote a little longer than she had before.

Then she refilled her coffee, crossed back to the table, pulled out the chair opposite Joel, set the notebook flat, and said, "So. Joel. Let's talk."

He nodded.

"Twenty-five years with Mr. Kessler too?"

"Since undergrad."

"And you drove up Thursday? From the Cities?"

"Hennepin County."

"Right. Close enough." She gave him a small apologetic smile. "I'm sorry. I've been in Detroit Lakes too long. Anything south of St. Cloud starts feeling like the same trip."

She checked her notes, or gave the impression of checking them. Either way, the pause did what it was meant to do.

"Now," Mallick said, "somebody mentioned, and I apologize, I don't remember who, but somebody mentioned you and Martin had a disagreement Thursday evening?"

There it was. Joel felt his jaw tighten. He'd known the question was coming from the moment she walked in with the Pyrex dish, but waiting for it and hearing it were different things. While she worked her way around the room, he'd had time to build the answer and sand the roughest edge off it. Now his mouth tasted like old coffee and coins.

"We had a conversation," he said.

"A conversation. OK." She wrote the word. "And was that Thursday? Or was it Friday? I may have the days mixed up."

"Thursday."

"Thursday. Thank you." More notes. "And what was the conversation about?"

"It was private."

Mallick looked at him with patient concern on her face, or maybe only patient attention. There was enough warmth in it to be sincere and enough room in it to work as technique.

"Private," she repeated. "I understand. I do. It's just, with the situation, I'm trying to put together a picture of the last several hours."

"I understand what you're doing, Detective." Joel kept his voice level, cooperative on the surface, locked underneath. "A man died in his sleep and you need a timeline. I'm telling you we had a private conversation Thursday night, it got heated, and it had nothing to do with his death."

"You're probably right. Most arguments between old friends don't, well." She gave a small, apologetic lift of the pen. "For my notes, what time was the conversation?"

"Late. Eleven, maybe eleven-thirty."

"And where was this?"

"On the deck."

"The deck. OK. Good." She wrote again. "And after the conversation, you went where?"

"Went to bed."

"What time?"

"Midnight. Maybe a little after."

"And you stayed there until Nate knocked?"

"Until Nate woke me up. Around two."

"Two. And between midnight and two, you didn't get up at all? Bathroom, glass of water, check your phone, any of that?"

"No."

"No." She wrote it down. The pen scratched over the paper while the refrigerator sat quiet for once and a deputy's radio muttered low from the hallway. "You didn't hear anything? Footsteps, doors, anybody moving around?"

"My door was closed."

"Which bedroom are you in?"

"Upstairs. Second guest room, end of the hall."

"Not the one by the bathroom?"

"That's Paul and Tommy's side. I'm at the other end."

"Oh. I always get these lake-house layouts mixed up. Too many rooms." She waved the pen as if embarrassed by herself. "So you didn't hear anything. First thing you knew was Nate knocking on your door at two."

"Correct."

Mallick put her pen down. She picked up her coffee instead and sat back.

"Joel, can I be straight with you?"

"You can try."

"You're very calm." She took a sip. "I don't mean that in a bad way. You're just calmer than everybody else in here."

"OK."

"And you've got the cleanest account in the house." She looked at her notebook. "Thursday night, you had this argument with Martin, and you won't tell me what it was about. That's your business. But your timeline's neat. Bed at midnight, door closed, didn't hear a thing, woke at two." She looked up. "Everybody else has got something that wanders. A time they're not sure about, a trip to the bathroom, a detail they want to fix after the fact. You don't have any of that."

"Maybe I sleep better than they do."

"Middle-aged men who've had scotch, hot-tub heat, and bad lake-house mattresses don't usually sleep straight through," she said. "One of them gets up to piss. One of them goes looking for antacids. One of them opens the wrong door and swears at the dark. That's just how these weekends work."

"Maybe I got lucky."

"Maybe you did." She did not smile. "Or maybe you're better than your friends at sorting what sounds bad from what sounds true."

That hit closer than Joel wanted. Heat moved up his neck. Thursday night came back in one ugly flash: Martin on the deck rail, lake black behind him, saying the thing calm enough to make it worse. Joel had gone to bed with his molars aching from how hard he'd clenched them. They ached again now.

"I'm telling you what happened."

"I know you are," she said. "I'm just still deciding whether you're telling it in the order it happened or the order that helps you most."

She sat with her hands folded over the notebook.

A loon broke across the lake and Joel's head came up before he could stop it, the cry close enough to a person hollering from the dock to raise the hair on his arms.

"Nobody's a suspect here," Joel said.

"Of course not. This is an unattended death and I'm making sure I have the picture right." She picked up her pen again. "You're right that it was probably a cardiac event. That's almost certainly what happened. A man his age, his habits, I'd expect the same thing."

The word probably sat on the table between them. Maybe it was a move. Maybe she truly meant it. Joel couldn't sort that out, and the uncertainty worked on him anyway.

"Did somebody tell you I was the last one he fought with?" Joel asked.

Mallick tipped her head. "Somebody told me there was a disagreement."

"That's not what I asked."

"No," she said. "It isn't."

He hated that he'd given her even that much.

"Is there anything else?" he asked.

"Not right now. I appreciate your time, I really do." Mallick stood and pushed the chair back in. "I might have some follow-up questions later, if that's all right."

"I'm not going anywhere."

"No," she said, with a soft little smile. "I suppose none of you are, not today anyway. And the coffee really is good."

She headed for the hallway. Joel stayed where he was with the cold mug in front of him and the scraped-out feeling she'd left behind. One question never came.

The missing question wasn't whether he'd been angry at Martin. Anybody with ears could've asked that inside three minutes. Joel had an answer ready. Yes, he was angry. No, he wasn't going to explain why the anger had gone private and humiliating before it ever got loud. He'd worked that answer over carefully enough to say less than the truth without quite turning it into a lie.

She hadn't asked. Maybe she missed it. Maybe she'd already found better splinters to work at.

Nate had choked on the minute mark like there was more attached to it than the time. Then he'd nearly talked himself into a story about flashlights and cell bars and which light switch he'd hit first. Tommy had jumped in to say exactly how long Martin stayed in the hot tub when nobody had blamed the hot tub for anything, then told Mallick twice he hadn't gone back in there. Cliff knew which room mattered and which door stuck and whether the study ever got a portable air conditioner. Paul kept sanding everybody's answers down half a second after they came out, including his own.

Maybe that was only shock. Maybe a dead friend turned every sentence in the house strange. But Joel stopped feeling singled out. If Mallick was building a map, she had more than one houseguest on it.

She could still be exactly what she looked like: county casserole, wrong name, bathroom before body, all of it. Joel would have paid good money to believe that version. But fools did not get four grown men to volunteer the shape of a house they were supposedly just sitting in. Fools did not make a clean timeline look dirty by setting it next to five messy ones.

Maybe she was an idiot. Maybe she was the smartest person in the house. Joel couldn't tell, which was its own problem.

Upstairs, her footsteps moved from room to room. Joel stared at Martin's draft board until the handwriting started to look older than yesterday.


# Chapter 5

The casserole was still in Martin's refrigerator. Mallick had asked the tall one, Decker, to tuck it onto the second shelf before she started with the lawyer, and now the Pyrex sat behind a jar of pickles with the lid fogged white from the cold. It gave her a domestic prop, solid and harmless in a house where every other object had started acquiring legal consequence. She was in the great room, where she'd set up at the long pine table with her notebook open, her reading glasses pushed to the end of her nose, and the county's cheapest ballpoint in her right hand.

She'd finished with Beckman. That had been interesting, interesting the way a locked door was interesting. He knew exactly how much silence was worth. She'd thanked him and let him go, and now she was looking at the runner leading to the stairs. Deputies' boots had dried a scatter of lake grit into the wool, but under that she could still see the older traffic pattern, heel marks crossing heel marks, the nap rubbed backward in a dozen directions. There was too much movement for a house where a man was supposed to have died quietly in his sleep.

Mallick wrote: *Beckman: clean. Too clean? Or genuinely clean?*

She underlined *genuinely* and drew a small question mark next to it. Then she capped the pen and went to the kitchen.

The refrigerator kicked into its grinding cycle. She opened the door long enough to confirm the casserole was still where she'd left it, then poured herself a glass of water from the tap. The water here tasted like iron and pine. That was lake-house plumbing.

Paul Decker was standing in the doorway of the great room. He'd been waiting. She could tell because he was holding a coffee mug he'd already finished, the handle turned outward, gripped with both hands, trying for relaxed and missing by a mile.

"Oh, Mr. Decker," she said. "Is it Decker? I'm so bad with names. Hold on." She flipped her notebook back a page. "Paul Decker. Insurance, you said?"

"Claims adjuster," he said. "Mutual of Omaha, but the Minneapolis office."

"Claims adjuster." She wrote it down. She'd already written it down twice. "Now that's a job. My neighbor Gary had a tree come down on his garage last March, big old ash, just split right down the middle, and the man they sent out was just wonderful. Do you do homeowners, or is it more commercial, or whatever walks in?"

"All lines," Paul said. "Homeowners, auto, commercial. Whatever walks in."

"Whatever walks in. Sure." She pulled out a chair at the kitchen table and gestured to the one across from her. "Do you want coffee? I think there's still some in the pot. It might be a little old by now, but I can make fresh if you want."

"I'm fine."

"You sure? I can't drink old coffee myself. My stomach. But some people prefer it stronger, so." She shrugged and sat down and opened her notebook to a fresh page. "Now, Mr. Decker. Paul. Can I call you Paul?"

"Sure."

"Paul. I just want to go over a couple of things, if that's all right. Nothing formal. I'm just trying to get the picture straight in my head because, honestly, I get confused sometimes with these, when there's a lot of people in a house and everybody's got a different version." She smiled. "I'm sure you understand how that goes."

"I do." He sat down across from her. His posture was good, straight-backed, hands flat on the table, the coffee mug set to one side. He had the tabletop stillness of somebody used to getting the story in order. She noted this.

"So. Last night. You all had the draft, the football thing, and then drinks, the hot tub, and bed. Is that about right?"

"That's about right." He paused. "The draft went until about ten-thirty. Then we were on the deck for a while, maybe until midnight. Martin went to bed before the rest of us. Tommy went up maybe twelve-thirty, and I think I was one of the last ones. Maybe one, one-fifteen."

She wrote the times down. "And you slept where?"

"Upstairs. The guest bedroom on the right side, if you're facing the lake."

"The right side. And Martin was in the master?"

"Yes."

"And that's on the left?"

"That's on the left. At the end of the hall."

She nodded and wrote. "Now, Nate, Mr. Griggs, he said he found Martin at, oh, he said around two. Does that sound right to you?"

"I think it was a little after two. He woke me up. He was standing in the doorway and he said Martin's not breathing, or words to that effect. Not breathing. I got up immediately."

"And you went to Martin's room?"

"I went to Martin's room. I tried CPR. For several minutes. But he was already gone." Paul's jaw worked. It wasn't for effect, she thought. She'd seen enough grief to know the mechanics of it, and this was the real thing, the muscle clenching because the words were in the way. "His skin was cool. Not cold, but. He'd been dead a while."

"I'm so sorry," Mallick said, and she meant it, in the way she always meant it, which was genuinely but with her pen still moving. "That must've been awful. I can't imagine. My husband passed in '09 and I wasn't even there for it. He was at work, just dropped, and I still sometimes think about whether it would've been better to be there or not. I go back and forth."

Paul looked at her. The mention of a dead husband had the effect she expected, a softening around his eyes, a slight dropping of the interview posture. People gave her things when she laid down one true detail of her own first. It was transactional and also true. Pete had dropped at work. She did go back and forth.

"I'm sorry," Paul said.

"Oh, it's been a long time now. But you don't forget how it feels." She flipped a page. "Now, when you went in and found Martin, Nate was already there, right? And you said you tried CPR. When you went in, was he on the bed?"

"He was on the bed."

"On his back?"

Paul paused. She watched the pause. In her experience, a pause before a factual answer meant the fact was being measured, not remembered.

"On his side," Paul said. "His left side. Facing the window."

"His left side. Facing the window." She frowned and flipped back in her notebook. "Oh, that's funny. I thought the deputies' report said he was on his back when they got there this morning. Flat on his back, arms at his sides." She looked up. "But you're saying he was on his left side?"

"He was on his left side when I got there."

"Huh." She wrote it down slowly, her handwriting deliberately large so he could see she was recording it. "So between when you got there a little after two and when the deputies arrived at, what was it, eight-thirty, he went from his left side to his back."

The silence in the kitchen was thick enough to hear the old refrigerator starting its next cycle, that grinding mechanical sound that probably woke everyone in the house every forty minutes.

"I moved him," Paul said. "For the CPR. You have to put the person on their back for chest compressions. So I rolled him onto his back."

"Oh, of course." She wrote it down. "That makes perfect sense. CPR. You'd have to, wouldn't you. And the arms at his sides?"

"I positioned him. For the compressions. You put the heel of your hand on the sternum and you do the compressions there."

"No, sure, I understand. I took a course once. At the YMCA, if you can believe it." She smiled. "Although I think they've changed it since then. When I learned it there was the breathing part, but now I hear they don't."

"They still recommend rescue breaths for people trained in it," Paul said. He wanted the protocol right. "I was trained. I did compressions and breaths for about four minutes. Then I stopped because there was no response and I could tell it wasn't going to work."

"Four minutes. That's very precise."

"I was looking at the clock on the nightstand. The digital one. It said two-fourteen when I started and two-eighteen when I stopped."

"Two-fourteen and two-eighteen." She wrote both numbers down, then drew a small box around them. "See, that's what I mean. Most people would say a few minutes. You've got timestamps. That's your training, I bet."

"Probably."

"My husband was a roofer. When he estimated a job he'd measure to the quarter-inch and then add ten percent for waste. He said people who round up are lying and people who round down are selling you a job." She wrote *clock on nightstand: verify position, verify model* in the margin of her notes and then drew a small circle around it.

"Paul, that's really helpful," she said. "I appreciate you being so specific. Most people say a few minutes or a while, and that's not always helpful when I'm trying to put a timeline together."

"I try to be."

"Do you mind if I ask, when you left Martin's room, did you close the door?"

"I closed the door."

"And you went back to bed?"

"I went back to bed."

The answer came too fast and too flat, and they both heard it. Paul's hands were still on the table, but his thumbs had moved, tucked under his palms. Mallick boxed the times again and wrote *door shut* underneath them. She wanted Martin's room before the body left the house, and she wanted that nightstand clock before somebody helpfully unplugged it.

"Well," she said, standing up. "I think that's all I need for now. Would you send in, oh, who's next on my..." She checked her notebook as if she hadn't planned the order three hours ago. "Haywood? Is Mr. Haywood around?"

"Cliff," Paul said. "I think he's on the deck."

"Cliff. That's right. Cliff Haywood. Would you mind? Oh, and Paul, if anybody starts cleaning, don't let them bury my casserole behind the orange juice. I'll forget it exists."

"Thanks."

"The secret is the fried onions on top. French's. Don't let anybody tell you the fancy ones are better."

Paul nodded and stood and carried his empty coffee mug to the sink and rinsed it. She noted that too: the quick rinse, the clean sink, the automatic tidy-up. His hands erased themselves from things without asking permission.

---

Mallick moved the interview to the deck. The kitchen had made Decker go straight-backed and careful, and she wanted Haywood in a different setting. People behaved differently depending on where you put them. The kitchen made Decker precise. She suspected the deck would make Haywood expand.

She was right. Haywood came out with his own coffee, a fresh cup, and pulled one of the Adirondack chairs around to face her at a companionable angle rather than across from her. He'd changed his shirt since this morning. She'd seen him in a navy polo at seven, and now it was a sage green linen thing, open at the collar, the kind of shirt that cost more than it looked like it cost. She knew the type. Her second cousin sold commercial real estate in Fargo and dressed the same way, studied casualness with a pitch always warming in the wings.

"Detective, I appreciate you taking the time with all of us," Cliff said, settling into the chair. "I know this can't be easy, coordinating, what is it, six interviews? Seven, if you count the deputies and everybody."

"Oh, I lose count." She laughed. "I'm not very organized, honestly. My sergeant says I'd lose my own head if it wasn't attached, which, you know, he's probably not wrong." She flipped through her notebook, passing Decker's pages. "Now, you're Cliff. Cliff Haywood. And you do..." She trailed off and looked at him expectantly.

"Real estate development. Commercial and residential, mostly outer-ring suburbs. Plymouth, Maple Grove, that area."

"Oh, nice. Good market out there?"

"It's got its challenges." The smile wasn't fake. It was practiced. He wore confidence the way other men wore a watch, something visible and checked often. "But we're positioned well."

"Positioned well. I love that." She wrote it down. She wrote down everything. Most of it was useless, but the act of writing put people on a tape delay. They watched her pen and forgot to watch her eyes. "So. Last night. The draft, the drinks, the hot tub. Same as everyone else?"

"Same as everyone else. Although I turned in a little earlier than the rest, maybe eleven-thirty. The hot tub does me in. My back."

"Oh, I hear you. My back is terrible. You know, this house, the deck is beautiful, by the way. Is this original cedar?"

"I think Martin had it redone maybe six, seven years ago. He mentioned it."

"It's gorgeous. And the view." She looked out at the lake. The September light was hitting the water at an angle that turned it amber near the shore and steel-gray farther out. A breeze was coming off the water, warm enough for September but with an October edge underneath it, the first real suggestion that the weather was thinking ahead. Two loons were working the shallows by the dock, and she could hear one of them calling, that sound like someone laughing at a joke they didn't want to be laughing at. "I'd never leave."

"It's hard to beat."

"Did Martin use the place a lot? Year-round, or just summer and early fall?"

"Mostly summer and early fall. The draft weekend, obviously. Some winter weekends, I think, for ice fishing. He wasn't up here full-time."

"And he kept it nice. The inside, I was admiring the study. That little room off the hallway, is that the..."

"That's not the study," Cliff said. "The study is two doors past the half bath. Farther down the hall."

She stopped writing and looked up. She let the look hold for exactly the amount of time it took Cliff's expression to adjust, a quarter-second of openness replaced by the smooth surface reforming.

"Two doors past the half bath," she repeated. "I thought it was that first room, the one with the coats."

"No, that's a closet. The study is the next one. It's got a desk, bookshelves, a painting of the lake, and a yellow legal pad open on the blotter."

"A yellow pad." Mallick looked back to the page and wrote it down. "He keeps one there all the time?"

Cliff blinked once. "Usually. Or he did the last time I was in there."

"Of course." She smiled and didn't look up. "Thank you for that. I always get turned around in these big houses. My place is eight hundred square feet, so anything with more than one hallway..." She laughed. "You've been to the study, then? During your visits?"

The pause was shorter than Decker's had been. Haywood was faster at recalibrating. "Over the years, sure. Martin and I had some business together. He'd show me paperwork sometimes. It's not, I mean, we're friends. Were friends. People go in each other's rooms."

"Of course they do. I wasn't suggesting anything. Oh, goodness, no. I was just impressed you knew the layout so well. I've been to my sister's cabin a dozen times and I still can't find the bathroom." She shook her head, self-deprecating, and flipped to a new page. "Now. When you went to bed last night, eleven-thirty, you said, did you hear anything during the night? Anybody moving around, doors, that sort of thing?"

Cliff took a sip of his coffee. The sip lasted a beat too long. "I'm a heavy sleeper. Once I'm out, I'm out."

"Oh, I envy that. I haven't slept through a whole night since about 2004. The cats wake me up. I've got two, and they..." She stopped herself, waving a hand. "Sorry. Nobody needs to hear about my cats. So you didn't hear anything. And you woke up when? What time this morning?"

"Six-fifteen, six-thirty. Around there."

"And Martin was, you knew already? Somebody told you?"

"Paul told me. He was in the kitchen when I came down. He said Martin died during the night."

"Paul told you in the kitchen. Was anybody else there?"

"Tommy. Tommy was there. And I think Nate was somewhere. I could hear him on the phone, maybe in the other room."

She wrote it all down. Then, under *study,* she added *yellow pad open?* and circled it once. The layout could have been old knowledge. The open pad felt newer. She wanted the room before anybody had another minute alone with it.

"Cliff, I really appreciate this. You've been so helpful. Can I ask you one more thing?"

"Of course."

"The casserole I brought, is it still in the refrigerator? I can't remember if I asked somebody to shove it in there."

"I think Paul put it in."

"Bless him. If it gets pushed behind the mustard I'll be taking home a science project." She stood up. "I'll go check on it. Would you mind pointing Whitfield my way if you see him? Doug Whitfield."

"He went down toward the dock a while ago."

"The dock. All right. Thank you."

Haywood nodded and stood and shook her hand. He shook her hand, which nobody else had done, and that told her everything she already suspected about the way he moved through the world. It was a handshake that ended interviews and closed deals. His grip was firm and calibrated, like he'd settled on this exact pressure years ago and never revised it.

She returned the grip and smiled and went inside. On the way through the great room she passed the big one, Renaldi, the coach, coming out of the half bath. He was drying his hands on his jeans and he looked at her the way you'd look at a car coming the wrong way down a one-lane road: total focus, no expression. She said, "Oh, excuse me," and he said, "Yeah," and moved past her with his shoulders turned slightly, the way a large man does when he doesn't want to touch you in a hallway. She filed that away too, the shoulders, the single syllable, the hands still damp on the denim.

---

She opened the refrigerator long enough to verify the casserole was still on the shelf where Decker had tucked it, untouched and sweating cold under the plastic lid, then shut the door.

At the kitchen counter she dropped the neighborly voice and flipped back through her notes.

She drew a line from Paul's boxed times to *door shut*, then another from Cliff's correction to *yellow pad open?* She still didn't know whether Haywood had just betrayed old familiarity or this morning's traffic, and she didn't have time to stand there deciding it.

Through the deck doors she could see Whitfield down at the dock bench, seventy or eighty yards away through the pines and birch, sitting still as fence wire with his head bent toward the water.

She took her notebook and went out.

The stone path to the dock was warm under the late sun. Pine needles popped under her flats. Whitfield didn't turn when he heard her coming. Up close he looked even rougher than he had from the house. He had hollowed cheeks, the gray scrape of beard, and that wired-tired look you got when your body stopped remembering how to sleep.

"Mr. Whitfield?"

He looked up.

"I'm Mallick. I was hoping we could talk for a few minutes."

He held her eyes, then looked back at the lake. "Forgot meds."

It was two words, quiet enough that she almost missed them.

"You need a few minutes."

He nodded once.

He wasn't evasive, she thought. It was logistical. Or maybe it was chemical. The line of his jaw held that clenched patience of somebody waiting for his own body to catch up with him.

"All right," she said. "Come find me when you've got them in you."

He gave her a quick glance at that, embarrassment and gratitude getting mixed together and canceling each other out, then nodded again. "Yeah."

She walked back up to the house before he'd had to decide whether to say anything else. He didn't call her back. He didn't need to. Whitfield wasn't dodging. He wanted a little time for whatever he'd forgotten to kick in before he had to sit under her questions.

She didn't have to go looking for Renaldi. He was in the great room by the sliding door with a paper cup of water in one hand, staring through the screen at the lake as if it had personally offended him.

"Coach," she said. "You got a minute for me?"

Tommy Renaldi turned. "Yeah."

He came to the table without asking whether he had a choice. Up close he looked bigger than he had in the hallway, the kind of big that made furniture look temporary. The pale burn scar on his left forearm showed when he set the paper cup down. He kept flexing the fingers of his right hand against his thigh, not full fidgeting, more like a man checking that his hand was still there.

"Now, you're Tommy. Tommy Renaldi. Apple Valley football."

"Eastview," he said. "Apple Valley's where I live."

"Eastview. Shoot. That's right." She wrote it down, though she'd had it written down since this morning. "My nephew played Eastview once. Got flattened by a nose guard and talked about it like he'd seen combat. Boys are funny that way."

"Yeah."

There was no sales pitch, no qualifying language, and no urge to be liked. Decker had answered like a man filling out a form. Haywood had answered like a man sliding a brochure across a desk. Tommy answered like every question was a drill he didn't respect.

"So. Last night." She clicked her pen. "Draft, drinks, hot tub, bed. You turned in around midnight?"

"Twelve-thirty."

"And you were upstairs on the left if you're facing the lake."

"Right."

"On the right. Sorry." She smiled at him over her glasses. "I keep flipping this house around in my head."

"Yeah."

"Happens to me with A-frames. They all look like they were designed by somebody who hated hallways." She let him not laugh. "When did you wake up?"

"After two. Maybe a little after."

"Because Paul came and got you?"

Tommy shook his head. "Because there was noise."

"What kind of noise?"

"People moving around. Nate. Paul. Doors. I don't know." He rubbed the back of his neck once, hard. "House wasn't exactly quiet."

That was useful. She wrote it down.

"And then Paul told you Martin was gone."

"Yeah."

"Did you go into Martin's room?"

"No."

It was a fast answer, faster than Paul's. Tommy didn't look away after he gave it, though. He held her eyes like he was willing the line to stay simple by force.

"Did you go upstairs again before the deputies got here?"

"To my room."

"For what?"

"Phone charger. Washed my face. Same reasons anybody goes upstairs in the morning."

"Sure. Of course." She made the note in large block letters. "The service is better outside here than in my place in Detroit Lakes. Out by the drive especially. Half the county feels like talking through a sock these days. Did you make the 911 call from the driveway or from the deck?"

"Driveway."

"Mm-hmm." She nodded as if this had only answered a weather question. "Now, when you went up for your charger, Martin's bedroom door was shut, right?"

Tommy's head came up a fraction. "No."

"No?"

"It was open a little."

She looked down at her notebook. "Open a little."

"Yeah."

"How little is a little."

"Couple inches. Maybe more. I didn't measure it."

"Oh." She kept writing. "That's odd. Paul told me he closed it after he tried CPR."

Tommy leaned back in the chair. Wood gave a dry complaint under his weight. "Then somebody opened it."

"Did you?"

"No."

"Did you look in?"

"No." Then, because the first no had come too cleanly, he added, "Not really."

She let the gap open. People hated gaps more than questions.

"Not really," she repeated.

"I saw the door was open. That's all." His jaw tightened. "I didn't go in there if that's what you're asking."

"I'm not asking anything special. I'm trying to keep straight who was where in the house before breakfast. That's all." She turned a page. "Who else was upstairs by then?"

"I don't know. Everybody's bags were up there. People were in and out."

"Doug?"

"Maybe."

"Nate?"

"I don't know."

"Haywood?"

"I said I don't know."

There it was, the coaching edge coming through, volume not yet up but the muscles behind it warming.

"All right. No problem." She put a palm up, neighborly. "I ask the same thing six ways. Occupational defect."

Tommy exhaled through his nose. "Yeah."

"One more and then I'll let you go." She tapped the pen against the notebook. "This morning when you came downstairs, was the hall light on upstairs or off?"

He frowned, not because he didn't know but because he didn't see why she got to ask. "Off."

"And Martin's door was still open then."

"Yeah."

"Was it wide enough to see the bed from the hall?"

"I didn't stand there checking sight lines, Detective."

"No, of course not."

"I came down. Paul was in the kitchen. That's what happened." He leaned forward now, forearms on the table. "Look, if you're asking whether I was up there screwing around with the room, I wasn't. Guy died. Everybody was rattled. That's the whole story."

That wasn't the whole story, she thought. But it was a strong candidate for the voice he'd chosen to tell it in.

"Rattled makes people do all kinds of housekeeping they didn't mean to do," she said mildly.

Tommy stared at her.

"I'm sorry?"

"Nothing." She gave him the soft little shrug. "Just talking. My mother used to wipe counters when she got bad news. Different generation."

He kept staring another beat, then sat back. "You got anything else?"

"No, I think that's enough for now." She closed the notebook. "If you see Mr. Griggs, would you send him my way?"

Tommy stood. "He'll be downstairs."

"That's what I was afraid of."

That almost got a smile out of him, but not quite. Then he took his paper cup and walked off toward the sink, shoulders squared like he was heading back onto a field where the game had already gone bad.

Mallick waited until she heard the cup hit the counter. Then she opened her notebook and wrote one line:

*Renaldi: says Martin's door open by morning though Paul says he shut it after CPR. Upstairs before deputies for charger/wash face. Too quick on denial.*

Below it she boxed three words: *sternum, sheets, clock.*

If Decker had done four real minutes of CPR, the room or the body ought to admit it somewhere.

There were six men in the house. Five of them were telling versions of sleep. By breakfast the body had been handled, a bedroom door had been reopened, and maybe the study had seen traffic too. She still couldn't tell whether one man had been making rounds or whether everybody had touched the scene for a different reason. Either way, the house had not held still.

She tore the page out, folded it into her blazer pocket, and went to find her phone. The county medical examiner's office picked up on the third ring.

"It's Detective Mallick in Becker County," she said. "Before you get too far with Kessler, I want the sternum photographed and the bedding held."

She hung up and stepped into the hall. "I need Griggs now," she told the nearest deputy. "Before he hears what anybody else just told me."

Griggs was a talker. Talkers only got worse with prep time.


# Chapter 6

It was the goddamn voicemail. Nate Griggs stood in the basement holding his phone toward the ceiling, tilting it through a range of useless angles and watching the signal bar blink in and out while the search page loaded in pieces: Google logo first, then a white void, then the top result crawling in letter by letter.

*Can police recover deleted voicemails from a phone*

The first result was a legal advice subreddit, which no serious person would consult and Nate was going to read with the concentration he'd bring to his own biopsy results. He thumbed it open. The page loaded in strips, each paragraph arriving on its own schedule, the top comment sitting there with a thousand upvotes and a username that sounded more gamer-tag than legal authority.

*Depends on carrier. Verizon keeps metadata (call time, duration, number) for 1-2 years. Actual voicemail audio is generally retained on their servers for 30-40 days after the user deletes it. Law enforcement can access recordings through a subpoena or court order.*

Thirty to forty days, if Reddit knew what it was talking about. He was on day one.

Nate sat down on the pull-out sofa. The mattress was thin enough that the metal frame bit into the backs of his thighs through the fabric. Doug's duffel bag sat on the other pull-out, zipped so tight the seams lay flat. Doug packed everything flat and compact, no bulge, no spill. Even his bag managed to stay out of the way. The lake was audible through the small casement windows, a steady lapping that Nate's brain, helpfully, converted into a countdown.

That was the whole problem.

He'd left it a little before nine on Friday evening, after the draft was done and they were all on the deck with beers and Martin was doing his thing where he pretended to be above trash talk by simply not responding to trash talk, which was Martin's most infuriating move and had been for twenty-five years. Nate had taken his phone to the basement bathroom, the one with the shower that smelled like lake water and old PVC, and he'd called Martin's cell and left a voicemail because that was their thing, the bit, leaving long ranting voicemails about each other's draft picks because who leaves voicemails anymore, that was the joke. They were the last seven guys in America who used voicemail on purpose.

There were six guys in the house now, and the number kept slamming into him.

He'd gone up to Martin's room two hours ago, thinking he could check the phone one more time, make sure the deletion had taken, see if there was a "recently deleted" folder he'd missed in the dark at around three-fifteen in the morning. The nightstand had been bare. Martin's phone, charger cord, watch, wallet: all of it bagged and taken after the deputies finally got there, well after the seven a.m. call to dispatch. The bedroom door was shut now with a strip of yellow tape across it that looked like it came from a hardware store. Martin's phone was in an evidence room somewhere in Detroit Lakes, and Nate couldn't check whether the deletion had actually worked, couldn't verify that the voicemail wasn't sitting in some purgatory folder waiting for a detective with a subpoena to bring it back to life, if there was anything left to bring back.

He kept coming back to the voicemail, to what he'd actually said. He could hear it in his own voice, the podcast cadence, the buildup rhythm he used when he was doing his weekly "Roster Autopsy" segment, and the word *autopsy* was another one he wished he could take back from the universe:

*Kessler. Martin. My guy. I've been staring at your roster for like fifteen minutes and I am — I'm composing your obituary right now. This team is dead. You are dead to me, Kessler. Dead. You took Addison in the fourth — the fourth, Martin — when Nico Collins was right there, just sitting there, staring at you, begging you to take him, and you went with a guy who had six targets last week. Six. That's not a wide receiver, dude, that's a bystander at the scene of a crime. That's a guy who showed up to the stadium and accidentally ended up on the field. And then — AND THEN — you took the Vikings defense in the seventh round. A defense. In the seventh. I can't — I actually can't with you right now. I'm at your funeral, man. I am standing at the podium delivering the eulogy and I'm telling people, 'He was a good man. A generous man. And he took a defense in the seventh round and deserved everything he got.' And I haven't even — shit, wait — the Pacheco pick, which I'm saving for the pod, because I want to do a full segment on how a man murders his own season in front of six witnesses and nobody intervenes. Your roster is a corpse, Kessler. A goddamn corpse. I'm performing the autopsy Monday morning. Rest in peace, your playoff hopes, 2024 to 2024.*

It went on. It went on for another ninety seconds after that. He'd riffed on Martin's backup quarterback as a "death wish," called the bench "a graveyard," circled back to the Addison pick a second time because he'd lost track, and signed off with "this message will self-destruct, much like your season and your legacy and honestly your dignity as a human being." He'd said *dead* four times, *kill* twice, *murder* once, *funeral* once, *obituary* once, *corpse* twice, and *rest in peace* at the end like a sign-off for a man who was going to be dead in six hours.

Nate put his phone face-down on the mattress and pressed his palms against his eyes. He could still see the afterimage of the search results through his eyelids. Maybe the thirty-to-forty-day window was real. The voicemail might still be sitting on a server in Iowa or wherever they kept the things, or maybe the internet strangers were wrong and he was destroying his nervous system over call logs. He had no way to tell.

At around three-fifteen in the morning, he'd gone into Martin's room, where Martin was still in the bed, still dead, his arm hanging off the edge at the angle that had made Nate check on him in the first place. He'd picked up Martin's phone from the nightstand, the screen protector cracked in one corner, the home screen still a photo of the lake at sunset, and his hands had been shaking so badly he'd had to use both thumbs to navigate the voicemail app. He'd found the message, his own name next to it, 8:47 PM, three minutes forty-one seconds. He'd pressed delete. The phone had asked him to confirm. He'd confirmed. And then he'd stood there in the dark room next to his friend's body and the part of his brain that formatted experience into content had clicked on, clean and hateful: *this would make an incredible episode.*

He hated himself for that thought. Even the self-loathing came packaged like a segment outline: confession, vulnerability, producer-Nate whispering that honesty played. His friend was dead in a lake house in Becker County and Nate's brain was still pitching beats for a show with a 2.3-star rating on Apple Podcasts and four hundred dollars a month in revenue before Mia's student loans.

His phone buzzed. A text from Mia, timestamped six hours ago: *How's the weekend going? Miss you.*

He hadn't answered. He hadn't answered because what was he going to say, *Martin's dead and there's a detective upstairs who brought a casserole and I committed a felony at three in the morning*? He left the phone on the mattress.

The ceiling above him creaked. Someone crossed the kitchen. A cabinet door clicked shut, then a mug touched the counter upstairs. Nate caught himself sorting the sounds into layers, footsteps, ceramic, lake water under all of it, the way he did when he was listening for clean tape, and that made him angrier than the sounds themselves.

He needed better signal and something more useful than *maybe*. If carriers only kept call metadata, they'd see a three-minute call from Nate's number to Martin's at 8:47 PM and maybe ask a question. If somebody still had the audio file with Nate saying *you are dead to me, Kessler* and *I'm at your funeral,* that was a different category of problem. He needed to know whether deleted meant deleted without doing one more thing that looked like panic.

He stood up too fast. A head rush hit him. He'd had six beers and a scotch last night, maybe ninety minutes of sleep total, and twelve hours of adrenaline chewing through him. His "Griggs on the Grid" cap was on the floor by the sofa, the logo Paul once called "the saddest thing in podcasting." He picked it up, put it on, took it off. The cap was a prop, part of the show, and the show was the last thing he needed right now, but his hand wanted it the way his mouth wanted to narrate and his brain wanted to riff. It was all the same muscle, and the muscle did not know how to be still.

He went upstairs. The third step creaked under him.

Paul was in the kitchen with a glass of water. He took one look at the phone in Nate's hand and tipped his head toward the deck.

"Better signal out there," he said.

"Right. Yeah."

Nate kept moving.

The deck was getting cold. September in northern Minnesota did that. The afternoon had been golden and warm, the lake flat and bright, and then the sun dropped behind the white pines and suddenly you needed a hoodie and your breath was considering becoming visible. Nate sat in one of the Adirondack chairs and held his phone out and watched the signal bars flicker between one and two.

He loaded a new search: *restore deleted voicemail Verizon account owner law enforcement*

The top carrier page was blunter than the Reddit thread had been: deleted voicemails could sometimes be restored through customer service, after account verification, within a limited window. A law-firm FAQ under that said providers could also freeze voicemail records once law enforcement sent a preservation request. That was new, and worse. Nate couldn't call pretending to be Martin without making a record of the call, couldn't answer the account questions even if he tried, and couldn't touch Martin's phone because Becker County already had it in a bag.

The two people who could get a clean answer were Martin and Detective Mallick. One was dead. The other was inside with a casserole.

Nate sat with one bar of signal and the first useful plan he'd had all day: stop talking. He wouldn't volunteer context, get ahead of anything, or say *voicemail* unless somebody else said it first.

The sliding glass door opened behind him. He flinched hard enough to knock his phone off the armrest.

"Mr. Griggs?" It was Mallick. She was holding a coffee mug and smiling with the same easy neighborly warmth she'd brought in with the casserole. She'd found him faster than he'd hoped. "I was hoping we could have a little chat, if you're not in the middle of something. Oh, did you drop your phone? These chairs, they're beautiful but the arms are so narrow."

---

She'd set up at the kitchen table. Her leather notebook was open, her reading glasses were on the beaded chain around her neck, and the green bean casserole she'd brought that morning sat in the refrigerator behind her, still covered in foil and untouched. Nobody was going to eat a casserole brought by a detective to the house where their friend had just died. It also wasn't helping that it was green bean casserole and nobody actually liked green bean casserole.

"Sit, sit," Mallick said, pulling out the chair across from her. "I'm so sorry to bother you. I know this has been just an awful day. Can I get you anything? I think there's still coffee in the pot. Oh, shoot. I think I turned it off. Did I turn it off? I'm always doing that at the station, Debbie gets so mad at me."

"I'm good," Nate said. He sat down. The chair legs scraped on the floor and the sound was too loud for the room.

"So." She put her reading glasses on and looked at her notebook. "Nathan Griggs. You go by Nate?"

"Nate. Yeah. Just Nate."

"And you mentioned you're a podcaster? That's so neat. What's the show, 'Griggs on the Go'?"

"'The Grid.' 'Griggs on the Grid.' It's a fantasy football show. Draft strategy, weekly matchups, start-sit decisions, that sort of thing."

"Oh, I'm sorry, 'the Grid.' Like the football field? The lines?"

"Sort of. More like the roster grid, the player, it's a spreadsheet thing, kind of." He watched her write something in her notebook. She'd gotten the name wrong and he'd corrected her and she'd written something down and Joel had told them all, two hours ago, that she did this with everyone, the wrong detail followed by the correction followed by the note.

"How fun," Mallick said. "My nephew Tyler does something like that, I think. The thing where you pick the players?"

"That's it. Yeah. So you draft real NFL players onto a fake roster, and then their real-game stats generate points for your team. It's a huge industry. Thirty-three million Americans play. I've done a twelve-part series on the market size alone. Not that that matters." He heard himself doing it, giving her the long version because silence made his skin itch. Mallick sat there with her coffee and let him keep going.

"Thirty-three million," Mallick said. "My goodness. And you all came up here to do that? To pick your teams?"

"Twenty-five years. Same weekend, same house, same guys. Martin set it up our first year out of college and it became the thing. The one thing nobody missed." He stopped. "Well. Martin's the one who can't. Who didn't." He stopped again. Death had always been show language for him, a segment title, a tagline, and now every version of the word felt filthy.

"Twenty-five years," Mallick said, and it sounded warm rather than evidentiary. "That's real loyalty. And how did the draft go? Thursday night?"

"Good. It was fun. The usual, guys arguing over picks, Tommy yelling, Joel trying to rules-lawyer every trade. Cliff doing Cliff stuff where he offers you something that sounds great until you realize you just gave up your first-round pick for a backup tight end and a promise."

"And Mr. Kessler? Did he have a good draft?"

Nate laughed before he could stop it, reflexive and genuine, and then heard it land in the kitchen wrong, a laugh about the draft performance of a friend who was in a morgue. "No. He had a terrible draft. Martin always had a terrible draft and then somehow made the playoffs anyway, which, I've actually done the numbers on this on the pod, his projected finish versus his actual finish over twenty-five years, and the variance is..."

"Oh, that's thorough. What made it so bad? His draft?"

He could feel the slide starting. The things that made Martin's draft terrible were the same things he'd ranted about in the voicemail, and silence was still the one skill Nate Griggs had never learned.

"He reached for guys. He always reached. He took Addison in the fourth when there was, look, clear value sitting there on the board, and he went with the name he recognized instead of the guy the numbers said to take. Then a defense in the seventh. That's a cardinal sin. You don't draft a defense until the last two rounds. Everyone knows that. And the kicker in the tenth. I mean, we all gave him shit about it. That's the whole point. The draft isn't just about picking players. It's..." He caught himself. His brain had been accelerating toward *the trash talk is the game, the insults are the love language, when I said his team was dead that was just...* "It's the arguing. The competition. It's the only time most of us see each other."

Mallick nodded, sipping coffee. "Now, you mentioned you record things for your show. Were you recording Thursday night?"

"Parts of it. No, it wasn't a hidden camera situation. Ambient draft audio, reactions, the group ripping each other. I always get some tape for the pod. Martin was fine with it. Everyone was fine with it." He heard the extra explanation coming out of him and couldn't stop it. Anxiety was already three sentences ahead of the conversation.

"Oh, that's wonderful. We might want to have a listen to that, if you wouldn't mind."

"Sure. Absolutely. It's just the draft. It's just us being us."

"Great." She made a note. "And your phone, was that the only way you'd be in touch with Mr. Kessler? Texting, calling, that kind of thing?"

"Yeah, texts mostly. We've got a group chat that's been going for, I mean, years. Thousands of messages. Draft talk, trades, the league standings. The group chat is basically the league and the league is basically the group chat."

"Mm-hmm. And phone calls?"

"Less. We're all texters mostly. Sometimes you'd call to say come out to the deck, or ask somebody to bring more ice up from the basement, that kind of thing."

"So no calls to Mr. Kessler that Friday? The day before?"

The question arrived clean and Nate could feel every door in it, the three or four ways he could answer that would be short, functional, and safe: *Maybe, I don't remember. Probably a quick one. Nothing important.* He had clean exits and his mouth walked past all of them because Nate's mouth did not take direction from the part of his brain that understood consequences.

"I mean — yeah, probably, at some point? We were all here, you call people. And voicemails, that's — we do this thing. A voicemail thing. Where you leave long, stupid voicemails about each other's picks, because nobody leaves voicemails anymore, right? So it's this ironic — it's a bit. Like, your mom leaves voicemails. Bill collectors. That's it. So you leave a three-minute rant about somebody's terrible roster because that's the joke, that's the whole — because voicemail is basically a dead—" He heard it, the word *dead* arriving in the sentence like a siren, and he tried to steer around it. "—a thing nobody uses. So we use it. As a joke."

"That is funny," Mallick said. She was looking at him with warm, settled attention. Nothing about her suggested she had anywhere else to be. She was not writing anything, which was either because what he was saying didn't matter or because she was letting him build speed, and he didn't know which was worse.

"And the things we say — this is the thing I want to explain, because it sounds — if you heard it without context, the shit we say to each other, it's — you say things like 'your team's done,' 'you're finished,' 'rest in peace your season,' and that's not — when I said — look, when you say a guy's roster is dead, you don't mean—"

He stopped. His hands were frozen mid-gesture, both of them, in the air between him and the detective, shaped around a word he was trying to take back. He'd said *when I said it.* Not *when you say it.* Not *when someone says it.* *When I said it.* It was past tense. It was first person. He sounded like a man describing a thing he had done.

Mallick set her coffee mug down on the table. She picked up her pen.

"I'm sorry," she said. Her voice was the same voice it had been, the neighborly voice, the my-nephew-plays-that voice. "When you said his roster was dead? To Mr. Kessler?"

"That's just how we — everyone says that—"

"Oh, sure, sure. I'm just, I want to make sure my notes are right. I didn't have any messages from you to Mr. Kessler that evening. But you're saying there was a voicemail?"

The kitchen went quiet. Through the window above the sink, the last light sat flat on the lake. Mallick's notebook was open to a page of small, even handwriting, and Nate had the sudden, sick certainty that every lawyer on earth would tell him the same thing right now: stop talking. He was already past that.

"I left a voicemail," Nate said. "Friday night. On his phone. I was — it was a bit on his draft. How bad his picks were. And I said things like 'you're dead to me' and 'I'm at your funeral' and 'your roster is a goddamn corpse,' and that's just — that's how you talk about fantasy football. Every guy in every league says that stuff. Thirty-three million people talk like that."

"I understand," Mallick said. She was writing.

"And I deleted it."

He said it fast, like getting the worst part out before his nerve caught up. Once it was in the room, the rest followed.

"I went to his room. After we found him. Early, like three-fifteen in the morning. And I went on his phone and I deleted the voicemail. Because I heard it back in my head after he died and it was — I'm saying 'you're dead to me' and 'I'm at your funeral,' and that's just sports, that's just the game, but if someone listened to it who didn't know—"

"Without the fantasy football context," Mallick said.

"Yes. Exactly. That's exactly it. Without context, it sounds insane. So I went in and deleted it. That's what I did."

Mallick's pen stopped moving. She looked at what she'd written, then looked at him over her reading glasses.

"Mr. Griggs, I just want to make sure I have this right. You entered Mr. Kessler's room after he was found deceased, you accessed his phone, and you deleted a voicemail you had left earlier that evening. Is that accurate?"

Put that way, it sounded like exactly what it was: a man going into a dead man's room and tampering with a dead man's phone. Five minutes of context were gone in one sentence.

"It was a fantasy football voicemail," Nate said. His voice was smaller now. The performance was over. The pacing was gone, along with the hand gestures and the whole build. The sentences were short and had nowhere to go. "Three minutes. Just me being an idiot about his picks. That's all it was."

Mallick closed her notebook. She didn't snap it shut. She closed it with the calm of someone who had reached the point she'd been waiting for. She took off her reading glasses and let them hang on the beaded chain.

"Well," she said. "I really do appreciate you telling me that, Nate. Thank you."

She used his first name. She hadn't used his first name before. He wasn't sure whether that was better or worse and he couldn't ask because asking would also become information, and everything he said to this woman became information, and the only way to stop giving her information was to stop talking, and stopping was the one thing he had never learned to do.
