"I think we might need to follow up on a few things," she said. She stood and took her mug to the sink. "Oh, can I warm you up some coffee? Shoot, I did turn it off. I'm sorry about that. I always do that."

Nate sat at the kitchen table and looked at the refrigerator with the foil-covered casserole inside and the draft board still hanging on the great room wall.

His phone pressed against his thigh. He still hadn't answered Mia's text. Through the kitchen window the lake was going dark, the amber light pulling off the surface, and he could already feel the upstairs version of himself assembling, the one who'd start talking too loud and too evenly so the other guys would hear calm and maybe mistake it for truth.


# Chapter 7

Doug took his pills in the basement bathroom with the door locked, though he didn't need to. Nate was upstairs, talking through the floorboards in that pitch he used when he wanted other people to hear how calm he was, as if calm could be proved by volume.

He started with the Lexapro. The label read Escitalopram oxalate, 10 milligrams, once daily with or without food. The white sticker carried his name in hard black print, Douglas Whitfield, apartment number in Richfield beneath it like an address somebody else had borrowed. Clinical data said absorption ticked up a little with food, and Doug hadn't eaten since yesterday's lunch. He swallowed it dry and felt chalk bloom on his tongue. It had been three months now, the same as the divorce. The prescription had arrived two weeks after Steph left. Maybe it was working. Maybe he was just getting used to waking up in a different life.

He took the Ativan next. Its label read Lorazepam, 0.5 milligrams, as needed. It was needed.

For years he'd sold both classes of drug, back when brand names still mattered and lunch got you into the back office. Now he tipped one into his palm and took it from the tap with the same hand he'd once used to pass out sample cards. The mirror showed nothing new except the number on the scale back in Richfield: twenty-seven pounds gone since Steph said she was done. It was the best diet he'd ever been on. He'd have had a hard time recommending it with a straight face.

Both bottles were in his duffel now, zipped into the side pocket under a rolled pair of socks and a phone charger he'd brought for Nate and already forgotten to hand over. They'd started the weekend in the shared upstairs bathroom, tucked between Tommy's industrial bottle of Advil and Paul's toothpaste after everybody dumped their gear there on Thursday evening. The same clutter showed up every year: toothbrushes, razors, Cliff's moisturizer nobody was allowed to mention. Doug's bottles had sat there through Thursday night and all of Friday. Around three Saturday morning, he went up and got them.

That was after Nate shook him awake to say Martin was dead, after the six of them stood around in the great room not believing it, after Paul pounded on a chest that wasn't coming back, after somebody said call 911 and somebody else said wait. While the house creaked and men moved in other rooms and a toilet flushed overhead, Doug took the stairs, skipped the third step because everybody knew better, opened the medicine cabinet, and lifted both bottles out with his thumb over the labels so they wouldn't click together.

It wasn't because of evidence or detectives. He could already see the question arriving in a stranger's mouth and six old friends' faces behind it.

*Are you under psychiatric care, Mr. Whitfield?*

Tommy would go flat and careful. Joel would store it. Paul would do that insurance face that looked like concern and audit at the same time. Nate's whole face would say *shit, Doug*. Cliff wouldn't say anything at all, which would be worse.

Twenty-five years with this group had left him with habits. He gave up the chair with the lake view. He took the beer run nobody remembered assigning. He laughed before a story stalled out. He volunteered for cigarettes, ice, garbage, all the little errands that let a man leave a room without anybody asking why. The bottles would change that. So he took them. If that counted as tampering, it was tampering by shame.

Upstairs, the kitchen was bright with lake light. Paul had made coffee, strong as ever because Martin liked it that way. Martin's Lagavulin still hung in the great room, smoke and iodine caught in the paneling. The bottle stood on the counter with two fingers left in it, cap off because Martin never screwed the cap back on. The kitchen still held a faint onion-and-browned-cheese smell from the dish Mallick had brought and tucked away, under the coffee and the lake damp and the old scotch in the wood.

Doug poured into the blue mug with the chipped handle and skipped the cream. Cream in coffee still brought Steph back whole: her feet tucked under her on the couch, both hands around the mug, blowing once across the top even when it did not need it. She used to leave a half-inch in the bottom every time. She said the last swallow was always punishment.

Tommy was already on the deck, working the railing with both hands, pace-turn-pace, knuckles cracking in little bursts. Morning light caught the scar on his left forearm. Tommy had said years ago that it was a grill accident. Maybe it was. Doug had never asked. Not asking was part of what he did for people.

At the table, Joel sat with nothing in front of him. He had no mug, no phone, no newspaper, just his jaw moving like he was trying a case in his own head and the witness kept changing the answer.

"You eat?" Paul said.

"Not hungry."

Paul slid the bread bag six inches closer anyway. "You should anyway."

"Yeah. Probably."

Paul gave him the usual once-over, the one that started as friendship and drifted too easily into triage. Martin's version had always been cruder. Martin would have shoved toast at him and told him to quit looking like a nineteenth-century orphan. Paul just let the bread sit there like a quiet recommendation and turned back to the stove.

Nate came down the stairs in the middle of a sentence and kept going. Mallick hadn't asked about the hot tub yet, right? Because the chemicals had to matter somehow. Chlorine or bromine or whatever Martin used, and maybe that was nothing, but what if it wasn't nothing, because she'd asked him about the coffee maker and who asked about a coffee maker unless they were building toward something.

"Nobody asked about the hot tub," Joel said.

"Not yet."

"She won't."

"How do you know that?"

"Because it isn't relevant."

Nate looked at Doug for backup. Doug gave him a small nod, meaning nothing in particular, and Nate took it as permission to keep spiraling.

Cliff came in from the hallway showered, hair still damp, coffee mug in hand, the only man in the house who seemed to believe Monday still existed. He topped off his coffee, asked if anybody wanted toast, and set bread in the toaster with the brisk competence of a man who thought order might still be available if you moved fast enough.

"Toast?" Cliff asked him.

"I'm good."

"It's sourdough."

"I'm fine."

Orange coils lit behind the toaster slots.

Coffee in hand, Doug moved to the corner of the sectional by the window. The draft board still hung on the wall. Martin's commissioner picks were circled in red, same as always: Kelce in the second, which made everybody groan; Lamar in the third, which started a five-minute argument Thursday night; in the seventh, a rookie receiver nobody had heard of, followed by Nate asking if Martin was drafting him or adopting him. Martin had smiled through all of it. That was what Doug kept catching on now, not the jokes or the arguments, just the smile and the little gap between Martin's front teeth when he liked his own move.

The board turned the room into a before-and-after picture. Thursday night, Martin had been alive and needling everybody. Now the same red circles looked like somebody had marked evidence.

Paul reached past him for the bread bag, then the spoon. Nate checked Doug's face for a signal and took a nod for permission. Cliff asked about toast because he was asking everybody, not because he expected Doug to complicate the morning. Out on the deck Tommy kept cracking his knuckles. Joel sat at the table with his jaw working and let his face say whatever it wanted.

From the corner of the sectional Doug had a clean view. Tommy was trying to wear panic out through his hands. Joel had gone so still it looked deliberate. Nate kept talking because silence would make him hear himself think. Paul was making coffee and acting like competence could keep a body in the room. Cliff looked like he had already arranged tomorrow.

---

After lunch Mallick found him on the deck with the paperback he'd brought from Richfield open on his knee and unread.

"Mr. Whitfield? Doug? You got a minute?"

He had nothing else to do.

She settled into the next Adirondack chair with the leather notebook in her lap and the click-pen she'd been worrying all morning. The glasses on the beaded chain bumped lightly against the notebook when she wrote.

"We talked a little yesterday," she said. "I just had a couple things I wanted to make sure I had right. How'd you sleep Friday night?"

"Fine."

"All the way through?"

"Yeah."

"Some of the other fellas mentioned noises. Footsteps and a door and all that. Basement might've been quieter, though."

"It was."

Her pen scratched. She looked back toward the house.

"I keep looking at that board in there," she said. "All those names and the little red circles — somebody told me Martin did those himself? The commissioner, is that right?" She flipped a page back. "How'd you two know each other, you and Martin?"

"College. Same group."

"Were you close?"

"We were friends."

"A good friend?"

"He was good to me."

That made her look up. She didn't pounce, exactly. She just looked up.

"In what way?"

Doug thought of July, not the vague idea of it but the actual call. It had been Wednesday afternoon, too hot outside, blinds shut in the apartment because the sun on the beige carpet made the whole place look temporary. He had been half asleep on the mattress on the floor when Martin called instead of texting.

*Did I wake you?*

*No.*

*Whit, it's three-forty.*

Doug had laughed then, too fast. He said he worked in the field and the field was very flexible. Martin had let that go by without even the courtesy chuckle. Then he asked what Doug had eaten. Doug said coffee. Martin said coffee was not food. Doug said divorce had been good for portion control. Martin said bullshit, flat and immediate, and told him to go see what food he actually had in the apartment.

Now Doug said, "He called me in July because I sounded half asleep in the middle of the afternoon. Asked what I'd eaten. Made me go look in my fridge."

Mallick waited.

"There wasn't much in it," Doug said. "I made a joke about the divorce diet."

"And he?"

"Said bullshit. Told me to come to the draft."

She wrote that down slower than the rest.

"That's a pretty specific kind of friend."

Doug looked out at the water and let that sit there.

"That sliding door," she said after a second, nodding toward the house. "Does it always rattle like that? I keep thinking somebody's coming in and it's just the wind working at the frame." She made a note. "Anyway. Friday night, after the hot tub and everybody finally shut up — what time did you go downstairs?"

"Midnight. Maybe later."

"And then you slept straight through."

"Yeah."

"Didn't hear Nate get up?"

Doug almost said *he's quiet* and stopped there, because even that sounded like more than he wanted on paper. "Didn't hear him."

Mallick nodded as if she'd been handed something useful, though all he'd given her were three or four flat stones.

"Anything else over the weekend strike you funny before Saturday morning?" she asked. "Arguments, bad blood, somebody acting off?"

Doug could have given her plenty: Joel's shut jaw on Thursday night, Tommy's extra voltage, Cliff asking Martin about roof age and property taxes like he was already halfway into an appraisal, even Nate working too hard at being Nate. He carried those scraps around because nobody ever asked him to do anything with them. Mallick, with her casserole manners and notebook, was probably the first person in the house who would.

"I don't think so."

"No?"

"They're always like this." Doug shrugged. "Twenty-five years. There's always something."

"Fantasy football," she said, smiling.

Doug didn't return it. The energy wasn't there.

She asked about the basement door, the lock, whether anybody else stored things down there. Doug answered in the fewest words available and let the gaps stay open after each one.

"Just you and Nate sleeping down there?"

"Yeah."

"Anybody use that little bathroom besides you two?"

"Not much."

"Storage room too, though? Laundry, bags, extra towels, all that?"

Doug felt the question in his ribs before he felt it in his face. "Some bags."

"Mm-hmm. People always spread out in places like this. You set down a duffel Thursday and by Saturday it lives there." She made another note. "Funny how fast a weekend gets itself organized."

Most people rushed to fill silence. Mallick didn't. She just let it sit between them, then put down one more line in the notebook.

When she stood, she paused the way she'd paused after every interview all weekend, leaving a little space for a last-minute correction or confession. Doug gave her nothing. Silence was the only thing in the house he trusted himself to handle better than she did. Something in her expression shifted, not annoyance, not surprise either, but recalculation. Then she nodded and went back inside.

Doug stayed on the deck until the sliding door shut behind her. Then he took the stone path through the pines toward the dock, dry needles giving under his shoes.

---

The dock pushed thirty feet into Elbow Lake, boards silvered by weather except for a few newer ones Martin had replaced over the years. A bench sat near the end. The swim platform was beyond it. Clear water below, sand and stones and a scatter of old beer caps sunk into the bottom. Near one piling a school of minnows held steady against nothing visible, all of them facing the same direction as if there'd been an instruction Doug had missed.

Doug sat on the bench with his feet hanging over the side.

The place still knew how to be itself. Soft September light lay on the water. Trees were just starting to turn on the far shore. A motorboat droned somewhere out of view, and its wake reached the dock late and gentle. Doug thought about the apartment in Richfield with the beige carpet and the neighbor who watched *Jeopardy!* loud enough to feel personal, then about the gray towels from Target, the mattress on the floor, the blank walls. The lake house pushed back with its own inventory: splinters in the dock, old beer caps under the water, Joel once coming up from a cannonball with blood running down his shin and suing Martin for negligent dockwork until Martin paid him off with a sixth-round pick.

That got a smile out of him.

The July call kept coming back because Mallick had made him say enough of it out loud to wake the rest. He had gone to the kitchen that day because Martin would not let him stay horizontal and wiseass his way out of it. There was mustard, two beers, soy sauce, a bagged salad gone wet, and half a takeout carton that had started to fur over in one corner.

*Read it to me,* Martin had said.

*I'm not doing inventory for you.*

*Then give me the highlights.*

Doug had read it because Martin had already heard too much: the slow voice, the air conditioner rattling in a room with no other sound, the way Doug kept trying to turn the call into a bit and Martin kept refusing to laugh. When Doug got to the takeout carton, Martin said, *Jesus Christ, Whit. That's not groceries. That's evidence.*

Doug smiled despite himself now, remembering that. At the time he had leaned his forehead against the cold door and said Martin sounded like a narc. Martin said good, maybe a narc was what Doug needed. Then he said, in the same plain irritated voice he'd use to tell somebody the grill was out of propane, *Come to the draft. You can sit in the basement and say six words all weekend. I don't care. Just come. I'm not doing this with you from Richfield.*

Doug had tried one more deflection. He said he had territory meetings. He said the timing was lousy. He said maybe next year, after things settled down.

Martin had cut straight through that too. *You're a forty-seven-year-old man sleeping on a mattress on the floor and living on mustard. Don't tell me about timing.*

Martin didn't make a speech after that. He didn't offer a soft landing. He just told the truth in his ordinary voice, which was worse because it meant he hadn't reached for drama to make the point. Doug had laughed because he wanted the call over. Martin stayed quiet until the laugh died on its own.

Then he said, *Tell me now if you're coming.*

Doug had come because Martin noticed things other people let pass. The weight loss, yes. The midafternoon sleep, yes. But more than that, the cheap way Doug kept trying to make his own deterioration easier to look at. Martin would let a joke breathe if it earned the air. He would not let one do the work of an answer.

That was the surprise now, sitting on the dock with the water knocking softly against the pilings. Martin dead, detective in the house, pill bottles hidden in a duffel, and Doug was still glad he'd come. These men were oblivious and exhausting and at least half full of shit, but the weekend gave him what the apartment didn't: noise, history, other bodies in the rooms. Martin had understood that much before Doug did.

A loon called out on the lake. By the time the sound thinned away, the light had gone from gold to gray.

Doug went back up the path and let himself into the house. The kitchen windows had gone blue. He ate one cold piece of Cliff's sourdough standing at the counter, the heel of the loaf, no butter.

From the great room came Mallick's voice, easy and apologetic.

"Well, now, I keep mixing up what was where in this place. Those little basement windows face the lake, right? I just want to look one more time. Somebody told me that downstairs bathroom lock sticks and then somebody else said no, that's the upstairs one, and I'd hate to go writing down the wrong bathroom. My mother would rise from the dead just to tell me to label things better."

Paul said, "Basement one sticks sometimes."

Doug stopped chewing.

The duffel was still beside his pull-out, the side pocket half unzipped from the morning. He saw it all at once without seeing it: orange cap, white label, his name in block print, Mallick's eyes settling there for half a second and never leaving it alone again.

"I can show you," he said.

The words came out too fast. Paul and Mallick both looked over.

"You don't have to," Paul said.

"It's fine." Doug set down the crust and was already moving. "I'm heading down there anyway."

He took the basement stairs two at a time. The lower level smelled like detergent and old lake towels and the stale sweetness of the basement bathroom cleaner Martin bought in bulk. Nate was on his own pull-out in socks, holding his phone up near the little lake-facing window as if two extra inches might convince the service bars to return.

"Any luck?" Nate said without looking over.

"No."

Doug crossed to his sofa and yanked the duffel toward him. The side pocket caught on the blanket. He pulled harder. The zipper gave, and one orange bottle nosed up through the gap.

He slapped his hand over it before it could hit the floor.

Nate looked over then. "You good?"

"Yeah."

"What're you doing?"

"Moving my stuff."

Nate frowned at the bag. "Now?"

"Apparently."

Mallick was on the stairs before Nate could ask anything else.

"Don't mind me," she called. "I just wanted another peek down here, and then I promise I'll stop traipsing through everybody's sleeping arrangements."

Doug had time for one choice. He could shove the duffel into the laundry closet and look panicked, or pick it up and look like he'd meant to move it all along. Looking planned was closer to innocent. He zipped the side pocket as far as it would go, slung the bag by one strap, and stood.

Mallick came down with the notebook tucked under one arm and her reading glasses low on her nose. Her gaze touched the duffel, left it, came back to it for the length of a blink, then moved on to the room.

"Oh, am I sending you packing?" she said.

"No. Just getting it out of the way."

"Mm-hmm."

She went first to the bathroom door and worked the lock once, twice, a little rattle of metal. Then she stepped to the window by Nate's sofa and looked out toward the darkening lake.

"You boys really can hear the water from down here," she said. "Funny what carries and what doesn't."

Nate still had his phone lifted toward the window. "Cell service doesn't carry worth a damn, if that helps."

"That does help." She wrote something down. "Now, Friday night, if somebody was walking around upstairs, from this sofa maybe you hear it and maybe you don't. Hard to know. Pipes do their thing, floorboards talk, everybody's had a few. Houses tell on you and cover for you at the same time."

Doug stood with the duffel hanging from his hand. The strap cut into his fingers. Setting it down would have made it a normal thing. Not setting it down made it worse. He kept standing there anyway.

Mallick turned and gave the room another slow look, taking in the pull-outs, the folded blankets, the laundry closet, Doug with his overnight bag clenched like he expected somebody to frisk it.

"Folks keep most of their luggage down here?" she asked mildly.

"Some of it," Doug said.

"Any reason somebody besides you and Nate would've been in and out during the night? Towels, medicine, toiletries, whatever people forget they need till two in the morning?"

The word *medicine* hit him hard enough he thought Nate would hear it.

"Maybe towels," Doug said. "Not much else."

Her pen paused. "Mm-hmm."

She asked Nate two more questions about what he had heard and when. Nate answered in fits and starts, phone still in his hand, giving her three possible times and a theory about bad acoustics that he did not really believe himself. Doug barely heard him. Every shift of the duffel brought a soft plastic knock from inside the side pocket. It was a tiny sound. To him it was a church bell.

Mallick finally tucked the notebook shut against her palm.

"Well. Thank you, gentlemen. I appreciate it. And I'm sorry again for the marching around. Pretty soon you'll all be rid of me."

Nobody said that seemed likely.

She went back upstairs. Her steps faded. The house settled around them again.

Nate lowered his phone.

"You leaving?" he asked.

"No."

"Okay."

That was one of the reasons Doug could stand Nate in doses. Nate could talk for an hour when he was scared, but if he heard the floor drop out under a person he usually stopped.

Doug set the duffel on his sofa. For a second he thought about moving the bottles again, somewhere smaller, somewhere dumber, the laundry hamper or behind the extra paper towels or under the sink in the bathroom. Every option felt more suspicious than the last. He unzipped the side pocket just far enough to make sure both bottles were still there and zipped it shut again.

Nate looked away on purpose and gave him that much privacy.

"You want a smoke later?" Nate said.

"Maybe."

"All right."

Doug kicked off his shoes and lay back on the pull-out fully dressed, the duffel against his hip because now that Mallick had seen him moving it, putting it anywhere else felt like another decision he would have to make for a reason. Above his head the refrigerator kicked on, rattled through its cycle, clicked off. Sleep didn't come, and Nate didn't ask again.


# Chapter 8

The house was worth four hundred thousand, maybe four-fifty if a buyer came in from the Cities, saw the water first, and didn't clock the soft board by the grill or the way the refrigerator sounded like it was breathing through a wet towel. Cliff had been pricing it in his head since Thursday because he priced everything. He noticed the stain worn off the deck rails, the screen door that no longer sat square in its frame, the cloudy lower corners of the lake-facing windows, and the old dock bolts starting to bloom orange. None of that killed the number. It just shaved it down around the edges. Martin Kessler's lake house on Elbow Lake was still worth enough to change somebody's month, maybe somebody's year.

It was not worth what Cliff needed.

What Cliff needed was forty thousand dollars.

The number had been living in his chest since March of last year, a low infection that flared every time he opened his laptop or checked his bank balance or drove past the duplex on Dale Street that had eaten his capital and his partnership with Martin and most of his ability to sleep through the night. Forty thousand dollars. Not forty thousand in the abstract, the way people said "forty thousand" when they meant "a lot." Forty thousand specific dollars that Martin Kessler had owed him for twenty-three months from a joint deal on a Roseville duplex that had gone sideways the way only real estate went sideways: slowly, expensively, with both partners telling different stories about whose idea the purchase had been.

Martin had brought the lead. Cliff had put up the capital. The place needed a new foundation, a new roof, and asbestos remediation the inspection had somehow missed. The inspector had been a guy Martin knew, a guy Martin had used before, a guy who had apparently inspected the duplex the way Martin inspected anything that wasn't immediately on fire, which was to say he looked at it, decided it was fine, and walked away. The duplex was gone. Sold at a loss that split unevenly because the capital had been Cliff's. Martin's half of that loss was forty thousand dollars, and for twenty-three months Martin had been making repayment noises. The right noises. "I know, I know, I'm working on it." The same noises Cliff heard from buyers who needed six more weeks and contractors who were definitely mailing the lien release today.

Tomorrow one of Cliff's project notes would go from ugly to officially in default. By October two more would come due. On Friday his lender had left a voicemail that began with "Mr. Haywood" instead of "Cliff," which was how men in loafers let you know the friendly part was over. Martin had owed him real money at exactly the moment real money had stopped being something Cliff could bluff his way around.

Now Martin was dead, and dead men didn't write checks, but dead men had estates, and estates had paperwork, and somewhere in this house there had to be paper that could make Cliff's forty thousand into an obligation instead of a memory.

It was Sunday morning. The kitchen smelled like bad coffee and the lake. Cliff stood at the counter with a mug he wasn't drinking from and watched the others move through their versions of routine in a house where the host was dead. Paul sat at the table in the wire-rimmed glasses that made him look like a disappointed accountant, thumbing through his phone with tiny impatient scrolls, stopping every few seconds to squint and reread whatever he'd just found, as if the facts might settle down if he stared at them long enough.

Joel was on the deck in one of the Adirondack chairs, facing the water, but every few seconds his eyes ticked from the dock to the tree line to the gravel drive, like he was checking the edges of the property without wanting anyone to catch him at it. Coffee sat in his hand, untouched. The steam had quit ten minutes ago and Joel either hadn't noticed or didn't care.

Doug was somewhere in the basement. He'd been disappearing down there every chance he got, peeling off from rooms the second conversation turned loud or useless. Cliff hadn't followed. Whatever Doug had going on, it showed up more in the vanishing than in anything he said.

Near the front door, Nate paced the three steps between the coat hooks and the doormat that got the best cell signal, talking too fast and filling every gap before the voice on the other end could answer. "No, I'm fine, I'm fine," Nate said, free hand conducting an invisible orchestra. "It's just, no, listen, it's, they haven't said we can leave yet. The detective, the, yeah, the one with the casserole. I know. I know it sounds, yeah."

Tommy sat on the deck too, in the other Adirondack chair, not talking to Joel. Six feet between them, the same lake in front of them, and a silence that had hardened into furniture. Tommy's hands rested on the armrests, knuckles white from gripping. The muscle in his jaw kept jumping. He never once looked at Joel.

Mallick had left an hour ago. She'd told Paul, Paul being the one she treated as the group's designated adult, that she'd be back in the afternoon and nobody should leave the property. She'd said it with a smile meant to make it sound like a suggestion. Cliff didn't buy the smile. Very little about Mallick struck him as accidental. Cliff sold for a living and recognized performance when he saw it. The casserole, the wrong name, the bathroom before the body. All of it had been arranged to look unarranged. Whether she was good enough to matter was a separate question.

What mattered was that she was gone. What mattered was the study.

Cliff had already been in Martin's study once, Saturday night, in those scrambled hours between Martin's death and dawn when every man in the house had apparently decided to creep through the dark and do his own bad idea. Cliff's bad idea had been simple. He'd gone looking for the promissory note Martin should have signed. Or should have signed and never did. That was the problem. Martin had acknowledged the debt in texts, emails, conversations Paul and Tommy had both overheard, but acknowledgment was not documentation, and if Martin's estate paid debts it would pay the ones backed by paper first. Cliff's forty thousand existed mostly in Martin's word, and Martin's word had gone to zero at about one-thirty Saturday morning.

He hadn't found the note. What he'd found was Martin's desk, Martin's filing cabinet, locked, and Martin's wall safe behind a framed lake landscape print. The safe was a SentrySafe, wall-mounted, combination dial. The dial had been sitting one tick short of the final number, not open but not really shut either, the way you'd leave a door on the latch when you meant to come right back.

That was either Martin's trust or Martin's laziness. In twenty-five years Cliff had never been able to separate the two. Martin left the safe one tick off. Martin left the front door unlocked. Martin left forty thousand dollars unpaid. Same gesture every time. A belief that things would hold together without being secured, and a long run of being right until he wasn't.

Saturday night, Cliff had opened the safe, seen folders and envelopes and cash, then heard the third stair from the top creak and bailed. He'd gone back to the pull-out sofa in the basement and stared at the dark while his heart pounded against the mattress springs. Cliff Haywood, who had sat across from loan officers with underwater properties and zoning boards with rejected permits and told them things that were not entirely true with a resting heart rate of sixty-two, had folded because a stair creaked.

Now it was Sunday morning. Mallick was gone. The staircase was empty. Cliff's pulse was seventy-four. He'd checked it in the bathroom, two fingers to his wrist, because he liked data even when the data was his own body failing to be calm. Seventy-four was fine. Seventy-four was a man about to go for a walk, not a man about to open a dead friend's safe for the second time in thirty-six hours.

The refrigerator cycled on with its loud asthmatic hum, the same rhythm Doug had probably listened to all night from the basement. Martin had never replaced it. Martin had never replaced much in this house except the deck, and only because the old one had literally rotted through. That had been one of the Roseville problems too. Martin believed maintenance was optional. That philosophy worked better on a lake place than on a rental property that punished neglect with vacancy and code violations.

"Gonna make a call," Cliff said to the room.

Paul nodded without looking up. Nate gave a thumbs-up nobody had requested.

Down the hallway, the half bath wore its nautical hand towels, probably Linda's doing, and the walls held the annual draft photos, twenty-five of them, seven men aging in the same frame. Cliff glanced at the 2003 one without stopping. They were young enough there to believe a bad night either became a story or disappeared. Tommy had his arm around Martin. Martin leaned into him. Whatever sat on their faces in that picture, it was not guilt.

Martin's study was the last door on the left. Cliff stepped in, closed the door, and listened. He heard coffee sounds from the kitchen and Nate's voice by the front door, lower now. The house offered nothing that sounded like footsteps.

The room smelled like printer ink, paper dust, and old cardboard, the dry attic smell of records kept long past useful life. Martin's desk sat under the window. The file cabinet leaned a fraction of an inch left, probably because one wheel had gone bad years ago and Martin never fixed it. The bookshelves held financial planning guides, Tom Clancy hardcovers, and old college textbooks Martin had never bothered to dump. Through the window Cliff could see the gravel drive. Mallick's county sedan was gone. Their cars filled the rest of the view: Paul's Accord, Joel's Volvo, Tommy's F-150, Nate's Subaru with the podcast stickers, Doug's Camry, Cliff's Audi. The Audi was leased. The payment was fourteen hundred a month. He was three weeks late, which put him in the grace period, a term that meant the same thing in car loans as it did in religion. You were forgiven right up until you weren't.

He took the lake print off the wall. The safe sat where it had sat for years. One tick on the dial. The handle turned. The door opened.

Inside were three manila folders, a small law-office envelope, a letter-size manila clasp envelope, and a stack of bills clipped together. Cliff took the cash first and counted without removing the clip. The stack was twenties and fifties, just under two thousand dollars. He put it back. Two thousand wasn't the answer. Two thousand was a week of pretending the answer was on its way.

The first folder was labeled HOUSE. It held the deed, tax records, and a homeowner's policy with a carrier Cliff recognized as aggressively average. The house was paid off, no mortgage. That was fine. It was an unencumbered asset, though not his.

The second folder was INVESTMENTS. Edward Jones statements, a Vanguard index fund, some municipal bonds. The most recent statement put Martin around two-forty. Martin wasn't rich. He was comfortable. It was the kind of money that looked substantial until you subtracted a divorce, two decades of taxes, and forty thousand dollars in unrepaid debt.

The third folder was PERSONAL, and Cliff's hands slowed. PERSONAL in a dead man's safe could mean anything. It could be medical records or love letters, paper too charged to leave in a desk drawer where you'd see it every day. What Cliff needed was a signed note.

He found Martin's birth certificate, an expired passport, military discharge papers from the two years Martin spent in the Army after high school and before college, and the divorce decree. At the bottom sat one loose sheet in Martin's square handwriting.

*I acknowledge that I owe Cliff Haywood $40,000 (forty thousand dollars) for his share of the capital loss on the Roseville property at 1847 Dale St. I have not yet repaid this and I intend to repay it in full by end of calendar year.*

There was no date, no signature, no witness line, and no notary, nothing any lawyer would call binding. All Cliff had was Martin's intention and Martin's handwriting. That was it.

Cliff read it twice. Thin evidence, but evidence. The kind of page his lawyer would call helpful in a tone that meant not enough, then bill four hundred dollars an hour for the distinction.

He put the sheet back and took up the small envelope next. This time he opened it. The return address belonged to a family-law firm in Edina, and inside were a certified copy of the dissolution judgment and a quitclaim deed transferring Linda's share of the lake house to Martin. It was old divorce paper, clean and final and useless to him. He slid it all back in, flattened the flap, and set it aside.

That left the letter-size manila envelope.

It was heavier than the others and closed with a brass clasp threaded through the flap. Martin had written two letters across the front in black Sharpie.

**RT**

Cliff's pulse kicked again. RT meant one thing at first, automatic and mechanical. It could be RTI Holdings, the LLC Martin had used for the Roseville deal, some real-estate tag, some code tied to the duplex, the money, the missing paper trail that could turn forty thousand dollars into an enforceable debt instead of a dead man's vague promise.

He lifted the clasp, slid the flap free without tearing it, and drew out four yellow legal-pad sheets folded into thirds.

It was not a contract or a partnership document or anything Cliff had pictured in the three seconds between seeing the letters and touching the paper.

The first page began in Martin's handwriting:

*August 17, 2002*

*I'm writing this down because somebody should, and because I don't trust myself to remember it right if I don't write it now while it's still clear. This is what happened on Saturday night, August 10, 2002, at the cabin on Wolf Creek Road, Washburn County, Wisconsin.*

Against the edge of Martin's desk, Cliff shifted his weight and kept reading. Nate's voice drifted faint through the house. A mug touched the counter in the kitchen. Through the open study window the lake lapped at shore.

Martin and Tommy had driven up on Friday afternoon to a cabin outside Minong that belonged to a friend of Tommy's uncle, a man named Gunderson. They were supposed to close the place for the season, drain the pipes, shutter the windows, and leave by Sunday morning. The cabin had a caretaker named Ray Toomey who lived in a single-wide on the next lot and handled the place in the off-season. Martin wrote that Toomey had been around Friday, sober enough to point out the shutoff valves and the breaker box, then less sober after dark. By Saturday night Tommy was worse than that. He'd been drinking since noon and kept drinking because there was nobody there to tell him to stop.

Cliff's eye caught where it always caught first: the pieces that survived argument. August 10. Wolf Creek Road. Washburn County. Ray Toomey. Martin wasn't spilling guilt in a bar voice. He was setting down names and places the way men did when they wanted something to outlive denial.

Around nine Tommy went inside ahead of Martin to grab the flashlight and the last case of beer. Martin heard him moving through the kitchen, swearing at a stuck drawer, banging cupboard doors hard enough to rattle the windows. Then the sounds changed. He heard a metal knock, then a heavier crash. After that came a silence of a different kind, and then light in the kitchen window and the smell of smoke before Martin even saw the fire.

Tommy had knocked over a kerosene heater. The spill caught the pilot flame. Kerosene ran across the kitchen floor and the old pine took it greedily. Martin ran in and found Tommy standing in the front room, not moving, eyes fixed on the kitchen, mouth slightly open, like the message had reached his face but not the rest of him yet. Martin grabbed his arm. Tommy came with him. The kitchen ceiling came down behind them with a sound Martin compared to a piano dropped from a second floor. Four minutes and the cabin was fully involved.

Ray Toomey had been asleep in the back bedroom.

Cliff stopped there. He read the line again because asleep changed the size of it. A dead caretaker was one shape of disaster. A man asleep in the back room while two drunk friends made it out to the yard was another. The top sheet had gone slick against his thumb.

They hadn't known he was there. Martin wrote that they'd assumed Toomey had gone back to the trailer or into town or anywhere else a drunk caretaker might go on a Saturday night.

In the right-hand margin, in darker ink crowded tight against the legal-pad line, Martin had added what came after: the volunteer department found what was left of Toomey the next morning. The coroner put his blood alcohol at .14. The smoke detector battery was dead. He had been drunk, asleep, and trapped.

Blood alcohol. Dead detector battery. Trapped. The margin note hit Cliff in the language he knew best: stacked facts, each one clean enough to survive cross-examination. Martin had built the page the way a man built a file against himself, and against Tommy.

On the fourth sheet the handwriting tightened. Tommy had wanted to call 911 from the road. Martin had talked him out of it, not because he was cold but because he had done the math and then hated himself for how quickly he'd done it. Tommy was drunk, he had entered the cabin first, he had knocked over the heater, and a man was dead. Tommy's future, as Martin calculated it on a dark county road outside Minong, looked like manslaughter at minimum and maybe worse if somebody decided the drinking and the trespass and the dead man added up to more than negligence.

At the bottom of that sheet, in the same darker ink, Martin had added the rest: he'd driven back at three in the morning, alone, with work gloves from the trunk. He repositioned the heater to make it look like it failed where it stood. He adjusted what he could of the spill pattern. Later, the fire investigation ruled it accidental: faulty heater, intoxicated caretaker, dead smoke detector. Nobody ever knew Martin and Tommy had been there.

By work gloves from the trunk, the room had changed temperature. Up to that point Cliff could still tell himself he was reading about a bad night, a drunk mistake, a house that went up too fast. The drive back stripped that away. Martin had gone back to make the fire say accident.

The last paragraph sat at the bottom of the fourth sheet like Martin had forced it there:

*Tommy is not a bad person. He was drunk and it was dark and he didn't know Toomey was there. But a man died because of what Tommy did and what I did after, and I need to write it down. I don't know what I want done with this. I don't know if I want anything done with it. I need it to exist somewhere outside my head. If it's only in my head then it's just a thing I think about, and it has to be more than that. It has to be a thing that happened.*

*-- Martin Kessler*

Cliff went back to page one and read it again, slower this time. The second pass was worse. The main account was the night itself. The cramped darker notes were what Martin learned after. None of it read like a melodramatic drunk confession he scribbled and forgot by morning. He had built a record and hidden it for twenty-two years.

When Cliff lowered the pages, the room had not changed, but it no longer felt neutral. The file cabinet was still crooked. The Tom Clancy hardcovers were still crammed double-deep on the middle shelf. Water still ran in the kitchen sink. A loon called from somewhere out on Elbow Lake. Every ordinary thing in the room looked like it had agreed to sit beside this and say nothing.

For half a minute he simply stood there with Ray Toomey's name in his head and Martin's handwriting in front of him.

Then Cliff's brain did what it always did. It ran the numbers.

Tommy had one of those public suburban lives built on Friday nights, booster-club goodwill, and people saying Coach like it was his first name. Cliff had sold houses in those suburbs. He knew what men like Tommy were worth to their towns. He knew what happened when the wrong scandal hit a school district and a booster culture at the same time. Men who survived affairs and DUIs and screaming at refs did not survive hidden dead bodies from twenty-two years ago.

The math came clear in one ugly piece. Tommy did not have to hand Cliff forty thousand dollars. Tommy just had to stop acting like Martin's debt was an awkward ghost story and help make it real, push Paul, push the estate question, and back Cliff in the room instead of letting him sound like the asshole asking for reimbursement at a funeral.

That was when the plan changed.

Before the envelope, Cliff had been working toward a conversation he could manage with two beers and the right tone. After the envelope, a bad conversation could blow the whole thing up. If Tommy panicked, he might deny everything, bolt, or come at Cliff sideways in a way you couldn't walk back. There was too much under the paper and too much attached to Tommy's name. Cliff needed copies, distance, and a sentence that held up under daylight.

He took out his phone and photographed each sheet, front and back. That gave him eight images. The shutter sound had been off for years. He checked every photo for focus and legibility before moving to the next. Martin's handwriting was clear, Tommy's name was clear, and so were Ray Toomey, Washburn County, August 10, 2002, and the signature.

Then he opened the notes app and typed three lines he immediately deleted. `Need to talk to you about something sensitive.` Too soft. `Need help collecting a debt.` Too naked. `Need advice on leverage tied to an old death.` Absolutely not. He deleted that one so fast his thumb slipped.

He settled for forwarding the photos to his own Gmail, then to the backup account he used when deals got ugly and he didn't want one more thing living in a single phone. After that he texted Aaron Miller, the Minneapolis lawyer who handled Cliff's contracts when banks got twitchy and partners got sentimental.

Need discreet call today if possible. Found handwritten statement in deceased debtor's papers. Also a second signed document that creates exposure with a third party. Need to know what I can use and what I can't.

He stared at the message, changed `exposure` to `risk,` changed it back to `exposure,` and sent it before he could keep sanding the edges off. Sunday morning or not, Aaron billed like a man who liked being needed. He'd answer eventually.

Cliff put the sheets back into the envelope, slid the flap under the clasp, and turned the brass tab down until it sat the way it had before. He returned the envelope to the safe behind the folders and beside the cash. The small divorce envelope went back in front. He shut the safe, left the dial one tick off, and hung the lake print over it again.

The print was bad in a generic way, with too-blue water and pines that looked copied from every other lake picture in Minnesota. It covered the safe and that was what mattered. Martin had always been willing to live with a thing being slightly wrong if it kept functioning.

In the half bath Cliff washed his hands because they were shaking and hot water helped. His fingerprints were already all over the house, same as everybody else's. This wasn't about fingerprints. It was about giving his face a minute to catch up.

The watch on his wrist was a Tag Heuer from 2017, back when the business had been good enough to mistake momentum for permanence. The shirt on his back had gone on a maxed-out card in August because he still had to look like a man people trusted with keys and earnest money. In the mirror he still read as listings and steakhouse tabs, not overdue notes and a lender who had switched to Mr. Haywood.

Back in the kitchen, Cliff poured fresh coffee. Paul was still at the table. Joel had come inside and was leaning against the counter with his arms crossed, looking at nothing.

"Mallick say what time she's coming back?" Cliff asked.

"Afternoon," Paul said. "Didn't specify."

"Okay. Think I'm gonna take a walk. Clear my head."

"Sure."

Joel gave him a nod that barely rose to greeting. Cliff nodded back and went out the front door with the mug.

The porch step gave him a spot nobody inside could see. Morning sun lit the gravel drive. Across the road, a few birch leaves had started to turn. The air smelled like pine sap, lake water, and the sweet edge of leaves beginning to rot. It was perfect listing weather. Cliff had sold mornings like this in brochures and online galleries and copy about peaceful retreat living for places forty minutes from the nearest grocery store and one septic failure away from a disaster disclosure.

The phone in his pocket held eight photographs and a text now flying toward Minneapolis. That mattered. So did the fact that Tommy was twenty feet away on the deck, breathing hard enough that Cliff could hear it when the house noises settled.

Cliff moved off the porch and took the side path toward the garbage cans, more for a cleaner sight line than for privacy. From there he could see the deck through the gap between the railing and the hydrangeas. Tommy still had both hands on the chair arms. He looked not just upset but braced, like a man waiting for impact that had been delayed too long. Nate came out with his phone still in his hand and said something Cliff couldn't catch. Tommy flinched hard enough to slosh coffee over his knuckles.

"Jesus Christ," Tommy snapped.

"I just asked if you wanted more coffee," Nate said.

"Then ask from over there."

Nate threw up a hand and backed off. Tommy didn't apologize. He stood too fast, paced once to the far end of the deck, then came down the steps and cut around the side of the house. He stopped fifteen feet from Cliff without seeing him, bent at the waist, and spat into the grass. It wasn't a hangover spit either. It was a dry, sour heave that brought up almost nothing. Tommy stayed folded there, palms on his knees, staring at the ground until the spell passed.

Cliff held still.

When Tommy finally straightened, he looked toward the lake with the blank, ugly concentration of somebody trying not to come apart in public. Then he wiped his mouth with the back of his hand and walked back to the deck like nothing had happened.

That settled it better than any abstract thought Cliff had been dressing up as strategy. There would be no deck conversation tonight and no smooth opener over beers. If Cliff walked up to Tommy now with Martin's pages in his pocket and forty thousand dollars in his mouth, Tommy would hear only the first part. Maybe he caved. Maybe he lied. Maybe he did something dumber than either of those. Cliff had seen enough men at closing tables to know when the room had gone too hot for a clean ask.

His phone buzzed once. It wasn't Aaron, just the Gmail confirmation banner sliding down and disappearing. That was good enough for now. Enough to give him a few hours to stand in the kitchen, talk about nothing, and wait for the call.

He needed copies first, then the lawyer, then Tommy.


# Chapter 9

At Sunday lunch, Joel Beckman sat at the kitchen table with a turkey sandwich he wasn't eating and the minor satisfaction of having gone fourteen hours without anyone asking about the argument. A personal best for this weekend, though the bar was roughly ankle-high. The previous record had been six hours, overnight, because even these people had to sleep eventually.

Nate was trying to scrape the last of the mustard from the jar with a butter knife. Cliff was at the counter cutting an apple into mathematically precise slices. Tommy was on his second sandwich, maybe his third. Joel had stopped tracking. Paul sat across from him with a cup of coffee he kept warming in the microwave, the same cup he'd been nursing since morning, which Joel found either disciplined or insane.

Doug was eating a bowl of cereal, because Doug had apparently decided cereal was a valid Sunday lunch option and nobody had the energy to argue.

The chair at the head of the table was empty. Nobody had mentioned it. Everyone had noticed.

"Mustard's done," Nate announced, holding up the jar. "We're officially post-mustard. I want that noted for the record."

"There's more in the garage fridge," Paul said.

"The garage fridge. Of course. Because Martin had a condiment annex." Nate set the jar down and took a bite of his dry sandwich. After a while he said, "His garage fridge had better beer than my actual fridge. That's not a bit. That's just true."

The kitchen was quiet except for the refrigerator's cycling hum and Tommy's methodical chewing. Afternoon light came through the windows at a low angle and caught dust above the table. The room smelled like stale coffee, mustard, and the wet brown edge of Cliff's apple, a Sunday-house smell that said the weekend had gone on too long.

"So what happens now?" Tommy asked the table. It came out in the tone coaches used when they already had an answer and wanted the room to arrive there on its own.

"Mallick said she'd be back this afternoon," Paul said. "She's at the station going through her notes."

"I mean with us. The house. All of it."

"Martin's estate will handle the house," Cliff said, and Joel heard the smoothness in it, the way Cliff's voice could flatten any question into terms of property and transfer. Cliff knew things about Martin's finances that the rest of them didn't. Or thought he did, anyway. For two days now Joel had watched him move through the house like he was appraising it.

No one had said suspect out loud since Saturday, not after Paul snapped that it was a hell of a word to use in Martin's kitchen. But the house had built a quiet little safety plan around Joel all the same. If he stepped onto the deck, somebody else drifted out there. If he took too long in the downstairs bathroom, Tommy hollered through the hall asking if he was alive. Even Doug kept surfacing in whatever room Joel was in, as if by accident. It was amateur surveillance, obvious enough to insult him. Joel might have been insulted if he hadn't also understood it. Anger made people nervous. Anger plus a dead body made them start taking attendance.

"I just want to go home," Doug said quietly. Then he caught himself. "Not that. I mean. We all do. Obviously."

"Mallick hasn't cleared us yet," Joel said.

Tommy looked at him. "She hasn't cleared anybody, Beck."

"She's cleared some of you more than others."

"What's that supposed to mean?"

Instead of answering, Joel took a bite of the sandwich. The turkey was dry. Martin always bought the good deli turkey, the kind you had to ask for at the counter, and somehow it was already going stale. Two days. That was how long it took for the provisions to start outlasting the host.

Nate was watching him with the expression Joel had learned to recognize as the lead-in to a bit: eyebrows up, mouth slightly open, the podcaster warming up. This time Nate didn't launch. He just kept chewing and looked at Joel with something that wasn't quite sympathy and wasn't quite curiosity.

"Can I ask you something?" Nate said.

"You can ask."

"Are you, and this is just me genuinely asking, not for anything, just between us, gonna tell us what you and Martin were fighting about?"

The table went quiet. Not silent. Tommy was still chewing. The fridge was still humming. But the conversational temperature dropped in a way Joel could feel against his skin.

"I told Mallick it wasn't relevant."

"You told Mallick it wasn't relevant to her investigation," Paul said carefully. "That's not the same as it not being relevant."

"It's not relevant."

"Bullshit," Tommy said. Matter-of-fact, not angry. Coaching mode. "You show up Thursday night already pissed off, you're in his face at midnight screaming loud enough that I could hear it from the hot tub, and Martin's dead twelve hours later. That's relevant."

"Martin died of a cardiac event."

"We don't actually know that yet," Paul said.

"We effectively know that."

"Joel." Paul set his coffee down. The microwave-warmed cup made a soft thud on the table. "We've been here for three days. Mallick keeps coming back. Nobody's been cleared. And you're sitting here telling us the argument everybody keeps circling isn't relevant."

"The investigation isn't centered on the argument."

"It's centered on you," Cliff said without looking up from his apple. "Which is the same thing."

Joel pushed his plate away. The sandwich sat there with one bite missing, reproaching him with its dryness.

The truth was that Joel had been waiting for this conversation. He hadn't wanted it, but he'd been waiting for it, the way you waited for a dental appointment you'd already rescheduled twice. The argument was the one piece of information that still belonged to him and not to the investigation, and holding it had become physically exhausting in a way he hadn't expected. His jaw ached. His shoulders had been knotted since Saturday morning. For three nights he'd slept maybe three hours at a time in a guest room that smelled like Martin's fabric softener, lying in the dark and rehearsing both the speech he'd planned to give Martin and the speech he now had to give these men. Lately the two speeches kept bleeding into each other until he couldn't tell which one belonged to which audience.

What he hadn't rehearsed was saying Karen's name in this kitchen. Once he said it here, in Martin's house, it stopped belonging to him and Karen and Martin and became group property, something these men could pass around, argue over, and fit into whatever rules they used to make sense of themselves. That was probably childish. It was still the bargain he'd been trying to make all weekend: let them suspect the anger, keep the humiliation private.

"It's personal," he said.

"We know it's personal," Nate said. "That's why we're asking."

"You don't know what kind of personal."

"Then tell us what kind."

Doug was staring at his cereal. He hadn't said anything since the I want to go home admission and the quick retraction after it. Joel could still see Doug's eyes moving from one man to the next, the quiet surveillance Doug had perfected over twenty-five years of being the one nobody watched.

"Look," Tommy said, leaning forward, elbows on the table. "We're all stuck here. Dead friend, detective, same questions. And you're the piece that doesn't add up."

"I'm not the—"

"Yeah you are. You know you are." Tommy tapped the table once. "So say it. Whatever you got, just say it, because the rest of us sitting here pretending we're not wondering? That's bullshit."

Joel almost laughed. Pure Tommy — the halftime challenge, the coaching certainty. Tommy Renaldi, who had flushed evidence down a toilet while a detective stood on the other side of the door, lecturing Joel about transparency.

"Okay," Joel said. The word came out smaller than he intended. "You want to know what the argument was about."

"Yes," said four voices at roughly the same time. Doug's mouth moved but no sound came out.

Joel looked at the empty chair at the head of the table. Martin's chair. The chair where Martin Kessler had sat for twenty-five drafts, running the league, managing the group, making sure everyone had a drink, making sure Tommy came back every year, making sure the whole machine kept turning. The chair where the most generous, most controlling, most thoughtfully selfish man Joel had ever known had presided over a weekend that was equal parts reunion and performance.

"Martin was fucking my ex-wife," Joel said.

---

The sentence hit the table like a dropped plate.

Nate's sandwich stopped halfway to his mouth. Tommy's jaw paused mid-chew. Cliff set down the knife. Paul closed his eyes and sat very still through one full breath.

Doug looked straight at Joel. It was only a look, but Joel recognized it because he'd been seeing it in the mirror for a month now: the face of a person trying to understand a betrayal big enough to rearrange the furniture in his head.

"Karen?" Paul said.

"That's the one."

"Jesus Christ," Tommy said.

"For eight months. Since January." Joel heard how steady his own voice was and found it strange. In all the versions of this conversation he'd rehearsed, he'd been shouting by now. Instead the facts came out flat and precise, the way he delivered an opening statement, leading with the evidence and leaving the emotion for later. "She told me a month before the draft. We were in the parking lot of Ellie's dorm, dropping off her stuff for fall semester. Karen waited till Ellie went inside and then told me in the car. Slichter Hall parking lot. She said she wanted me to hear it from her."

He could still see the fluorescent August light flattening the inside of the car, Ellie's bedding receipt crumpled in the cup holder, the campus map folded wrong between the seats because Karen had been using it ten minutes earlier and then just dropped it. Karen kept both hands on the steering wheel though the engine was off. She told it straight, eyes forward, like if she looked at him she'd have to stop. Joel had watched a kid drag a box fan across the parking lot and spent one stupid second trying to convince himself he'd misheard the noun.

"Hold on," Nate said. "A month before the draft. So you came here knowing."

"I came here to talk to him."

"You came here to confront him," Cliff said.

"I came here to talk to him. I had a whole plan for being calm about it. I was going to wait till Saturday morning, when everybody was hungover and slow, take him down to the dock, and ask him why."

"Instead you screamed at him Thursday night," Tommy said.

"I didn't scream."

"Beck, I heard you from the hot tub."

"I raised my voice. There's a legal distinction."

"There really isn't."

Joel rubbed his face with both hands. Legal precision was habit. Sarcasm was habit. Neither one helped him explain what it felt like to sit in a dorm parking lot watching your daughter wave from a second-floor window while your ex-wife told you that your friend of twenty-five years had been inside her since New Year's.

"Martin and Karen," Paul said. "How."

"How what?"

"How did it start? How long did you know? How did Karen even..."

"I don't know how it started. Karen said it started at a New Year's party. I wasn't there because I had Ellie that weekend. They ran into each other and one thing led to another and apparently one thing kept leading to eight months of another." Joel could hear the cross-examination tone taking over, sharp and clipped. "She said it wasn't planned. She said she didn't mean for it to happen. She said all the things people say when they absolutely meant for it to happen."

"Did Martin know you knew?" Doug asked.

His voice was so quiet everybody turned to him. It was the first interruption Doug had made all day.

"Yeah." Joel swallowed. "I called him three weeks before the draft and told him I knew. He said he was sorry. He said it was over. He said he'd ended it in August."

"Had he?" Cliff asked.

"I have no idea. Karen says yes. Martin said yes. I can't cross-examine a dead man."

"Then why'd you still come?" Paul asked.

Joel gave a short laugh. "Because not coming would've been letting him keep it clean. He gets eight months with my ex-wife and then I politely skip draft weekend so nobody has to feel weird? Fuck that. I came because he was going to look at me while I knew."

"So Thursday night," Nate said. "That's what the argument was."

"Thursday night I got drunk and all the calm shit I'd planned to say turned into the actual shit I wanted to say. Which was louder and less articulate." Joel picked up the abandoned sandwich, looked at it, and put it back down. "I called him a piece of shit. Several times. In several configurations. He stood there and took it because that's what Martin did. He absorbed things. Hands in his pockets. Head tilted. Like he was listening to a weather report he found mildly interesting. I wanted to hit him so badly I could taste it."

"Did you?" Tommy asked.

"No. I went to bed. I lay there for three hours thinking of every goddamn thing I should've said and didn't, and then Paul banged on my door at two in the morning and Martin was dead."

The refrigerator cycled off. In the sudden absence of it, the house went properly quiet. Joel could hear the lake through the windows, that small steady lapping that didn't care what was happening inside.

Paul opened his eyes. Grief was still there. So was something new. Joel couldn't name it yet, but he could see the crack forming.

"I'm sorry," Paul said.

"Don't be sorry for me. Be sorry that he died before I could finish telling him what I thought of him." Joel's voice tightened. "That's the part that gets me. I had things to say to him. I drove up here with them loaded and ready. And then he just fucking died."

Tommy leaned back and blew air through his teeth. His gaze went to the ceiling, then to Joel, then to the empty chair.

"Shit, Beck," he said.

"Yeah."

---

By Joel's professional assessment, what followed was the most structurally bizarre conversation he'd ever participated in, and he'd spent sixteen years in Hennepin County criminal court.

They moved out to the deck because the kitchen had gotten too small for it, and because Nate said he needed air. Joel suspected Nate mostly needed room to think out loud without clipping the counter on his way around. Tommy came back from the garage fridge with beers, the good IPAs Martin hoarded. Paul brought his coffee. Doug took the Adirondack chair farthest from the center of things, his cereal still sitting inside and going soft in its bowl.

The September afternoon was golden and ridiculous. The lake was flat. The trees were just starting to turn at the edges. Two loons called somewhere out past the dock. It was exactly the kind of afternoon Martin built the whole weekend around, the kind of clean expensive weather that could almost make this feel like a bad weekend instead of damage.

Nate settled into a chair with his beer. "Okay. I have to ask a gross question."

"That's how we know you're alive," Joel said.

"No, I'm serious. Was this like... were they just fucking, or were they doing couple shit too? Because those are different categories of offense."

"Jesus Christ," Paul said, at the same time Tommy said, "Doesn't matter."

"What? They are. One is a drunken horrible thing. The other is your friend sticking his dick into your life for eight months."

"Please don't phrase it like that," Paul said.

"How would you like it phrased?"

"I'd like not to hear 'sticking his dick into your life.'"

"I don't know the details," Joel said. "I didn't ask whether he left a toothbrush there."

Joel had made the mistake of thinking the hardest part would be saying it out loud. It turned out the harder part was sitting here while they sorted it into categories, as if Nate might actually draft an adultery bylaw if you gave him a legal pad. He took a swallow of Martin's beer and tasted grapefruit, bitterness, and the stale little insult of drinking someone else's stash on someone else's deck.

Tommy pointed his bottle at Nate. "Here's the rule. You don't fuck your friend's ex-wife. Period. I don't care if they've been divorced six months or six years."

"They're divorced, Tommy," Cliff said.

"I know they're divorced. I said friend."

Cliff leaned against the railing with his apple. The careful slices were gone. He was down to the core now, scraping fruit from around the seeds with his front teeth. "She's his ex-wife, Tommy. She's a grown woman who made a—I'm saying Joel doesn't own her."

Nate held up both hands. "I'm not saying own. I'm saying there should still be a waiting period. Minimum."

"That's not better," Paul said.

"It's worse, actually," Joel said.

Tommy kept chopping the air with his bottle like a second gesture might turn outrage into policy. Cliff had worried the apple down to seeds and stem. Nate only looked straight at Joel when Karen came up; the rest of the time he aimed his arguments at the lake. None of them had language worth a damn for it. Still, the knot under Joel's sternum eased a notch. The thing was out here now, ugly and specific, and he wasn't carrying it by himself anymore.

"What about Karen?" Nate asked. "She gets a vote in this. She's not just the field everybody's arguing over. She chose it too."

"Karen's choices are Karen's business," Joel said. "I'm not mad at Karen."

Nate blinked. "You're not?"

"No. We're divorced. She doesn't owe me loyalty. She can sleep with whoever she wants. Martin was the one who sat at my table for twenty-five years and then put his cock in my ex-wife anyway."

Paul made a low sound in his throat and took a drink of coffee that had to be cold by now. Tommy stared out at the lake like he was trying to find somewhere to throw the whole conversation. Even Cliff lost his smoothness for half a second.

"Okay," Nate said. "Yeah. When you say it like that, the hearing gets very simple."

Joel laughed before he meant to. It surprised all of them, him most of all. "Hearing. Great. Good. Do I get compensatory picks or what?"

That got Nate first, a short bark that escaped before he could stop it. Tommy followed with a low laugh of his own. Even Paul's mouth twitched. Cliff smiled thinly. For half a second the whole thing had rules again, and Joel could see how much he liked that. Joel folded over Martin's beer and laughed into the neck of the bottle, brief and helpless. Foam slicked his thumb. He wiped it on his jeans and took a swallow while the deck kept going, because that was easier than acting like the joke hadn't gotten him.

Cliff's smile disappeared first. "And it still isn't murder."

Paul turned to him. "No. But it's why Joel was in his face Thursday night, and I'm done pretending otherwise."

The hearing joke had gotten laughs. Paul's sentence knocked the deck flat. Paul set the mug on the rail with a small hard click and stayed standing.

"He was my best friend," Paul said. "I've spent two days trying to be fair to him. I don't think I've been fair to you." That last part was to Joel. Then Paul looked back at the lake. "Next time Mallick asks about Thursday night, I'm telling her why."

Joel should've felt vindicated. Instead the back of his neck went hot. Being believed and being written down were about to become the same thing.

Nobody spoke for a second. The deck seemed to pull tighter around the sentence.

Cliff straightened from the railing. "You're going to hand her motive because suddenly everybody wants to feel morally clean?"

"This isn't about clean."

"It's about—" Nate waved a hand. "It's about not feeding her a different fake answer every time she comes back, Cliff." No joke on either side of it. The air changed again.

Cliff ignored him. "The minute you say affair, she stops hearing cardiac event and starts hearing reason."

"She already hears reason," Doug said.

All five of them looked at him.

Doug shifted in the Adirondack chair. "Sorry. I just mean she keeps asking the same thing because the answer keeps wobbling. This doesn't wobble."

Doug almost never spent that many words in a row on somebody else's business. Hearing him do it made Joel straighten in his chair before he caught himself.

Tommy pointed at him. "That's exactly right."

"It's not exactly right," Cliff said. "It's a dead man sleeping with a divorced woman. Ugly, yes. Not some capital crime."

Joel felt the laugh go out of him. "Could everybody stop grading the offense like this is instant replay? That's not the problem."

Cliff looked at him. "Then what is?"

"The problem is everybody in this house has been acting like I blew up at him over nothing. That's the problem." Joel heard the rawness in his own voice and let it stay there. "You want to say divorced woman like that cleans it up, fine. It doesn't clean up him sitting at my table for twenty-five years and doing it anyway."

Tommy nodded once. Hard. "Exactly."

Paul didn't raise his voice. "I'm not—nobody's calling him a monster. I'm saying Thursday night wasn't just some random blowup, and she already knows it, Cliff. She knew it Saturday."

Cliff let out a short breath through his nose. "And Karen? You want her in a police notebook because the rest of you need the story to feel balanced?"

There it was. Not Martin. Not fairness. Ellie hearing it sideways from somebody with a badge.

Joel looked out at the water so he wouldn't have to look at any of them while he answered. A boat moved somewhere beyond the trees, just enough engine noise to remind him the rest of the lake still had ordinary people on it. "I don't want Ellie hearing this from somebody else," he said. "I don't want Karen getting a call from a county detective on a Sunday because Martin couldn't keep his dick in his pants. I don't want any of that."

Paul turned now. "I'm not volunteering Ellie. I'm not telling her anything Joel doesn't want out there. But the next time she asks me about Thursday night—" He exhaled. "I'm telling her why. Not everything. Just why."

Nate rubbed a hand over his face. "Yeah. Same. I can't do the old-friends-had-words version anymore. It's bullshit."

Tommy took a swallow of beer. "Tell her. She wants motive, give her the real motive. Beck yelled at him because Martin was fucking his ex. That's cleaner than half the crap we've already fed her."

"That's exactly why you don't tell her," Cliff said. His smoothness was starting to show the seams. "We've already got a detective circling the house. You want to narrow the beam onto Joel even more?"

"Maybe she narrows it the right way, then," Doug said, almost to his lap.

"What?"

Doug didn't look up. "At the actual reason. Not some made-up one."

Nate stared at him. "Whit, you've said more in the last two minutes than the last two days."

Doug gave the smallest shrug. "I know."

Doug kept staring at the deck boards like the sentence might crawl back into the crack he pulled it from. Joel knew that feeling too well. Say the one true thing in the room and suddenly everybody acted like you were the one who'd broken it.

Paul kept his eyes on Cliff. "I'm not asking permission. I'm telling you what I'm doing."

That cleared the last of the joking out of the air. Cliff looked from Paul to Tommy to Nate, taking the count. For the first time all afternoon he wasn't steering the room; he was checking how many of them he'd already lost.

Then he turned to Joel. "That's what you want? Karen's name in a notebook, Ellie maybe hearing about it sideways, all because everybody out here suddenly decided honesty matters?"

Paul stayed squared to the lake. Tommy stood with the bottle hanging from two fingers, jaw working. Nate had folded in on himself, elbows on knees now, no bit left in him. Doug still wasn't looking anybody straight in the face, but he hadn't taken back the sentence. Cliff saw the same thing Joel did a second later: whatever story they'd all been handing Mallick had died right here on the deck boards.

"I want it to stop sounding like I came up here looking for blood for no reason," Joel said. "After that, I don't really give a shit how tidy it feels."

For the first time all afternoon, Cliff had nothing smooth ready. He grabbed his beer off the arm of the chair and went back inside without offering to get anyone else one.

Nobody called after him.

A cabinet door banged in the kitchen. Then another one banged. Cliff was done pretending not to be rattled.

Paul stayed at the rail. "If she asks, I'll give her Thursday night. The affair. That's all."

Joel let out a breath that felt like it'd been living under his ribs since the dorm parking lot. "If she asks me, I'm not using Karen's name unless I have to."

"Fair," Paul said.

"And don't lawyer it," Tommy said. "Just say it straight."

Joel looked at him. "You saying that to me is offensive on about six levels."

"It can be offensive and right."

That got a brief sound out of Nate, not quite a laugh. "He's got you there, guy."

Paul rubbed at the bridge of his nose. "We need one version. Not a coordinated one. Just the real one."

Doug lifted his head. "None of us have told her the real one yet." He looked at his hands. "Just saying."

Nobody had anything to say to that.

Joel kept the bottle in his hand and let the quiet sit there. The deck ticked in the sun. The lake knocked softly at the dock posts. Nobody asked him another question.

The loons called again, farther out this time. Whatever happened when Mallick came back, it wasn't going to be the same six men handing her the same story.


# Chapter 10

Her leather notebook lay open on Martin Kessler's kitchen table to a page that would've embarrassed her if any of her colleagues at the county office saw it. Across it she'd drawn a grid. The columns were labeled with initials: PD, CH, NG, DW, TR, JB. The rows were labeled with the physical evidence. Check marks, question marks, and, in two cases, small x's that meant confirmed absent crowded the page. The grid looked like a badly designed crossword puzzle, and it told her less than she wanted and more than she expected.

By her count of the refrigerator cycles, it was close to ten o'clock. Since seven-thirty she'd been sitting there and she was no closer.

At last she took off her reading glasses and set them on the table. The beaded chain caught on her coffee mug and she untangled it with the patience of a woman who'd been untangling the same chain from the same kinds of things for a decade.

Martin Kessler was dead of cardiac arrest. The medical examiner had been clear: hypertrophic cardiomyopathy, undiagnosed, aggravated by alcohol, sildenafil, and prolonged heat exposure from the hot tub. It was a natural death. People died like this. Healthy-looking men in their late forties dropped dead and their friends stood around looking stunned, and the medical explanation was usually sufficient.

But Martin Kessler's house had been rearranged while his body cooled.

With the glasses back on, she looked at her grid.

The body. Martin had been found in bed, and by the time the deputies arrived Saturday morning, he was on his back with his arms at his sides. Tidy. Composed. People who died in their sleep did not usually end up tidy and composed. They ended up in whatever position the last involuntary movement left them: twisted, half off a pillow, one arm thrown, a leg bent. Paul Decker, bless his insurance-adjusting heart, had corrected her on Saturday about which way Martin's head had been turned. "I think it was more to the left, actually, toward the window." The correction told her two things. Paul had seen the body in its original position. The body was no longer in that position when the deputies arrived. Someone had moved Martin and straightened him out.

The wiping had to wait until Sunday morning for the BCA team to drive up from St. Paul, which was its own little joke on county resources. They found partial cleaning on the bedroom doorframe, the edge of the mattress, and the headboard. Partial. Whoever wiped got the right side of the headboard and missed the left. He got the doorframe at shoulder height and skipped the handle entirely. This was not professional cleaning. This was somebody on hands and knees at three in the morning, wiping surfaces he'd touched, probably crying. Same room as the body work. Same panic. Probably the same man.

The safe. The wall safe in Martin's study was hidden behind a lake landscape print, which was exactly where a lake house wall safe would be hidden. Martin had left the combination dial one tick off from locked, so anyone who jiggled it would find it open.
