Cliff Haywood had corrected her on Saturday about which room was the study when she'd pretended to confuse it with the half bath. "The study's the second door, not the first." She hadn't confused anything. Cliff's correction placed him in a room he had no stated reason to enter, and it placed him there with enough familiarity to know the order of the hallway doors. Financial records were inside the safe. Cliff was owed forty thousand dollars by the dead man. It did not take the county's best detective to draw that line. There was no county's best detective. There was the county's only detective, which was a different thing.

The voicemail. Nate Griggs had spent forty-five minutes telling her about his podcast, his audience metrics, his recording setup, his microphone preferences, and the exact linguistic conventions of fantasy football trash talk, and in the middle of minute thirty-eight he'd referenced "the thing I left on Martin's phone, the voicemail, which, I mean, not that it matters, because it was just draft stuff." He'd said it with the desperation of a man who had rehearsed not mentioning a detail and then mentioned it anyway. Martin's phone, now in evidence, showed a deleted voicemail from Nate's number, received at 8:47 PM Friday and deleted at approximately 3:15 AM Saturday, after the death and before the call to police. Nate had deleted a message from a dead man's phone because the message contained the words "dead to me" in the context of fantasy football, and he was terrified a county detective in rural Minnesota would not understand the genre.

A sip of coffee confirmed it had gone lukewarm, and she drank it anyway.

The medications. The shared upstairs bathroom had a medicine cabinet that was too clean. Six men in their late forties sharing a bathroom for a weekend and the cabinet held a tube of toothpaste, a disposable razor, and a bottle of Advil. No prescription bottles, no allergy medication, no heartburn tablets, no sleep aids. Nothing. Saturday afternoon she'd opened the cabinet and clocked the emptiness the way she'd clock a rug that had been freshly vacuumed in a house where nobody admitted to vacuuming. Someone had cleared that cabinet out, and the only reason to do that was if it had held pills somebody didn't want found. Given the age and habits of the men in this house, the missing bottles most likely said depression, anxiety, blood pressure, or sleep. They said struggle in a language the group did not like speaking out loud.

In the notebook she wrote "meds: shame, not guilt" and underlined it.

And the Viagra. Sildenafil in Martin's blood, and no Viagra anywhere in the house. Not in his bedroom, not in his bathroom, not in his luggage. The ME had confirmed the level was consistent with a single dose taken four to six hours before death. Whoever got rid of the rest had not started in the half bath where she'd heard the flush Saturday morning. He'd started in Martin's room, because that was where the pills would have been before they became a problem. Tommy Renaldi had come out of that bathroom looking like he'd run a half mile in a sauna. On Saturday she'd asked if he was alright and he'd said, "Fine, yeah, fine," in the way people said fine when they were not fine and couldn't think fast enough to build a better lie.

Five.

Back at the grid, the thing that mattered was no longer the names. Five separate acts of tampering. More than one person for sure. Probably five completely different motivations.

Some boxes wanted to fill themselves in. Nate belonged to the voicemail unless Martin's phone had learned his passcode on its own. Cliff leaned hard toward the safe. The missing prescriptions narrowed to one man who wanted his bottles back in a bag before a deputy opened the cabinet. Tommy stood too close to the missing sildenafil. The body and the wipe marks still looked like one panic in one room, but suspicion was not proof.

Her glasses came off again. The chain clinked against the table.

For thirty-six hours she'd been working this on the assumption that somebody had either killed Martin Kessler or helped cover up his killing. Every instinct she'd developed over twenty years told her that a scene this contaminated pointed to one plan, one motive, one person leaving his fingerprints all over the aftermath.

The grid said otherwise: five separate panics layered over each other so sloppily they looked coordinated, with half the men in the house probably never knowing who else had padded down that hallway before dawn.

For a moment the idea sat there by itself, and then she laughed. It came out louder than she'd expected in the empty kitchen, a genuine helpless sound that she covered with her hand out of habit. There was no one to hear her, but the habit persisted, the way her cats still knocked things off the nightstand even though she'd stopped keeping things on the nightstand three years ago.

This wasn't a conspiracy. It was a committee that never met.

Five middle-aged men with bad knees, reading glasses, weak bladders, and private humiliations, sneaking through the same lake house between two and six in the morning. Somebody in the bedroom straightening Martin's body while Nate worried over a voicemail that had seemed funny at eight-forty-seven and threatening by three-fifteen. Cliff in the study with the safe. One man slipping bottles out of the upstairs cabinet. Tommy carrying a problem out of Martin's room and into the half bath. All of them hearing the same floorboards, the same doors, the same third stair from the top. All of them freezing when they heard somebody else in the dark. All of them deciding, with the confidence unique to frightened men, that the sound had to be nothing.

It was the most absurd case she'd ever worked. Once she'd investigated a snowmobile reported stolen in Richville that turned out to have been repossessed by the dealership, reported stolen by the owner who was six months behind on payments, and then actually stolen by the dealership owner's nephew, who rode it across Otter Tail Lake in March and went through the ice forty yards from shore.

This was better. Panic made people fix only the part with their fingerprints on it. It meant the lies wouldn't line up. One man would remember a door, another a flush, a third would swear the hallway had been empty because he needed it to have been empty.

She opened the refrigerator and looked at the casserole dish she'd brought Saturday morning, after the call came in about a death at a lake house on Elbow. Somebody died, she cooked something, she brought it. The foil was still on top, crimped neatly at the edges. Nobody had touched it. Twenty years of bringing casseroles to grief and she could count on one hand the number that actually got eaten, but she kept making them because the gesture mattered, or because she liked the cooking, or because it was easier to walk into a room full of suspects holding a Pyrex dish than holding a badge.

The fridge door closed again. It was probably better the second day. It usually was.

Back at the table, she wrote in her notebook: "Natural death. Separate tamperings. Rebuild bedroom/hall traffic." She underlined "bedroom/hall traffic" twice, then stopped with the pen still touching the paper.

Phone, safe, cabinet: those were starting to settle. The bedroom wasn't.

The body work and the wipe marks belonged to one panic. Nate's voicemail was deleted at 3:15 AM, after Martin died and before anyone called police. The remaining sildenafil had started in Martin's room before it ended in a toilet. Three separate errands touched the same space around the same dead man, and no one she'd spoken to had given her a clean account of who saw whom in that room, or in the hallway outside it, while the house was awake and hiding from itself.

She needed sightlines: the bed, the bathroom door, the top stair, the stretch of hall a kneeling man could hear better than see.

Notebook closed, blazer on, she went to find someone precise.

---

Mallick found Paul Decker on the back deck, sitting in one of the Adirondack chairs with a beer he wasn't drinking. The September evening had turned cool enough to see breath, and Paul had a fleece pullover zipped to his throat. The lake was a flat dark sheet beyond the tree line. A loon called once from far across the water, and the sound hung there until Mallick let the screen door bang shut behind her.

"Oh, Mr. Decker. I'm sorry. I didn't realize anyone was still out here."

Of course she had. He'd been visible through the kitchen window for the last twenty minutes.

"Just getting some air," Paul said. He didn't look at her.

"I don't blame you. It's close in there tonight." Mallick settled into the Adirondack chair beside his, which required a small negotiation with the chair and with gravity. "Would you look at that lake, though. My mother had a place up near Park Rapids when I was growing up. Not as nice as this. An A-frame, though. She loved an A-frame."

Paul glanced at her. Calculation crossed his face: whether this was a conversation or an interview, whether Park Rapids had wandered in here honestly or tactically. Paul Decker was the kind of man who calculated because his profession ran on risk assessment and the accurate categorization of incoming information.

"Martin's parents built this one in the seventies," he said.

"You can tell. The paneling's got that warm look. Pine, is it?"

"Knotty pine."

"Knotty pine. There we go." With a tug at her blazer against the chill, she said, "I should let you sit in peace. I just had a question, and it's probably nothing. You know me and my questions."

Paul set his beer on the armrest. His posture shifted by a degree somebody less attentive would've missed. The shoulders squared. The spine straightened against the chair's slant. "Sure."

"I was looking back over Saturday morning, the sequence of events. I wanted to clarify one thing about Martin's phone. Did you happen to notice anyone near it after you found him?"

Paul's brow creased. It was not the crease of a man building a lie. It was the crease of a man searching an honest memory and finding nothing there. "His phone? No. I wasn't thinking about his phone. I was with him. With Martin. I was trying to—" He stopped. "I was in the bedroom."

"Of course you were. You were right there with him. So if anybody picked it up after that, you didn't see it."

"No." He rubbed his thumb against the wet label on the bottle. "No, I didn't."

Mallick nodded and looked out at the lake as if the answer had put a pleasant little check mark in her notebook.

"While I'm bothering you, the upstairs bathroom. The one you all share."

"What about it?"

"Did you happen to notice what was in the medicine cabinet before Saturday morning? Friday, say."

He frowned. "No. I don't think so. We've got our own stuff in our bags, mostly. The cabinet's more for communal things. Tylenol. Toothpaste. That kind of stuff."

"Sure. And you wouldn't know if somebody took prescription bottles out of there after Friday night."

"No." He paused. "Why? Is there something about the bathroom?"

"Probably not. I'm just checking boxes. You know how it is. Paperwork. My office wants every little thing accounted for."

Paul sat with that. A gust moved through the pines and then was gone.

"Can I ask you something?" he said.

"Of course."

"The safe in the study. Did somebody open it?"

She looked at him. This was useful. She hadn't asked about the safe, and he'd brought it up on his own.

"What makes you ask about the safe?"

"Martin always left the dial one click off. He did it every year. He'd close it and then not quite spin it all the way. We all knew." Paul took a breath. "If somebody was going through the house that night, they would've found it. I should've mentioned it before. I didn't think of it until today."

"And you didn't go into the study that night."

"No."

"But you knew about the safe."

"Everybody knew about the safe. Martin showed it to us years ago. He was proud of it. The combination, the mounting, the whole thing. He'd show somebody and say don't tell anyone, and then he'd show the next person. We'd all pretend we hadn't seen it before." The faintest smile touched his mouth and disappeared. "That was Martin."

"That's nice," she said, and she meant it.

"So you didn't go in the study. You didn't notice the medicine cabinet. And you weren't near Martin's phone."

Paul looked at her then. He was still holding the beer, but his hand had stopped moving. Condensation ran over his knuckles and he didn't seem to notice.

The pattern reached him a beat later. Phone, medications, safe: three separate questions in three separate parts of the house.

"Detective," he said.

"Mm-hmm?"

"You're asking about different parts of the house."

"I am."

"Because you don't think it was one person."

She let the silence hold.

"Mr. Decker." She was looking at the lake. "I think you straightened Martin."

Paul's hand tightened on the bottle.

"The headboard. The doorframe. The mattress edge. You wiped those."

"I moved him," he said. "His arm was hanging off the bed. The sheet was twisted around his—I straightened him out. And I wiped what I'd touched." He swallowed. "Not well."

"Why?"

"Because my DNA was all over a—" He stopped. "Exposure. I wasn't thinking straight. I was thinking like an adjuster instead of a—" He rubbed his face. "My best friend was dead and I was on my knees worrying about my prints."

"And the phone?"

"No."

"The medicine cabinet?"

"No."

"The safe?"

"None of that was mine." His voice had gone flat. "You already knew that."

"I did."

"So other people were in there. Doing their own—"

"At least."

He stared at the water. His jaw worked once. The beer was still in his hand. He hadn't drunk from it.

"I heard people that night," he said. "Moving around. Doors."

"And you told yourself it was the house."

"Yeah." He let out a breath. It hung in the cool air for a moment and dissolved. "It was a natural death. The ME said so."

"The ME said so."

"Then why are you still—"

"From where you were kneeling, how much of the hallway could you actually see?"

Paul blinked. "Not much. The bed blocks part of it."

"The bathroom door?"

"If it was open, maybe the edge."

"The stairs?"

"The top of them. Not the landing."

"So you get sound before shape."

"Yeah." He swallowed. "Yeah, that's right."

"Martin's phone was handled at 3:15." She let that sit. "And the pills had to come out of his room before they ended up in the half bath."

Paul stared at the water.

"You were in that bedroom. Or just outside it."

"I know where I was."

"So either people were moving through that space without you seeing them, or somebody's lying to me about when they were there."

Paul said nothing.

"I still think it was a natural death," she said. "But I don't close a file with that much traffic around the body unaccounted for."

He looked back out at the lake. Somewhere deeper in the house, a floorboard popped and settled.

"The casserole," Mallick said. "Did you try it?"

"What?"

"My casserole. The green bean. It's in the fridge."

"No." He blinked. "No, I didn't."

"It's better the second day," she said. She stood from the Adirondack chair with a small graceless push off both armrests that she'd stopped being embarrassed by years ago. "You should try it. There'll be a third day too, the way things are going."

She went back inside. The screen door banged behind her. Paul stayed on the deck with his warming beer.

In the kitchen Mallick sat back down at the table and opened her notebook. Under body/surfaces she wrote: "Paul admitted move + wipe after CPR." On the next line: "Phone/safe/meds not his. Bedroom traffic still doesn't close."

One square had gone from question mark to check mark. Under it the rest of the bedroom stayed messy: phone at 3:15, pills out of Martin's room, footsteps in a hallway Paul could hear better than see.

Through the kitchen window the yard had gone black and the dock was mostly guesswork.

She looked at the line again. To make it work she had to picture Nate slipping in and out while Paul stayed bent over the bed, Tommy palming the pills and getting downstairs without clipping a doorframe, grief shrinking Paul's field of vision to Martin's face and nothing else. Too many moving parts to leave as a guess.

Notebook in hand, she went upstairs.

The upper hall smelled like old pine, damp towels, and the flat medicinal note that had settled over the house since Saturday morning. Martin's bedroom door stood open. The shared bathroom door at the far end of the hall stood open too. A plug-in night-light near the baseboard threw a weak amber wedge over the carpet.

From Martin's doorway the room gave her the bed, the near nightstand, a strip of hallway, and not much else. First with the hall light on, then off, then with the bedroom lamp switched on and the hall left dim, she tested the sightlines. The room kept changing what it offered. With light behind you, the hall flattened into shadow. With light in front of you, the room flattened into shadow instead. If somebody stepped out of the shared bathroom and kept moving, Paul could hear him before he really saw him. If somebody came up the stairs and stopped at the top, all Paul would get was outline.

At the bedside she knelt where Paul would've knelt. Both knees complained. The bed frame erased most of the hallway. From that height the bathroom door disappeared completely. The landing disappeared too. One hand went to the mattress edge where the BCA had found the wiped strip. Martin's chest would've been directly in front of him. Martin's face would've been the whole assignment. A man trying to pump life back into his best friend at two in the morning was not running security. He was looking at a body and trying to make it not be one.

From the shared bathroom to Martin's door took six seconds. Eight if you were trying not to be heard. Heel to toe, she walked it and listened to the floor answer her. One board near the bathroom clicked dry and quick. One outside Martin's room sighed. The stair spoke once, low, and then shut up. A frightened person who knew the house could choose quiet badly and still think he was invisible.

That was enough time for whoever cleared the cabinet. Nate could've leaned in for the phone and back out before Paul looked up. Those fit.

The Viagra removal still snagged. Somebody had to go into Martin's room, find the pills, get back out, get downstairs to the half bath, and flush them before the house fully woke. That was more movement. More noise too. More chances to brush the doorframe, catch the light, or make Paul look up at exactly the wrong second. The picture of Tommy trying to make himself narrow enough for the house not to notice almost made her laugh again.

At the top of the stairs she listened. Old houses lied about sound. A tick in the pipe could be a footstep. A settling pop from downstairs could sound like a door on the hall. One bad sound, sure. Not a whole chain of them.

Back in the kitchen she wrote under bedroom traffic: "Can miss one in the dark. Hard to miss the rest." Then she circled "rest" until the paper roughened under the pen.

This time the notebook stayed shut. After rinsing her coffee mug and drying it, she set it on the counter next to the coffeemaker because she'd want it first thing and it was already clean and why open the cabinet twice. Then she turned off the kitchen light and went upstairs to the guest bedroom the deputies had cleared for her.

The third step from the top creaked under her weight, thin and accusing in the dark.


# Chapter 11

Paul woke at five-forty on Monday morning with the organized thought that someone needed to go through Martin's things.

Not the bedroom, not again; he'd been in the bedroom once already, Saturday morning, the CPR, his palms flat against Martin's sternum pushing rhythmically into a body that gave back nothing. His hands had been sore for two days afterward, a deep bruise across the heels of both palms that he kept pressing absently, testing. The bedroom had been police evidence, or close enough, though the deputies had cleared out Sunday afternoon and Mallick hadn't actually sealed anything. She'd told them to keep the rooms as they were. Paul still couldn't tell if she'd meant that gently or as instruction.

What he meant was the practical work. Paperwork. Martin was divorced from Linda, no children, self-employed. No HR department filing bereavement forms. No corporate life insurance packet with a named beneficiary and a list of next steps. Someone needed to find Martin's lawyer, the mortgage documents, the account information, the insurance policies. Someone needed to figure out what happened next, procedurally, to a lake house on Elbow Lake when its owner died during a fantasy football draft and five of his oldest friends had each, for reasons of their own, made the scene worse.

Paul was the right person for this. The paperwork of death was his terrain the way money had been Martin's: professionally, intimately, without romance. Enough houses after enough losses had taught him how fast grief turned into forms, signatures, inventories, appraisals. It wasn't a noble skill. It wasn't something he mentioned at parties. But it was what he had.

Paul woke in the green room off the hall, the downstairs guest room nearest the front of the house. Nate and Doug still had the basement pullouts. Mallick had taken one upstairs guest room Sunday night, and Joel, unwilling to sleep beside Martin's door, had stretched out on the great-room couch. Joel was still there, or pretending to be, on his back with one arm over his eyes, breathing with the measured deliberateness of a man who'd been lying awake for hours. Paul dressed quietly and padded into the kitchen.

The kitchen smelled like old coffee and the faint botanical sourness of the green bean casserole Detective Mallick had brought on Saturday and that nobody had touched. Paul could see the edge of the casserole dish through the glass refrigerator door, sitting behind two six-packs and the container of Anne's potato salad. Martin had complimented the potato salad at Thursday's dinner, not generically, but with the excessive precision that was Martin's way. Martin had said the mustard-to-mayonnaise ratio was ideal, that Anne understood the fundamentals, that Paul should tell her Martin said so. Paul had relayed the message. Anne, on the phone, had gotten embarrassed and pleased, and Paul had felt the familiar low-wattage irritation of a man whose wife was charmed by his best friend's effortless authority over small kindnesses.

From the pot Nate had set to auto-brew at five, Paul poured coffee. Nate was up somewhere. Paul could hear the low mumble of his voice from the direction of the deck. Narrating, probably. Processing the weekend through the only mechanism Nate had, which was talking into a microphone, organizing experience into segments, finding a rhythm for it. Paul didn't blame him. Everyone had a mechanism. Paul's was paperwork.

At the kitchen window he drank the coffee. The fog was low and gray over the lake, the dock dissolving into it thirty yards from shore. A loon called from somewhere in the mist, and the sound carried across the water, closer than the bird actually was. After he put the mug in the sink, he went upstairs to Martin's room. The third step from the top creaked the way it had creaked for twenty-five years, and this morning Paul didn't bother skipping it. It didn't matter who heard him.

The door was open. The bed had been stripped by the deputies, just the bare mattress now, white with a yellowish stain near the center that Paul's brain cataloged automatically as old, pre-existing, unrelated to the death. Sweat, probably. Martin ran hot. Martin slept without a top sheet half the year and had never bothered with a mattress protector, which Paul had mentioned exactly once, in the context of resale value, and which Martin had received with the look he reserved for Paul's most insurance-brained observations.

Martin's suitcase sat on the floor by the dresser, unzipped, clothes erupting from it in the way that meant Martin had been digging for something specific and had not bothered to repack. Three polo shirts. Two pairs of khakis. Boxers. A balled pair of running socks that Martin almost certainly hadn't run in. The dopp kit was on the dresser with the zipper half open. Paul looked through it: deodorant, a disposable razor, a bottle of ibuprofen, dental floss still in its original sealed packaging.

The floss made him stop. Not because it mattered but because it was Martin. Twenty-five years of the same running joke: Martin announcing at every draft that he'd finally committed to flossing regularly, Paul asking to see the receipt, Martin calling Paul the most tedious man in North America, and somebody, Nate usually, pointing out that this was objectively the least interesting recurring conversation in their friendship. Martin's dentist knew he didn't floss. Paul knew. Martin knew Paul knew. The sealed package was the punch line to a joke that had been running since they were twenty-two and would now, Paul understood, never get another round.

The floss went back on the dresser. Pocketing it would've been ridiculous, and the fact that he wanted to did something small and ugly to him, because it was a three-dollar package of waxed floss and Martin was dead and Paul's grief was apparently finding its expression in the urge to steal dental supplies.

Martin's reading glasses were on the nightstand, the cheap drugstore magnifiers he'd been wearing for five years, the ones Paul had been telling him to replace with a real prescription. The thriller novel beside them had a folded page about two-thirds in. Martin had been in the middle of something when he died. Paul picked up the book, looked at the cover, set it back. The page stayed folded where Martin had left it, at the last page Martin would ever read, marking a place no one would return to.

The phone charger had been taken with the phone when the deputies bagged it, but the empty outlet beside the bed looked wrong anyway, as if the cord should have still been there, draped down the side table, part of the room's usual clutter. Paul's eye kept going to the blank spot and trying to correct it.

The top dresser drawer came next because that was what a person doing practical work opened first. Sunglasses. Two hotel pens. A folded map of Becker County from some gas station promotion years ago. Receipts Martin had shoved in loose, including one from Thursday afternoon for the Busch Light and the ice. Paul looked at the date, the time, the total, then set the slip back down because this was how his mind worked and he knew it and this morning he wasn't interested in correcting it.

The room smelled like Martin's deodorant and something else, the Lagavulin maybe, a whisper of peat smoke caught in the curtains and the pillow and the years. Paul stood in the middle of the room and felt what it felt like to be in his dead best friend's space, going through his things, handling objects that were technically no longer Martin's but were not yet anyone else's either. Same lamp, same glasses, same half-read paperback, and the room had gone wrong anyway. It wasn't waiting for Martin to come back from the bathroom or from downstairs with coffee. It had already started becoming a room other people would empty.

From Martin's bedroom he crossed the upstairs hall, went back down, and turned toward the front of the house. Martin's study sat off the ground-floor hall just past the half bath, with its window facing the driveway.

The door to the study was open. It had been open since the deputies went through on Saturday, and Paul had glanced in once or twice without crossing the threshold, the way you pass an office that belongs to someone who isn't there yet. This morning he stepped inside and saw at once what was wrong. The frame behind the desk hung crooked. The safe was already visible in the gap.

The door of the safe stood open about four inches, the dial one tick off locked, Martin's usual habit. Martin had told Paul the combination once, years ago, during one of those conversations Martin began with "if anything ever happens to me" and that Paul had received the way he received all such conversations: gravely, precisely, filing them away in the mental cabinet where he stored things that were probably unnecessary but whose absence would be catastrophic. The combination was Linda's birthday. June ninth. Zero-six-zero-nine. Paul remembered it the same way he remembered policy numbers, automatically and forever.

Someone had been in the safe. Maybe the deputies. Maybe Mallick. But if Mallick had found a will, she would've asked who Martin had named personal representative. She would've said the word estate three times and written it down in that little notebook with the casserole grease on the cover. She hadn't. Which meant either the deputies had handled private papers without noting it, which Paul did not believe, or one of the men in the house had gone into Martin's wall and kept it to himself.

Paul pulled the frame aside, felt the loose wall anchor give a little, and opened the safe the rest of the way.

Inside were three manila folders with Martin's block capitals on the tabs: HOUSE, INVESTMENTS, PERSONAL. A stack of twenties and fifties pinched under a black binder clip. Paul counted without taking the clip off. Nineteen hundred and sixty dollars. A small cream law-office envelope from a Minneapolis estate-planning firm. A legal-size manila envelope with RT written across the front in Martin's hand.

That was all. No missing compartment. No dramatic hidden key. Just records, cash, an estate-planning envelope, and the legal-size thing with two letters on it that meant nothing yet and too much on sight.

Paul took the law-office envelope first.

The flap had been slit cleanly and tucked in years ago. Inside was a clear plastic sleeve holding six folded pages, the notary stamp faintly visible through the top sheet. Paul slid them out and unfolded them carefully, flattening the creases against Martin's desk blotter.

It was Martin's will. *Last Will and Testament of Martin David Kessler*, dated March 14, 2023. Eighteen months ago. Six pages, typed, notarized, with Martin's signature at the bottom of each page, the firm, slightly oversized signature Martin used for everything from contracts to birthday cards. A real lawyer had done it. Not some half-assed internet template. Martin had paid somebody in Minneapolis to make his death legible.

Paul read it standing up, his back against the desk, the safe open behind him.

The first page named a personal representative and revoked prior wills. The second page handled financial accounts and personal property: the investment portfolio to Martin's sister Carol in Duluth, the car to a cousin Paul had met once at a barbecue, a donation to the University of Minnesota Athletic Department. Standard enough. Generous but unsurprising. Martin had handed the aftermath to a real lawyer in Minneapolis, which was exactly what Paul would've expected and exactly what Paul had always quietly admired. The man hated loose ends. Of course he'd paid somebody to tie them off while he was still alive.

The third page was the lake house.

Paul read the paragraph once, quickly, then again slower, looking for the comma or buried clause that would change the meaning on a second pass. A third read went word by word, because sometimes a sentence really was as bad as it looked.

There wasn't a second meaning waiting in it. There was just the sentence.

Martin left the lake house on Elbow Lake, all structures, land, and contents therein, to Thomas James Renaldi.

Tommy.

Not Paul. Not the best friend of twenty-five years, the best man, the person with the spare key and the combination and the standing invitation to use the place any weekend between Labor Day and Memorial Day. Not Carol, who'd spent childhood summers here. Not the group collectively, which would have at least made sentimental sense, the house held in some informal trust for the annual draft and the lie that traditions stayed put if enough men kept showing up.

Tommy. The one Martin made room for. The one he covered for in ways Paul had always filed under temperament, affection, group management, any explanation that let the pattern stay small.

Paul flipped to the end of the paragraph, looking for a condition. Maintain the property for the annual draft. Permit the others reasonable use. Consult Paul on major decisions. There was nothing. Tommy got the house and everything in it. Clean sentence. No side letter. No hidden little courtesy clause that said Paul, at minimum, understood the place better than anybody else alive.

Martin's desk chair took his weight with the same wood-and-leather protest it had been making for years. The armrests were smooth where Martin's forearms had rested during decades of calls and calculations. Paul put his own arms there and felt the shape Martin's weight had worn into the leather.

Paul looked at the will again. Page three. *Thomas James Renaldi.*

His grip tightened on the armrests until the leather gave a dry squeak. Paul read the paragraph again the way he rechecked an estimate he already knew was wrong, hunting for the omitted line, the decimal in the wrong place, the clause that would adjust the loss back into reason. There was nothing to adjust. Tommy got the house. Paul felt himself reduced by a single clean sentence, a claim paid to somebody else while he stood there holding the file.

Paul had been Martin's best man. There had been five separate drives to the lawyer during the divorce from Linda because the proceedings dragged and Martin refused to handle it by email. Two Christmases after the divorce had been spent sitting with Martin while he drank Lagavulin in quantities that made Paul count ounces and then stop counting because counting wasn't helping and Martin wasn't listening. Martin had borrowed Paul's truck three times. Paul had listened to Martin talk about the consulting business for what amounted to probably a thousand cumulative hours over twenty-two years, and he had never, not once, said what he sometimes thought, which was: Martin, I have my own work, and my work is boring to you, and you have never once asked me about my day the way I ask you about yours.

His name was nowhere else in the will, either. Not as backup personal representative. Not in the scotch or the draft binders or any keepsake at all a man might leave the friend who knew how the house worked. Martin had managed, with six pages and legal witnesses, to turn Paul into an omission.

And he had left the house to Tommy.

Paul set the will down and looked back into the safe. The three folders were stacked in clean order. HOUSE. INVESTMENTS. PERSONAL. He opened HOUSE first because his hands needed something to do.

Inside were exactly the documents he would have expected Martin to preserve: deed, title insurance, property tax statements, dock permit paperwork, the septic inspection from two summers ago that Martin had complained about for forty straight minutes because the county fee had doubled. The neatness of it infuriated Paul. Martin had organized even his absence.

Paul checked INVESTMENTS next and then PERSONAL, not because he thought either would explain the will but because Paul's brain hated an unresolved file. Statements, beneficiary forms, a photocopy of Martin's passport, an old Army discharge document, a handwritten list of passwords that looked three years out of date. No letter saying *Deck, here's why.* No confession. No apology. No paragraph that turned the bequest into anything other than what it was.

On a yellow legal pad from Martin's desk, Paul copied the phone number from the law-office envelope. The office wouldn't be open yet. It was six-fifteen. By the time somebody answered, Paul would either have decided to be practical about this or he wouldn't. The number stared up at him in his own handwriting, tidy and accusing.

Then he looked at the legal-size envelope.

The letters RT had been written with the kind of pressure Martin used when he was labeling something he intended to keep a long time. The flap had been worked open and tucked back in, not neatly enough to hide it from anyone really looking. Several pages, maybe more than several. Thick paper. Handwritten, maybe. Paul picked it up, felt the weight shift, then turned it over once in his hands.

If he unfolded it now, alone, with the safe already disturbed and the will still hot in him, he became one more man in this house handling a dead man's record in private. Later there would be no clean way to say what had been in the envelope before Paul touched it, or what shape his reading of it had taken with the bruise from the will still fresh in him. If somebody else had opened the safe before him, the chain was already dirty. He did not need to make it dirtier.

Back went the RT envelope. Then the will, back into the clear sleeve, back into the law-office envelope. Paul returned the folders in their order and slid the cash stack into place under them. When he was done, the safe looked the way it had looked when he opened it, except that now Paul knew what was in it and the knowledge itself felt like a disturbance.

The frame stayed crooked. So did the safe door, cracked the same four inches. There was no point pretending the room had not already been entered. Paul tore the sheet with the lawyer's phone number from the pad, folded it twice, and put it in his pocket.

---

The chair Paul sat in on the deck was the one he thought of as Martin's, even though Martin had never claimed it out loud. It was the Adirondack nearest the lake, angled to catch the afternoon sun, with a knot in the left armrest where Martin rested his scotch glass, a natural coaster he'd discovered their first summer here and used every year since. Paul knew about it because Paul noticed things like that, and Martin knew Paul noticed things like that, and neither of them had ever seen a reason to mention it.

Paul sat in the chair now. Not claiming it. Just sitting in it and looking at what Martin looked at when he sat here.

The fog had lifted. The lake was silver and flat, and across the water a pontoon boat was cutting a slow, purposeless path, trailing a wake that took a long time to reach the near shore. The pines along the bank were dark against the brightening sky. The scrap of paper with the lawyer's number pressed against his thigh each time he shifted.

The memory that came next was Martin showing up at his house with a chainsaw.

It was 2017, maybe 2018. A spring storm had dropped a branch the size of a small tree onto Paul's garage. Not catastrophic, but the branch was too heavy for Paul to move alone and too awkward for a simple saw. Paul had scheduled a tree service for the following week. Martin arrived Saturday morning without calling, a borrowed chainsaw in the bed of his truck and a look on his face that said he had already decided how this was going to go.

Martin had never used a chainsaw. This became evident quickly. Martin held it like a man who had watched a YouTube video once and retained the confidence but not the technique. Four hours went by while he cut the branch into sections and Paul stood in the driveway offering corrections Martin did not accept, and by the end of the afternoon Martin had gouged the garage siding in two places, nicked the gutter, and cut a shallow bleeding line across his own forearm that he dismissed as cosmetic. Paul had pointed out, with the measured exasperation of a man who assessed property damage for a living, that the tree service would've cost a hundred and fifty dollars and the siding repair would run four hundred.

Martin had looked at him, sweating through his shirt, a piece of bark in his hair, the cut on his arm beading blood onto his watch, and said, "Yeah, but they'd have taken three weeks to schedule it, and you'd have spent every one of those nights calling me about the estimated damage to your garage. I know you, Deck. I did this for me."

Martin was right. Paul would have called. Probably not every night, but often enough that Martin's preemptive strike with a borrowed chainsaw was, in its stupid and destructive way, an act of genuine understanding. That was Martin. His generosity was aggressive and sometimes more expensive than the problem it solved, but it came from knowing people, knowing what would keep them up, what they'd worry into a project if left alone too long, and deciding to handle it before they could.

Paul sat in the chair and missed his friend with a force that made the sulk feel petty and correct, because Martin would have found that funny. Not the will exactly. Paul, in his chair, pissed off enough to sulk about it. Paul could see Martin on the chainsaw day with bark in his hair and blood drying under his watchband, grinning at Paul's driveway like he'd personally saved the structure. That same grin would have shown up here too. It didn't make the hurt smaller. It just made Martin more himself inside it.

Then Paul's mind, because it would not sit still, went back through the years and landed on 2008.

Tommy's second kid had been born the week of the draft. Paul could still see Martin's email because Martin almost never used email for personal decisions unless the decision was already final. Subject line: `MOVE THE DRAFT`. The body was four lines. Tommy's kid came early. We do next weekend. If the deposit gets weird, that's on me. Sorry for the scramble.

There hadn't been a vote. Nate complained about flights. Joel said the new weekend conflicted with court. Paul had already rearranged things with Anne. Martin held the line and moved it anyway.

Three years later Nate's mother was in the hospital, and Martin said he'd save him a seat if he could make it. In 2016 Joel's divorce hearing collided with the weekend, and Martin told him to send his keepers and stop acting like the world was ending. Only Tommy got the date itself changed.

At the time Paul had read it as loyalty with extra horsepower, Martin protecting the tradition by protecting the one friend most likely to miss it. Sitting in Martin's chair with Thomas James Renaldi still burned into his eyes, it looked different. It looked as if Tommy had always been operating under a separate set of terms.

Another memory surfaced right behind it. The keeper deadline in 2014. Noon on Friday, clear rule, same as every year. Tommy forgot because Eastview had an away game the night before and one of his kids split a chin on the driveway when they got home and the whole day got away from him. At twelve-oh-seven Tommy texted Martin the names with a `shit` in front of them. Paul had texted back that noon was noon. Martin responded to the whole group before Paul had finished typing his second sentence.

He's in. Nobody's getting bounced over seven goddamn minutes.

Nate had called bullshit. Joel, for once, had agreed with Nate. Paul had not enjoyed finding himself on the same side as either of them, but rules were rules and Martin had written half of them himself. Martin took the heat, reopened the window, and told everyone to stop acting like federal judges. Two seasons later Nate missed a waiver deadline by nine minutes because he had "bad cell service at a Buffalo Wild Wings," and Martin told him tough shit, deadlines mattered, set an alarm next time.

At the time Paul had laughed. Nate's excuse deserved to die. Martin's hypocrisy had seemed like one more exception the league carried on its books without auditing, because Nate's Buffalo Wild Wings excuse was worse and Tommy was the only one Martin ever bothered to save in advance. Now it looked like another carve-out. Another place where Martin's fairness bent in the same direction.

There had been other moments, smaller and somehow louder in retrospect. The year Tommy nearly drove home Saturday night after Joel rode him too hard about a first-round pick and Martin disappeared down to the dock after him. The two of them sat out there forty minutes in the dark, talking too low to hear. Tommy came back mean-eyed and embarrassed and stayed the rest of the weekend. The time Martin quietly switched bedrooms so Tommy could have the one with the better fan because Tommy said his room was too hot and he wasn't sleeping. Martin told the rest of them the breaker was acting up upstairs. Paul had believed him until now.

Those moments had always felt like Martin doing what Martin did, managing the room, preserving the weekend, keeping the seven-man machine from losing a part. Paul had filed them under generosity because Martin had been generous with him too. There had been the Christmases after the divorce, the chainsaw, the potato-salad compliments, the hundred little phone calls, the photos of cracked retaining walls with *Total loss or repairable?* underneath.

It just had not been the first loyalty in Martin's life.

Paul thought about the safe again. Someone had gotten there first. Martin had kept a legal-size envelope in there under two initials Paul didn't recognize. The will was the one thing he'd chosen to spell out cleanly and officially, with witnesses and a notary stamp and no ambiguity at all, and it named Thomas James Renaldi.

The screen door opened behind him.

Joel came out carrying coffee in one of Martin's blue mugs, barefoot, his hair still flattened on one side from sleep or from lying awake and refusing to admit it. He stopped when he saw Paul in the chair.

"You been up since five?" Joel asked.

"Something like that."

Joel looked toward the lake, then back at him. "You find anything useful?"

There it was. The easy opening. Tell Joel. Pull the folded paper from his pocket, say Martin had a will, say Tommy got the house, say the safe had already been disturbed, say there was another envelope in there and Paul didn't know what it meant but it felt bad in his hands. Joel would listen. Joel would parse the language. Joel would probably get angry on Paul's behalf, which Paul might even have enjoyed.

Instead Paul said, "House paperwork. Financial stuff. I'm sorting it."

Joel took a sip of coffee. "That's not a no."

"No," Paul said.

Joel let that sit. Joel was too good a lawyer not to hear the gap between the words Paul used and the words he didn't. But he also knew Paul well enough to recognize when pushing would only make him retreat farther.

"All right," Joel said. "Just don't sit on whatever it is until Cliff turns it into a breakfast conversation."

That made Paul look at him. "Why Cliff?"

Joel shrugged. "Because Cliff turns everything into a breakfast conversation. Or a real estate seminar. Same thing."

It was a cheap line, not quite a joke, but it carried enough truth to land. Paul almost told him then. The safe had already been touched. Cliff had been weird all weekend. The connection was flimsy and maybe unfair and maybe exactly the kind of leap Paul professionally distrusted. Still, his mind had already made it.

But the will was too fresh. So was the ugly little bruise it had left. Paul didn't trust himself yet, and he trusted himself most in precisely those moments when he should not have.

"I'll tell you if it's something that needs telling," Paul said.

Joel nodded once, accepting the phrasing without endorsing it. "Okay."

Joel stayed another minute, standing at the rail with the mug in both hands, looking out over the water like a man trying on silence and finding it itchy. Then he went back inside. The screen door banged shut behind him.

Paul sat a while longer. Nate's voice started up again somewhere around the side of the house, lower now, maybe not recording after all, maybe just talking because the alternative was thinking in full sentences. A truck moved along the county road out front and was gone. The pontoon on the far shore completed its lazy circuit and disappeared behind a stand of birches.

He took the lawyer's number from his pocket and checked his watch. Six thirty-eight. Eighty-two minutes until the Minneapolis office opened, which was eighty-two minutes for Cliff to come downstairs talking, for Joel to ask again, for Tommy to hear his own name attached to Martin's house from somebody else's mouth.

No.

Paul folded the paper smaller, stood up, and went back inside before the house fully woke. At eight he would shut himself in Martin's study and call the lawyer. Until then, nobody in the house was getting Thomas James Renaldi out of him.


# Chapter 12

Tommy was alone on the deck for the first time since Saturday morning. Monday afternoon, sun low enough to turn the lake copper, and maybe fifteen minutes before somebody came out to check on him or ask if he wanted another round of Mallick's questions or start talking about Martin in that careful tone everybody had found since the body came out of the bedroom.

He had dragged one of Martin's Adirondack chairs to the rail so he could put his feet up and look at the water. A warm can of Busch Light rested in both hands. He wasn't drinking it. He kept turning it between his palms, thumb catching on the pull tab and the dent above the label. He needed something in his hands. The beer was closest.

It had been two nights since Martin. Not even three full days, and the house already smelled like stale beer and no sleep. Men kept picking up cups, setting them down, starting sentences, dropping them halfway through. Paul had spent the morning in Martin's study doing whatever Paul did when he was processing, which usually meant turning grief into categories. Joel was somewhere in the house, still angry at a man who couldn't catch any more of it. Doug had been on the couch with the same magazine page since breakfast. Nate was in the basement with his phone, which Tommy was trying not to think about.

Mallick had gone back to Detroit Lakes. She was working the case, supposedly. Tommy didn't buy it. That woman sorted people fast. She kept the tells, the mistakes, the little slips they didn't hear themselves make. She'd be back.

The screen door opened and banged shut behind him.

"Hey." Cliff's voice. Smooth as ever. "Brought you one."

A cold Busch Light came into Tommy's peripheral vision. Cliff stood there freshly shaved, loafers on, already wearing the little pleased look he got when he thought the hard part was over.

"Thanks." Tommy took the cold can, set the warm one on the boards, and cracked it. Cliff lowered himself into the chair beside him, closer than he usually sat.

"Hell of a view," Cliff said.

"Yeah."

"Martin always had taste. I'll give him that. The man knew property. The rest of life, maybe less."

Tommy drank. It was cold enough to register.

"You doing okay?" Cliff asked.

"I'm fine."

"You look like hell."

"I look like a guy who's been trapped in this house since Saturday with a dead friend and a detective who thinks I redecorated in my sleep. So yeah. Hell."

Cliff laughed, short and real for once. "Fair. Listen, I need to talk to you about something."

"Okay."

"It should've been a simple conversation. Now it's not."

Tommy turned toward him. Cliff had gone into that careful posture, shoulders pitched forward, beer on his knee, listening face already in place. Tommy had watched him do this at cookouts and draft nights and open houses. Cliff sold things. That was the center of him. He sold condos, he sold confidence, and right now he was trying to sell Tommy what came next.

"What conversation?"

"The estate. The forty Martin owed me."

Tommy shrugged. "Call a lawyer. That's not me."

"Sure, and I will if I need to. But if it gets formal, I go to the back of the line behind family and paper and everybody else's feelings about a dead man. I don't need a hearing. I need the room to understand the debt was real."

"We all know you two did deals."

"Doing deals and owing forty grand aren't the same sentence." Cliff took a drink. "You've got weight with this group, whether you like hearing it or not. If you tell Paul Martin meant to square me up, if you tell Joel this isn't me inventing a debt over a corpse, that changes the temperature."

"We were friends."

"You were close in a way the rest of us weren't." Cliff lifted a hand. "Not that. I mean Martin watched out for you. Everybody saw it. He made sure you came back every year, even when you tried to skip. You two had your own side conversations. Out here, usually."

"What's your point?"

"My point is you say Martin meant to pay me, people believe it faster." Cliff looked back at the lake. "I've got three projects stalled and one note already in default. Forty grand matters right now."

Tommy watched the water. A north breeze had come up and chopped the copper light into pieces. The air smelled like pine and lake water and garlic from inside. Paul was probably making pasta again, because feeding people was how Paul pretended he wasn't panicking.

"I can talk to Paul," Tommy said. "If Martin owed you the money, he owed you the money."

"He did."

"Then it's not a hard conversation."

Cliff nodded, but silence sat between them longer than Cliff usually let silence sit. He rolled the cold can between both palms and stared at it like he was reading numbers there.

"There's one more piece," he said.

Tommy waited.

"Sunday morning I went into Martin's study. The safe was practically open. Dial one click off."

"Okay."

"I was looking for paper on the debt. Found some. Found an envelope too."

Tommy tightened his grip on the can. "What envelope."

"Manila. Marked RT."

The lake kept moving. Somewhere past the dock, a loon called. Inside the house the refrigerator kicked on with that old mechanical groan Martin had been promising to fix for fifteen years.

"Doesn't mean anything to me," Tommy said.

"Yeah. I figured you'd say that." Cliff's voice stayed smooth, but the polish had a harder backing now. "I opened it because I thought RT was a property code or an address. It wasn't."

Blood beat in Tommy's ears, thick and hot, until Cliff's voice sounded like it was coming at him through water.

"I don't know what you're talking about."

"It was Martin's handwriting. August 2002. Washburn County. A caretaker named Ray Toomey." Cliff spoke in the careful, even tone he used when he wanted to sound harmless. "It wasn't hard to figure out why you two never discussed it in front of the group."

Tommy set the beer on the armrest. His hand stayed steady. The scar along his left forearm had started itching for the first time in years.

"You read it."

"I did."

"All of it."

"Enough."

"You took pictures."

Cliff met his eyes without blinking. The math behind it had changed. "I document things. Habit."

"Fuck you."

"Call it whatever you want." Cliff spread one hand. "It's relevant."

"You're blackmailing me."

"No. Blackmail is cash or cops. I'm talking about an arrangement." He kept his voice mild, almost patient. "You want that letter to stay buried. I want the money Martin owed me. Those goals don't have to fight each other."

Tommy looked at the craft beer, the leather loafers, the hands soft enough to look moisturized. He outweighed Cliff by forty pounds. Across from him sat a man who thought leverage was cleaner than force because leverage left his hands looking nice.

Tommy had already stopped hearing Cliff. Some colder part of him was measuring instead. It measured the distance to the dock and what sound carried over water. It measured how long it would take to walk Cliff down there under some bullshit excuse, shove him off the side, and hold him under if he came back up choking. The thought stayed put.

"What exactly do you want?" Tommy asked.

"Simple version? I want you to move the room." Cliff leaned back, relieved to be on terms he trusted. "You tell Paul the debt's real. You tell the others Martin meant to make me whole. You keep this from turning into six guys litigating whether I deserve to get paid."

"That sounds like litigating."

"No, it sounds like not wasting a week on performative outrage while my business bleeds out." Cliff took another drink. "I'm not asking you to lie about a number. I'm asking you not to let people make it personal because they always make it personal where money's concerned."

"It's already personal. Martin's dead."

"Exactly. Which means reason's in short supply." Cliff kept his voice low. "You've got the only vote in that room that can make this feel settled instead of ugly."

Tommy stared at him.

Cliff mistook the stare for uncertainty and kept going. Silence only made him push harder.

"Look, I'm not trying to hurt you here. I found something I wish I'd never found. But I did find it, and now we're both living in the world where I found it. So let's be adults about that."

"Adults."

"You know what I mean."

"Yeah," Tommy said. "I know exactly what you mean."

Cliff nodded, hearing agreement where there wasn't any yet. "Good. Then we're in business."

Tommy picked the cold can back up. The metal bit at his palm. He let Cliff sit in the confidence for one more breath.

"Okay," he said.

Cliff blinked. "Okay?"

"I'll talk to Paul. I'll help move it."

"That's a smart answer."

"It's not about smart. Martin owed you. He should've paid you."

"He should've, yeah."

"So I'll help."

Cliff studied him for a beat, deciding which version of Tommy he was looking at: angry Tommy, cooperative Tommy, or the too-still one. Then some private ease came over his face. He'd heard yes, and yes was all Cliff ever really listened for.

"Good," he said. "And the letter stays where it is."

"Nobody sees it."

"Right."

---

The memory never came back as a story. It came back as smell first, then temperature, then small pieces his body had kept after his mind refused them.

It was August 2002 at a cabin in Washburn County. He was twenty-five. Martin was too. The place belonged to somebody's family, or somebody's cousin, or somebody's friend from Eau Claire, and Tommy had stopped trusting himself on that part years ago. What he remembered cleanly was the feeling of being twenty-five and invincible enough to treat borrowed property like scenery.

They were outside Friday night with a bonfire going and too much beer already in them. Tommy remembered the lighter-fluid can in his hand because Martin kept telling him that was enough and Tommy, being twenty-five and full of his own strength, took that as an instruction to squeeze harder. After that, the sequence went soft around the edges. Maybe the wind shifted. Maybe dry brush carried it. Maybe the can flashed and he never saw the flash because he was laughing when it happened. However it moved, the fire was on the porch before Tommy had caught up to what he'd done.

The porch was old cedar, dry enough to go up in a hungry run. It caught with a crackle so loud it sounded amplified. It ate like something hungry, like something that had been waiting.

Martin yelled. Tommy ran toward it because that had always been his first instinct. Go at the problem. He snatched the hose from the side of the cabin and got nothing, no pressure, just a loose handle spinning in his hand. Then he dropped it and ran for the side yard where he thought the propane shutoff might be, because movement still felt like action even when it wasn't.

That was when he saw the truck.

A blue Ford pickup sat behind Martin's car, dull paint gone almost green in the firelight, mud dried around the wheel wells. It hadn't registered when they arrived. Maybe it had been tucked under the trees. Maybe they had been too busy hauling beer and talking shit to notice. Either way, there was a truck in the driveway, and the truck meant what the truck always should've meant. Somebody might be inside.

He did not know the name Ray Toomey yet. He only knew there was a cabin on fire and a stranger's truck in the dark.

He ran to the front door, grabbed the handle, and got hit by heat so hard it felt like a man shoving him backward. His left forearm clipped the metal frame and the skin cooked there on contact. He screamed, dropped back, and the door slammed shut. Inside, a beam or part of the ceiling came down. The room sealed itself. After that there was no voice. No movement. Just the fire climbing.

He tried once more from the side window and got smoke in his face so thick he gagged. Martin came around the corner then, eyes wide in a way Tommy had never seen on him before, and grabbed him by both arms.

"We have to go. Now. Tommy. Now."

"There's somebody in there."

"I know. We have to go."

"Martin."

"Look at me." Martin's hands dug into his shoulders. "There's nothing you can do for him. We need to go."

And they went. Martin drove. Tommy sat in the passenger seat with his arm throbbing and his mind doing the one thing it would never fully undo, which was accept that he had killed a man. It was not on purpose. He had not known anybody was inside. He had still killed him. He had been drunk and loud and stupid and too pleased with force to bother checking a driveway. The result was a dead man in a burned cabin and Martin driving thirty miles under the limit because Martin had already moved to what came after while Tommy was still inside the first fact.

Tommy remembered the drive in pieces too. He remembered burned skin and spilled beer. He remembered the window open because the cab smelled like smoke and melted nylon and his own arm. Martin's jaw locked so hard the muscle jumped at the hinge. He remembered a gas station somewhere outside Spooner where Martin bought bottled water and a towel and told Tommy not to touch the burn with lake water again if he wanted to keep the arm.

At the clinic the next morning, Tommy told the nurse it was a grease burn. Deep fryer accident at a friend's place. Martin stood beside him and did not blink. The nurse either believed them or didn't care. Tommy never found out which. The scar healed into a white ridge that always announced itself to a hand before it announced itself to an eye.

Three days later, in a McDonald's parking lot off Highway 53, Martin held up a local paper and read the headline in a flat voice: WASHBURN CO. CARETAKER DIES IN ELECTRICAL FIRE. That was when Tommy learned the name. Ray Toomey. Martin's eyes had gone dead in a way Tommy had never seen before. No anger. No panic. No grief. Just shutdown.

"They're calling it electrical," Martin said.

Tommy was still staring at the paper. "It wasn't."

"I know."

"We need to tell somebody."

Martin folded the paper once. Carefully. "And say what."

Tommy had no answer ready for that because every answer led straight back to his own hand on the lighter-fluid can.

"Tommy."

"We left him there."

"I know."

"We left him there, Martin."

Martin looked out the windshield. Cars slid past on Highway 53, families and contractors and people who had no idea two men were parked under a McDonald's sign trying not to become the worst thing they'd ever done.

"We don't talk about this again," Martin said.

Tommy waited for more. There wasn't any. Martin was already in the part of his mind where bad things became files. Tommy was still in the part where they were flames.

At some point after that, Martin wrote his own account and sealed it in an envelope marked RT. Tommy knew that much because Martin told him there needed to be a record in case anything ever came apart. Tommy never asked to read it. He knew what his own body remembered and what it could not line up cleanly anymore. He did not want Martin's version climbing into his head and taking over the parts he had spent years trying not to replay.

Denise asked about the scar on their third date. Tommy said, "Cooking accident. I was twenty-five and an idiot." Both parts were true. Neither part was the truth.

For twenty-two years Martin checked on him. The first few Septembers he did it bluntly, pulling Tommy aside by the shed or the dock path and asking if he was sleeping, if he was drinking too hard, if he needed to get his shit together. Later it got quieter. Martin would put a hand on Tommy's shoulder when a bonfire climbed too high. He would take the lighter fluid away without making a joke of it. He would turn off a news story about a cabin fire halfway through the weather. Sometimes it was only Tommy's name said low, with that extra beat under it. Tommy would nod. Martin would nod. Another year would go by.

Now Martin was dead. Cliff had pictures of Martin's written account on his phone, Ray Toomey's name in his mouth, and forty grand riding on Tommy's silence.

---

Cliff rolled the cold can against his knee. "One housekeeping item."

Tommy looked at him.

"The photos from Sunday are on my phone, and my phone backs up automatically. I'm only saying that because I don't like misunderstandings."

"So if the letter disappears, you've still got it."

"That's right."

"Anybody else have it?"

"Not the images." Cliff held his gaze. "I'm trying to keep this contained."

Tommy let that sit there. It meant the phone, the cloud, maybe a lawyer too, maybe not. Cliff was the kind of man who believed a paper trail was what separated professionals from morons.

"You'll get your money," Tommy said. "This week I talk to Paul. Then we're done."

Cliff nodded once. "That works for me."

"You don't say Toomey's name again. You don't come back to me with this again. No second conversation."

"Clean break."

"Yeah."

Cliff stood, stretched, and set his empty can on the deck rail for a second before thinking better of it and putting it on the boards. Even now he was staging surfaces. "I appreciate you being reasonable."

Tommy didn't answer.

"I'm going to see what Paul's doing about dinner," Cliff said. "You coming in?"

"In a minute."

"Don't sit out here too long. It drops fast once the sun goes."

"I know how September works, Cliff."

Cliff gave Tommy a quick smile, small and private and satisfied. Then the screen door banged and the house took him back.

Tommy stayed where he was. The chair itself was newer than the ritual. Martin had bought these Adirondacks the year after the fire, but Tommy had claimed this rail-side spot for the better part of twenty-five years, armrests worn smooth by beer cans and forearms and men pretending a lake view could absorb whatever they didn't want to say.

The scar on his forearm had stopped itching. Both hands lay flat on the armrests, palms down, fingers spread. Tommy looked at them as if they belonged to a man he recognized and didn't trust. They were big hands, scarred and bitten down at the nails. What could this body do if he let it.

Cliff's phone lived in Cliff's pocket. The backup lived somewhere Tommy couldn't reach, in a server or a building or some room full of humming lights and people he'd never meet. Cliff, though, wasn't abstract. Cliff was here. Cliff was flesh and bad shoes and a habit of getting close once he thought a deal was done.

Tommy looked toward the dock through the trees. The boards had a slight give under them. After dark they would go slick with dew, and the house lost sight of you fast. A man could crack his head on the edge going over. A man could come back up blind and coughing. A heavier man could hold him under in three feet of water until the kicking stopped.

He picked up the beer Cliff had brought him. The can was still half full. He tipped it over the rail and watched the spill flash once in the last gray light before it vanished into the pine needles.

Then he went inside.

The kitchen had shifted into one of those draft-weekend meals that existed because Martin used to refuse real planning and Paul refused chaos. There was pasta on the stove, garlic bread from Dent in the oven, and jar sauce improved by sausage and whatever red wine had been open since Friday. The football was on mute in the great room. Nate's phone sat faceup by the salt and pepper, charging off the wall with a cord stretched across the counter like somebody had started wiring the kitchen for surveillance and gotten bored halfway through.

"There he is," Nate said from the island. "Coach decided to rejoin civilization. You want a bowl or are you doing one of those weird guy things where grief makes you basically keto."

"Bowl's fine," Tommy said.

"Thank God," Paul said without turning around. "I made enough for eight because I forgot Martin's dead for half a second and then had to keep going."

The room went still for a beat, just long enough for the sentence to land all the way through them.

"Sorry," Paul said.

"You don't have to apologize for pasta math," Joel said. He was posted at the far end of the island with a beer and Martin's commissioner binder open in front of him, as if he might file an objection to the noodles. "That's the first thing anybody's said all day that made actual sense."

Doug was grating Parmesan over a cutting board with the severe focus of a man grateful for a job nobody else wanted. He glanced up at Tommy, nodded once, and went back to the cheese.

Cliff stood by the sink with a fresh beer already open, sleeves pushed neatly to the forearms. He had come in off the deck and gone right back into the room like nothing had happened out there. That more than anything made Tommy want to hit him. The ease of it.

Paul handed Tommy a bowl. "Can you grab the bread?"

Tommy pulled the tray from the oven and set it on the stove. Butter and garlic rolled into the room. For a minute everybody moved around one another with the old choreography, the kind you only got after years of standing in the same kitchens opening the same beers. Joel found plates. Doug carried bowls. Nate stole a piece of bread and yelped because it was too hot. Paul swore at him on instinct, then looked startled at himself for having done something normal.

They sat where they always sat when Martin wasn't there to assign people without admitting he was assigning them. Tommy took one end of the couch. Doug dropped into the recliner nearest the lamp. Nate stayed half perched on a barstool until Paul told him to either sit or start helping. Joel took the dining chair he liked because it let him swivel and make points with his whole torso. Cliff chose the armchair by the window, of course, the one with a view and no need to share an armrest.

Nobody said grace. Nobody had ever said grace at the lake. They ate in the ugly quiet of hungry men who had spent three days forgetting to be hungry.

Nate broke first. "This is deranged," he said through a mouthful of bread. "Not the pasta. The vibe. The vibe is deranged. We're six guys eating grief spaghetti while ESPN runs a Chiefs segment nobody can hear. If I described this on air, people would think I was reaching."

"Don't describe it on air," Joel said.

"I said if."

"You shouldn't even have the phone out."

"It's charging."

"It's always charging when it's also recording."

"That's slander and also technologically confused."

Paul rubbed his eyes. "Can we not do this tonight."

"We're not doing anything," Nate said. "I'm sitting here respectfully charging a device in the presence of multiple witnesses."

Tommy looked at the phone. The screen was lit. A little red waveform moved almost too small to see.

Nate caught him looking and pulled the phone closer with one finger. "Ambient only. I wasn't kidding. Silence in this house is making me itchy."

"Then turn on the game," Doug said quietly.

Everybody looked at him. Doug shrugged, still staring at his bowl. "I'm just saying. Mute makes it worse."

Nate reached for the remote and clicked the volume up two notches. A studio crew started arguing about preseason usage. The sound was bad and too bright and exactly what the room needed.

Paul set his fork down. "Before this turns into another night of everybody talking over everybody, there are a couple things we probably do need to figure out. Not legal stuff. Just practical stuff. Food tomorrow. Who's staying. If Mallick comes back."

"She'll come back," Joel said.

"Probably."

"Definitely."

Paul exhaled. "Okay. Definitely. That too."

Cliff dabbed sauce from the corner of his mouth with a napkin. "Since we're on practical stuff, I do need to put one item on the table."

Joel closed his eyes. "Jesus Christ."

"I'm not asking for a vote right now." Cliff kept his tone careful, almost reasonable enough to be insulting. "I'm saying I don't want the money Martin owed me getting lost because everyone feels bad and then three weeks from now somebody pretends they never heard about it."

Paul looked at him across the table. "Tonight?"

"When would you like me to start having money problems, Paul."

"If this is about the duplex again," Joel said, "I need everybody to know in advance I'm hostile."

"I'm aware you're hostile as a baseline." Cliff didn't look at him. "Forty thousand isn't gossip. That's money with consequences. I'm just trying to keep it from becoming a future misunderstanding."

Paul's face tightened. Tommy could see the calculation there, the claims-adjuster part of him already building folders and columns whether Paul wanted it to or not.

"I found some paperwork in the study," Paul said. "I haven't sorted what any of it means yet."

Cliff nodded like they were discussing weather delay on a job site. "Understood. I'm not asking you to sort it mid-pasta. I'm asking you not to treat it like I made it up because Martin isn't here to answer."

Nobody said anything.

That was the moment Cliff had bought with the deck conversation. Tommy felt it arrive. The room waited. Paul's eyes slid to him because Martin's name and Cliff's tone had both dragged him into it.

"He's not making it up," Tommy said.

Paul kept looking at him. "You know that."

"Yeah."

"How."

Tommy shoved another bite of pasta into his mouth and chewed before answering. A little stall, just enough to feel normal.

"Martin talked about it before. Not numbers every time, but enough." He set his fork down. "If he owed Cliff, he owed Cliff."

Joel leaned back in his chair. "That's not exactly documentary support."

"Nobody asked for a fucking affidavit," Tommy said.

"No, but since we're talking about money owed by a dead man, some level of specificity wouldn't be crazy."

"Specificity is Martin borrowed forty and didn't pay it back."

"According to Cliff."

"According to me too."

The room changed on that one. It was quiet. It was real enough.

Cliff lowered his eyes at the right second, like he was embarrassed to be defended. Tommy hated him for knowing the exact second.

Paul picked his fork back up, then put it down again. "Fine. I hear you. We can deal with it once I know what I'm looking at."

"That's all I'm asking," Cliff said.

"No," Joel said. "That is not all you're asking. You're asking us to preload sympathy before we've seen a page."

"I'm asking not to get buried in grief theater while October eats my ass alive."

That got Nate's head up. "Well, now we're talking like men."

Doug made a sound that might've been a laugh.

Paul said, "Can we maybe not say 'grief theater' at dinner."

"Fair," Cliff said at once. "Bad phrase."

"Terrible phrase," Joel said.

"I said fair."

Tommy kept eating. He didn't taste any of it now. He just tracked voices, positions, hands. Joel's leg kept bouncing. Paul's thumb rubbed the edge of his fork. Cliff relaxed enough to lean back and cross one ankle over a knee, loafer hanging there like the room owed him softness.

The conversation drifted because drift was what this group did when the alternative was admitting they were scared. Nate started talking about auto-draft settings. Joel told him auto-draft was an insult to sentient life. Paul asked if anybody knew where Martin kept extra trash bags even though it had nothing to do with anything. Doug said they were under the sink, voice so low they made him repeat it. For five or six minutes the room almost found its old stupid shape.

The almost was the dangerous part.

Tommy took his bowl to the kitchen when he was done. Paul was already at the sink running water over plates with more force than the plates required.

"You okay?" Tommy asked.

Paul gave a short laugh that wasn't one. "Not especially."

"Yeah."

Paul kept spraying sauce from a plate. "Did Martin really talk to you about Cliff's money?"

Tommy dried his hands on a dish towel he didn't need. "He mentioned it."

"Mentioned it how."

"Like it existed."

"That is a dodge, Tommy."

Tommy leaned a hip against the counter. "What do you want from me."

"An answer."

"I gave you one."

Paul shut the water off. In the sudden drop of kitchen noise, Tommy could hear the TV crew in the great room and Nate laughing too hard at something Joel hadn't meant as a joke.

"Okay," Paul said finally. "Then here's mine. Cliff can wait until tomorrow. I'm not having a debt meeting tonight."

"Fine."

"If he pushes, that's still my answer."

"Fine."

Paul looked at him for a second too long. "You don't have to say fine like I'm giving you orders."

"I'm saying it like I heard you."

Paul nodded once and turned the water back on. Conversation over. Or paused. With Paul it was hard to tell.

Tommy dried two bowls because standing there with a towel looked more harmless than going back to the couch too fast. From the island he could feel Cliff watching the kitchen without seeming to. Tommy did not look back.

When he returned to the great room, Joel had the commissioner binder open on his lap and Nate had moved his phone to the coffee table beside a stack of coasters, still screen-up, still listening. Doug was pulling at the label on his beer. Cliff had traded the kitchen chair for the armchair by the window, already settling in like he knew where the night was headed.

Cliff caught Tommy's eye just long enough to tip his beer toward the hall. It wasn't dramatic, just a tiny motion that said later.

Tommy sat down.

Nate said, "I would like it noted for the record that if we kill the league now, Martin wins from beyond the grave, because nothing would make him happier than controlling the room after death."

"You say that like it's a joke," Joel said.

"It's half a joke. Maybe sixty-forty."

Paul came in with a dish towel over his shoulder and lowered himself into the chair Martin used to call the bad-back chair even though nobody's back actually improved in it.

At some point the football had gone back to mute. Outside, the lake went from gray to black without anybody watching it happen.

Cliff stood to get another beer. On the way back he paused behind Tommy's shoulder like he was waiting for somebody to move a foot.

"Later tonight," he said, quiet enough that it should've passed for nothing. "Ten-thirty. Dock. Let's finish the conversation where it's calm."

Tommy didn't turn around. "Fine."

Cliff kept moving.

Tommy watched the dark window over the television. In the glass he could see the room doubled. Six men, a dead friend's house, a coffee table crowded with bottles, Nate's phone glowing red to show it was still running, and the black shape of the dock path beyond the reflection like the house had grown a second hallway straight into the trees.


# Chapter 13

If Nate looked at the great room from the right angle, Monday almost passed for any other draft-weekend cleanup. Football on mute. Bottles on the coffee table. Six men arguing about rules Martin used to pretend he hated settling because pretending he did not love power was part of his whole thing.

Joel ended that fiction in under a minute.

"So are we dealing with the roster or what?"

Nobody answered.

"Martin's roster," Joel said. "Season starts Thursday. His team's sitting there. Somebody has to decide what happens."

Tommy was stretched out on one end of the couch, one ankle on his knee, beer balanced against his stomach.

"His team plays."

"That doesn't answer the question."
