"It answers it fine."

Paul said, "I think Joel means bye weeks, injuries, waiver claims. The team still needs a manager."

Cliff lifted his beer from the armchair by the window.

"Let it auto-manage. Let the asset keep running. No need to get cute."

"Auto-manage is dogshit," Nate said. "Auto-manage started Dalton over Herbert last year. I did twelve minutes on it because I was furious and because no one in my real life respects boundaries."

Tommy pointed the bottle at him.

"Also because no app gets to finger-fuck a dead man's lineup."

Joel stared at him.

"That is a revolting phrase."

"It's the right phrase."

"On the merits," Joel said, "I unfortunately agree."

Nate put a hand to his chest.

"Finally. Constitutional law I can use."

Paul was in Martin's recliner by the fireplace, which felt wrong even before Nate decided whether Paul knew it.

"I'm not saying the app should do it," Paul said. "I'm saying one of us does it. As a tribute, maybe."

"Tribute," Nate said. "That sounds like a district in a dystopian YA book."

"You know what I mean."

Tommy sat up.

"No. No fuckin way. Not you."

Paul blinked.

"Why not me?"

"Because you took Kittle in the second round because you got horny off one preseason clip of him running without a limp."

"I did not get horny."

"You absolutely did," Nate said. "Deck, you drafted thighs. It was a body-based evaluation."

Paul looked around the room for backup and got nothing.

"That is not what happened."

Tommy kept going.

"I wouldn't let you pick a stripper with somebody else's cash. I'm not letting you run Martin's flex."

Joel said, "The court would like it noted that those are two separate skill sets."

Doug laughed into his water, quick and quiet, then looked like he wished he had not. The pint glass in his hand was plain water, no label, no sweat, nothing to hide behind. He still had that old pharmaceutical-sales reflex of checking the room before he answered, as if a sentence could have side effects.

Nate said, "Doug, you're on the floor with hydration and judgment in your eyes. What do you think?"

"Paul should do it," Doug said.

"Why Paul?"

"Because he'd do it." Doug took a sip. "The rest of us would forget by week six."

"You think I'd forget?"

"You'd turn it into content."

The laugh got around the coffee table before Nate could get his defense loaded. He opened his mouth, closed it, and pointed across the coffee table.

"Cheap shot. Fair. But cheap."

Cliff took another drink.

"Tommy's right, though. Let the team play. Let the app handle the maintenance."

Two quick Cliff-Tommy agreements in less than ten minutes made Nate keep his face still and log it anyway. Those two usually called timeout the second they landed on the same side of anything.

"All right," Nate said. "New issue. If nobody likes Paul and nobody likes the robot, what about a committee? Weekly text chain. Somebody says start the backup running back, somebody else says no, Martin would never, we all feel bad on our phones."

Joel looked over.

"That is not a committee. That is grief with notifications."

"It's participatory democracy."

"It's seven men trying to ventriloquize a dead friend in a group chat. That sounds miserable."

Paul said, "It also turns every lineup decision into another argument."

"And we are famously so rested for more arguments," Nate said.

Cliff said, "Then keep it simple. Auto-manage or Paul. Bright line."

Tommy rubbed a hand over his mouth.

"You don't get to make him a project. That's the part you keep not hearing."

Paul's voice stayed even.

"I'm hearing it."

"No, you're hearing me talk. You're not—look, he sat in that chair and picked every one of those guys. You don't come in after and fix his picks."

Nate let that sit a second because Tommy did not usually go back at a point unless he was already pissed.

Joel said, "Then say what you mean."

Tommy looked at him.

"He's done. His lineup's his lineup. Nobody gets to make it better for him now."

Nobody said anything.

Tommy leaned forward, elbows on knees.

"You leave his guys in. If somebody's on a bye, fine. If somebody tears an ACL, fine. You don't start getting cute with matchups like you're saving him from himself. He picked that team. That's his team."

Paul nodded once.

"Okay. I can see that."

Cliff said, "Inactive-only management."

Nate turned to him.

"You said auto-manage forty seconds ago."

Cliff gave a small shrug.

"I'm adjusting."

Cliff's shrug was not really an answer either, but it was the polished easy one he was offering, so everybody let it through.

Nate said, "What about waivers, though? Are we allowed to improve the team or are we preserving his last known bad decisions like they're constitutional text?"

Joel said, "Injuries and bye weeks force moves. Aesthetic corrections do not."

"Aesthetic corrections," Nate said. "You make fantasy football sound like zoning."

"I make everything sound like zoning. Stay with me."

Paul said, "Martin would hate auto-manage more than death."

Every head in the room turned toward Paul.

Paul spread a hand.

"I'm serious. He checked weather for kickers. He benched a defense once because the opposing quarterback had 'good shoulders,' which still means nothing to me. The man did not outsource."

"That was not his reasoning," Nate said.

"It was part of his reasoning."

Tommy said, "He also held grudges against entire franchises. Guy wouldn't draft a Charger for six years."

"Because they were spiritually unserious," Nate said.

"Because Marty was a lunatic," Joel said.

"Both can be true."

The room loosened for a minute. Nate could almost feel everybody leaning toward the old shape of it, the one where Martin would bark from the kitchen that they were all wrong and then somehow win anyway.

Then Cliff said, "So Paul handles injury subs, Nate handles commissioner settings, and the team stays basically as drafted. Clean enough."

Tommy looked at him.

"You in a hurry to close this file?"

Cliff smiled without much showing.

"I like a decision."

"Bullshit," Nate said before he could stop himself. "You like selling the feeling of a decision. Different product."

Cliff tipped his bottle at him.

"That's hurtful, Griggs."

"It's market research."

Doug said, "He is right, though."

Everybody turned toward him because Doug using a complete sentence was enough to quiet a room.

Doug stared into his water.

"About leaving it mostly alone, I mean. Martin would've hated the app doing it. He would've hated us pretending we knew better too."

He took another sip and shrank right back out of the spotlight.

"Fine," Nate said. "No app. No full necromancy. Limited intervention. We are preserving competitive integrity and honoring the dead and also, somehow, still taking this incredibly seriously."

Joel pointed at him.

"We should also freeze trades."

Nate turned.

"Can you trade with Martin's team?"

Joel gave him the full Beckman deposition stare.

"You want to trade with a dead man."

"I don't want to. I'm asking whether it's possible."

"Those are not materially different questions."

"If the platform accepts it automatically," Nate said, "then technically nobody is exploiting grief. They're exploiting code."

Joel said, "We already have a detective asking why five of us touched a crime scene. I don't think we need to add dead-man trades to the pile."

Tommy rubbed his face again.

"No trades. That's corpse-fucking with projections."

Joel shut his eyes for a second.

"Again, appalling phrasing. Again, correct."

Paul said, "No trades."

Doug nodded.

Cliff said, "No trades," so fast it sounded rehearsed.

The room went still on that one. Not the peaceful kind. Cliff had that answer loaded too fast, and everybody heard it.

Nate said, "Okay. Cool. Great. Love the way a sentence can die in here."

No one helped him.

"We need a commissioner," he said, because silence had always felt like a personal challenge.

"You," Tommy said. "You've already got the bullshit vocabulary."

"I don't want the job."

"Nobody wants the job."

Paul said, "Martin did."

"Martin wanted control," Joel said.

"I think for him that was the same thing," Paul said. "The control and—yeah. Showing up."

Nobody laughed, but nobody argued either.

Nate rubbed his face hard enough to feel stubble.

"All right. I'm interim commissioner of a league with no bylaws, a dead owner, and scoring settings imported from the Bush administration."

Tommy leaned forward and pointed.

"Don't touch punt return yards."

"I wasn't going to."

"You touch that, I'm driving across this room."

"Noted."

"Seriously, Griggs."

"I'm serious too."

Joel said, "Waiver priority has to redistribute. Martin had third. That's the only fair ruling."

"And free agency?"

"Injury and bye-week replacements only," Paul said. "Not optimization."

"No one gets to decide Martin finally believed in tight ends," Tommy said.

"That was never going to happen anyway," Nate said.

Doug said, "Could make one exception."

They all looked at him again.

Doug's mouth twitched.

"If a guy gets arrested."

The laugh came out bigger than the joke deserved, a little relieved. Nate pointed at him.

"There he is. Quiet assassin."

Doug ducked his head.

"I'm just saying."

Joel said, "Inactive, bye, injury, incarceration. There's our standard."

"That is an insane sentence," Nate said.

"And yet a legally coherent one."

Paul said, "Then that's the rule."

Cliff nodded.

"Clean."

There it was again. Not agreement exactly. Eagerness for the meeting to end. Nate felt it and did not know what to do with it, so he did what he always did and turned it into noise.

"Fine. Waiver priority redistributes. Martin's team stays alive. Paul babysits it without touching anything gross. I do commissioner paperwork. Punt return yards stay because Coach is threatening domestic violence. You can replace a player if he's hurt, on bye, or in handcuffs. Anything else?"

No one answered.

Nate looked up at the red circles over the fireplace. Martin's picks sat there in Martin's handwriting, a whole season waiting on a guy who was already out of it.

Only then did Nate realize he couldn't remember the last time Tommy and Cliff had gone a full room without needling each other.

---

They grilled steaks because it was Monday night and because no one in the house knew what else to do with meat Martin had already bought.

Before they went out, Nate went downstairs for a sweatshirt and came back with his phone and clip mic looped around his wrist. The basement got bad service and too much silence. The deck at least gave him one bar and other people's voices.

Tommy saw the wire.

"What the fuck is that."

"Commissioner's tape," Nate said. "If one of you claims tomorrow I changed waiver order in the dark, I want the record."

Joel said, "That is not what records are for."

"It is what my records are for."

He set the phone near his plate and clipped the little mic to the wire basket in the center of the table beside Martin's salt grinder. The red timer started climbing.

"Jesus Christ," Tommy said. "You recording us eat now?"

"I'm recording governance."

"You're recording steak noises."

"And history."

Tommy looked like he wanted to tell him to shove the thing in the lake, but he turned back to the grill instead. Nobody else objected either. Martin had let Nate turn draft weekends into scraps and cold opens and voicemails nobody asked for for years. Half this friend group probably lived in Nate's cloud without consent already.

Tommy worked the grill in his usual stance, feet planted, tongs in his right hand, beer in his left. He swore at flare-ups, checked the sear too often, and told Nate to quit hovering before Nate had moved within six feet.

"I wasn't hovering."

"You were preparing to hover."

"I was scouting."

"You scout with your eyes, not your breath. Back up."

Nate backed up.

"You know, in some counties that's verbal abuse."

"In some counties your podcast is a cry for help."

Paul barked out a real laugh at that. Even Cliff smiled.

Nate looked toward the table. His phone sat beside the basket, screen dimming, little red bar still there. He had told himself it was for notes and because Mia's texts only came through on the deck. The truth was thinner than that. He had not wanted the night to go unrecorded. Not because he thought anything was going to happen. Just because Martin was dead and Monday still kept trying to look like Monday, and the mismatch of that felt worth trapping somewhere.

By the time the steaks came off, the table on the deck had gone from bright to amber. Eight chairs around it. Martin's chair at the head stood empty, and Tommy and Cliff took opposite ends without discussion. Three open seats lay between them. Nate counted because he counted everything.

He took the side facing the lake. Paul sat on his left. Doug sat across from him with the same water glass. Joel took the chair beside Doug. No one mentioned that they were eating a dead man's ribeyes off a dead man's deck with a dead man's salt grinder in the middle of the table and Nate's mic clipped to the basket like it had been invited.

Tommy set the platter down.

"Anybody asks for sauce can eat my whole ass."

Joel cut into his steak.

"Your hospitality has really matured."

"Steak sauce is ketchup for cowards."

Paul said, "I wasn't going to ask."

"You looked like you were thinking about it."

"I was thinking about potatoes."

"Same face."

Doug made the little huff he used instead of a laugh. It was good steak and a good line, good enough for a minute.

Nate raised his beer.

"All right. To Martin. Patron saint of impossible scoring settings and meat bought in quantities that suggest psychosis."

"That is a terrible toast," Joel said.

"Improve it, then."

Paul lifted his glass.

"To Martin."

Glasses came up around the table cleaner this time. Even Tommy did it. Even Cliff. Doug raised his water last, almost apologetic about it. They drank, and the cold beer hit Nate's teeth hard enough that for one second he had the stupid, physical certainty Martin was about to come through the slider asking who overcooked the steak.

He did not, obviously. Nate still felt the absence hit him in the chest, stupid and physical, like he had braced for a hand on his shoulder and got air. The phone kept recording. Somebody's knife touched ceramic. The lake moved against the shore below them with that quiet slap it did when the air started cooling off.

Beyond the deck, the last of the light held on the water. Birches on the far shore had gone yellow at the tips. Somewhere in the reeds a bird made one thin sound and stopped.

Cliff kept his eyes on the lake.

"Nice night," he said, in his open-house voice.

Tommy said, "You sell the sunset and the dock comes free."

Cliff gave him half a smile.

"You want me to write up the listing, Coach, I can do that."

Tommy cut his steak.

"House comes with six alcoholics and one felony interview. That's a hard sell."

Cliff's smile came off for a beat. Joel looked down at his plate. Paul drank. Three bites in, Doug quit making progress and started cutting the same piece smaller and smaller. The porch light caught the water in his glass wrong, all pale pharmacy waiting-room shine.

Across the table Joel kept flicking looks at Paul. Paul gave him the smallest nod.

Nate reached for the salt grinder and saw the red timer before the screen dimmed again. Forty-three minutes. Forty-three minutes of grill hiss, cutlery, Tommy saying ass like it was a food group. The phone felt slick in his hand even sitting still. He left it alone.

Then Joel looked up and said, louder than he needed to, "Anybody remember when Martin walked off the end of the dock?"

The whole table took that like a life raft. Nate did too. Better Martin wet and furious than another ten minutes of them pretending waiver priority was a moral question.

"Oh, shit," Nate said. "Twelve. It was twelve."

"RG3 year," Paul said immediately. "We were arguing keeper value."

Joel pointed with his fork.

"He did not slip. He did not trip. He just kept backing up like the dock still had real estate behind it."

"He was making a speech," Nate said. "Not even a good one. He wanted fumble return touchdowns in the scoring like he was introducing civil rights legislation."

"Four scotches deep," Paul said.

"That's not drunk for Martin," Tommy said. "That's warmed up."

Doug said, "Boat shoes too."

Joel started laughing before he finished chewing.

"Boat shoes with no socks. Christ."

Paul put his fork down.

"The khakis went clear the second he came up."

Nate slapped the table once.

"Everybody at that table saw more Martin than they ever asked for."

Joel said, "I want that sentence out of the record."

"Too late," Nate said. "We are on tape, counselor."

Tommy said, "Looked like somebody vacuum-sealed a kielbasa."

Doug bent over his plate laughing. Even Paul laughed hard at that, the kind with no manners on it.

Tommy was laughing too, chin tucked, shoulders loose for once, and for a second Nate thought maybe that was all this night was going to be: six assholes being disgusting about a dead friend in the way that would've made him happiest. Then Tommy looked back at his plate before Cliff could catch his eye.

Nate pointed across the table.

"No, the worst part was his face. He came up mad. Not embarrassed. Mad. Like the lake had interrupted him."

"It had," Joel said. "He surfaced already arguing."

"He raised one hand," Paul said, lifting his own without thinking, "and he goes, 'Motion still carries.'"

Nate and Doug said it with him.

"'Motion still carries.'"

Joel wiped his mouth.

"Standing in three feet of Minnesota lake water with his phone drowned in his pocket, and he's citing Robert's Rules like a goddamn parliamentarian."

Doug said, "I brought him a towel."

"You did," Paul said.

"He wouldn't take it."

"Because he wanted a second," Nate said.

Tommy pointed with his fork.

"I gave him the second because his balls were trying to climb back into his torso."

Joel put a hand over his eyes.

"That is medically disgusting."

"It's medically true," Nate said.

Doug was still laughing.

"He told me to keep the towel warm till the vote closed."

Paul said, "Then he climbed back up and blamed Cliff for the whole thing."

"I was protecting statistical continuity," Cliff said.

"You were being a cheap, nitpicking asshole," Tommy said.

"I was preserving the historical record."

"It's fantasy football, not the fuckin Dead Sea Scrolls."

"He made us vote again," Paul said. "That was the part that killed me. Full recount from the dock."

"Because the first vote happened under adverse conditions," Joel said.

Nate could feel how badly he wanted the room to stay right here, in this dumb safe pocket where the worst thing Martin ever did was fall in and come up arguing.

"Because he was insane," Nate said.

The table broke again. Laughter carried over the darkening water, louder than it had any business being, and the phone in the middle of them kept taking it all down like Nate could save the shape of the night if he got enough of it on tape.

Cliff stayed with it a second longer than the rest. He wiped his mouth with his napkin, looked down the table at Tommy, and said, "You were halfway out of your shorts before Martin even came up. Fastest I've ever seen you move."

The old version of the night had an answer there. Nate could feel it climb into his own throat before Tommy even looked up, the automatic fuck you, the insult, the pile-on that would follow.

Tommy even looked up, ready for it.

It did not come.

Tommy set down his fork.

"Anybody need another beer?"

No one said yes.

Tommy carried two empties inside and came back with two fresh ones anyway. One went to Nate. The other went to Cliff. Tommy set it beside Cliff's hand without looking at him.

"Thanks," Cliff said.

"Yeah."

Nobody reached for the joke again. Plates resumed. Knives on ceramic. Doug set his water down a little too carefully. Joel looked at Paul, held the look, then let it go.

Paul said, "What time are people heading out tomorrow?"

"After breakfast, probably," Joel said.

"I should get on the road by nine," Paul said. "If I leave later than that, the Cities turns into a parking lot."

"I'm in no hurry," Cliff said. "I've got calls to make either way."

Tommy chewed, swallowed, drank. Buying time, Nate thought. Everybody at the table had his own version of that move.

"I'm staying till after lunch."

"To clean up?" Nate said.

"To not drive tired."

"Very responsible. Very adult. Hate this version of all of us."

Paul gave him a look.

"You should probably leave early too."

"What, because my life in Minneapolis is so organized?"

"Because you look like shit."

"That is friendship, right there. Honest feedback."

Doug said, "He's not wrong."

"Brutal. Absolutely brutal table."

A few laughs made it around, not the full break this time, just enough to keep the thing breathing.

By the time Paul said, "I'm going to start clearing this," the sun was down to a strip behind the trees.

Joel stood and stacked plates. Doug collected glasses. Nate reached for the basket to unclip the mic and saw the red line still moving across the top of the screen. An hour and change gone already. The phone was warm against his fingers, stupidly warm, like it had been doing real work. He let it keep running while he gathered forks into a pile.

Cliff took the empties, plus the lone water glass, and carried them inside to the recycling bin by the kitchen door. His loafers made a soft indoor sound on the hardwood. Tommy stayed at the railing with both hands flat on the top board, looking out toward the dock.

The slider kept opening and shutting. Plate stack. Sink water. Somebody laughing once inside and stopping quick.

Nate had the phone half lifted when Cliff came back out with a dish towel in his hand. Instead of heading for the table, he stopped beside Tommy at the railing.

"Coach," he said, low but not low enough. "Ten-thirty. At the dock. I want the forty settled tonight."

Tommy did not turn.

"For what."

"To finish the conversation from this afternoon. You either back the debt or I stop keeping it between us."

"No."

Cliff folded the dish towel once.

"You don't have to enjoy it. Just don't make me drag it into the house."

"We're not doing business tonight."

"Then don't call it business." Cliff's voice stayed light, smooth, too smooth. "Ten-thirty. Dock. Ten minutes. You give me an answer and it stays off the table."

Nate could see the red timer on the phone screen still climbing between his fingers.

Tommy's jaw moved once.

"You keep it off the table," he said. "Ten minutes."

Cliff nodded like they had agreed on a tee time.

"Appreciate it."

Then he turned back into the house with the same easy pace he used for everything, like he had just asked for more ice.

Nate looked at Tommy. Tommy was still staring toward the water.

"What the hell was that," Nate said.

"Nothing."

"That sounded pretty specifically like something."

Tommy picked up his beer.

"Go inside, Griggs."

They peeled off a man at a time after that. Doug first, with a quiet good night. Joel next, one more glance at Paul on his way in. Paul stopped at the slider with a hand on the frame, thought better of it, and went upstairs. Cliff said, "Night, boys," to the general air from inside the great room.

Nate stayed on the deck with Tommy, phone and mic still in his hand.

"You and Cliff doing real-estate detention later?"

Tommy kept his eyes on the lake.

"No."

"That sounded like a scheduled no."

"Drop it."

Nate waited a beat longer than he should have.

"We're all right?"

A lone bird called out in the dark. Tommy's fingers tightened on the railing.

"Sure," he said.

Nate did not move.

Tommy said, "We're fine, Griggs."

It ended the conversation whether Nate believed it or not.

"All right," Nate said. "Night."

"Night."

Tommy went in. A few seconds later the third stair gave its usual crack, then the hall above the great room carried his steps away.

Alone, Nate looked out at the black water and the one porch light burning across the lake. It was somebody else's dock, somebody else's house, somebody else's ordinary Monday night. In his hand the phone screen had gone dark again, still warm from recording.

He hit stop. The file saved without a title.

After locking the slider, Nate went downstairs to the basement. The pull-out waited there with his duffel and the sweatshirt he had forgotten to put on. His phone lit with Mia's last text telling him to come home safe, the new long memo sitting above it in Voice Memos like any other stupid scrap he would probably never use.

The opener hit him anyway. It was habit, reflex, brain damage.

He set the mic on the blanket, opened a fresh memo instead of the dinner one, and looked at the red record button.

"So here's the thing about a keeper league," he said to the empty room.

He got no farther.

Back upstairs, a floorboard moved. Then somebody said something too low to catch, and another voice answered from the great room.

Nate stared at the tiny new waveform jumping with his breathing. He listened to himself breathe for two seconds, hit stop, and deleted only that file.

The longer dinner recording stayed where it was, unnamed. The phone went facedown beside the mic. Above him, the low voices kept at it a little longer. Then the refrigerator kicked on and kept running.


# Chapter 14

Doug shoved the last flannel into his duffel and felt the prescription bottles knock once against his shaving kit. He'd already hidden them once this weekend. Now he zipped the bag and listened to the low voices in the great room and the way everyone kept acting like tomorrow was normal.

The great room looked half-packed. Paul sat at the kitchen table with his reading glasses and his phone, texting Anne about departure times, pausing after every line to read it back before his thumbs moved again. Joel had his laptop on the couch, answering the work emails he'd been ignoring since Thursday, the blue light catching the sharp angles of his face. The draft board was still there, and nobody had suggested taking it down. Nobody had suggested much of anything today. They'd spent the day in the same stalled rhythm, too awake to sleep, too tired to do anything useful, checking flights, rinsing plates, waiting to leave.

Mallick hadn't been back since Sunday evening. No follow-up calls, no uniformed deputies in the driveway, no casserole. The implication had settled over the house like weather: the investigation into Martin's death was winding down. Natural causes, suspicious tampering, no murder charge. Doug didn't know the legal specifics. Joel did. Doug understood the shape of it anyway. Martin died of a bad heart, five of his closest friends panicked in the dark, the panicking made a natural death look sinister, and now they were all going to go home and spend the rest of their lives knowing they'd made themselves look guilty of a murder that never happened. Tilt your head and it was almost funny. Doug didn't tilt his head.

Doug zipped the duffel. The sound was loud in the room and Paul glanced up.

"You're set?"

"Yeah. Early flight." This was a lie. Doug's flight wasn't until two-thirty tomorrow afternoon, but the lie gave him a reason to end the conversation, and ending conversations was what Doug did best. Twenty-five years in this group and his primary contribution had been his willingness to go get more ice.

Nate came in from the deck, sliding the glass door shut behind him. He'd been on the phone, Mia probably, or his producer, or whichever version of his future he was negotiating with at any given moment. At the kitchen counter he poured himself a glass of water, took a couple of distracted swallows, and stared through the dark glass as if the call had kept going out there.

"Mia says hi," Nate said, to no one in particular.

"Hi back," Paul said, without looking up.

"She wants to know if we're all okay." Nate said it flatly, the performance drained out of it, which meant someone who loved him had asked the question genuinely and he hadn't known what to do with that. He drank the rest of the water and put the glass in the sink. "I told her we're fine."

"Are we fine?" Joel asked from the couch, with enough edge that it could have been a real question or a lawyer's question, and Nate didn't answer either version.

"Where's Tommy?" Paul asked, and Doug heard the question carry more weight than Paul meant it to. Paul was asking about logistics, whether Tommy needed a ride to the airport, but the name landed differently now. Everything landed differently now.

"Deck, I think," Joel said. "Or the firepit. I saw him walk that way twenty minutes ago."

"And Cliff?"

"Took his phone into the study." Joel closed his laptop and set it on the cushion beside him. "Calling someone. His office. His lawyer. Does Cliff have a lawyer? Everyone has a lawyer now, I guess."

That was a Joel observation, dry and self-aware, the sarcasm pointed at himself as much as anyone because Joel was a lawyer and the week had made lawyers of them all. Nobody picked it up. The comment died there.

Doug sat on the arm of the stairs that led down to the basement and watched Paul keep typing, Joel keep pretending his inbox still mattered, Nate stand at the sink with the empty glass still in his hand.

The room looked finished. Bags stood by the front door. Paul was still working departure logistics. Joel had gone back to email. Nate was drinking water where he'd have had a beer on Thursday. Martin's chair at the kitchen table was still pulled back slightly, like nobody had wanted to put a hand on it. All of that should have meant the weekend was over. It didn't feel over. Doug could still feel the tension in the gaps between people. Tommy alone outside. Cliff in the study with his phone. The way Paul and Joel were careful with each other in a way that was new since Sunday, a carefulness Doug couldn't identify as alliance or suspicion or the sour space between them. The group's geometry had shifted, and from the edge Doug still couldn't see the shape of it.

Cliff came back from the study with his phone in his hand and poured himself two fingers of Martin's Lagavulin. Peat smoke mixed with the general smell of the house, pine paneling, lake damp, the ghost of Martin's presence that lived in the walls the way old smoke does. Cliff drank standing up, the way he did everything, leaning forward slightly, one hand in his pocket, already half turned toward the next room. The leather loafers he wore indoors shone under the kitchen lights.

"Paul." Cliff raised the glass an inch, a small toast or a small acknowledgment. "Joel. Doug."

"Hey," Doug said.

"We should talk about the house at some point," Cliff said. "Not tonight. But someone's got to deal with Martin's things. The estate, the property taxes, all that. There's a process. I've gone through it with clients. I'm happy to help coordinate, if that's useful."

"Let's get through the drive home first," Paul said. The qualifier he'd swallowed, the _in my experience_ he'd left out, the _to be fair_ he hadn't added, made the sentence sound like a door being held politely shut.

Cliff nodded. He drank. He looked at the draft board and said, "He'd have won the league this year. Those picks were solid." Then he took the glass out to the deck, and Doug watched him go, the loafers making a soft sound on the hardwood. The sliding door opened and closed. Through the glass, Cliff settled against the railing with the drink and looked out at the lake where the last light of the day had gone and the moon was putting a silver path across the water.

Joel looked at Paul. Paul looked at his phone. Neither of them said anything about what Cliff had said or the way he'd said it, which told Doug that whatever was wrong in the room had already been discussed somewhere without him. Doug let it sit. If they wanted him in it, they'd ask.

People drifted. Nate went down to the basement and muttered about packing. Paul made a last round of the kitchen, washing the dinner dishes nobody had finished, wiping down the counter, the caretaking that was Paul's way of grieving. Joel closed his laptop into his bag and said he was going to read upstairs. Tommy was still outside somewhere, a large silent shape Doug had lost track of.

Doug waited for the slider to open again. Waited for Cliff to come back in with one more practical suggestion nobody wanted or for Tommy to stomp through the house loud enough to make normal irritation possible. Neither happened. Paul shut cabinets softly. Upstairs, a toilet flushed. Then the refrigerator kicked on and took over the room. The night felt less settled than suspended, as if the house had stopped between breaths.

The house settled into the sounds of its own machinery: the refrigerator cycling on with its forty-minute hum, the wind through the pine trees pressing against the walls, the third stair step creaking under Joel's weight as he went up. Doug sat on the arm of the basement stairs and felt the quiet accumulate. It was a different quiet from the dock quiet. House quiet was full of people trying to sleep and failing, full of the sounds they made when they thought no one was listening. The dock quiet was empty. That was what made it clean.

Downstairs, Nate was on one of the pull-out sofas, phone in hand, thumb scrolling without purpose. He didn't look up when Doug came down. Doug lay on top of the sheets in his hoodie and sweatpants and waited for the pill to do what it had done every other night this weekend.

Doug tried the breathing count the app had suggested back in March. In for four, hold for four, out for six. By the third round he was counting other things instead: the bills on the counter in Richfield, the half-unpacked box Steph had left because it contained nothing worth forwarding, the refrigerator light on mostly condiments. In July Martin had called and, after two minutes of Doug saying he was fine, started asking what was in the fridge. Doug had lied once, then given up and admitted the answer was basically nothing. Martin had stayed on the phone until Doug ordered food. Doug had come to the lake house because Martin kept asking after that. Now the only place in the house Doug could imagine breathing was the dock.

It didn't. After an hour of Nate's breathing, the lake at the basement window, and Richfield waiting for him tomorrow night, Doug got up. Nate didn't stir. Doug put his shoes back on and pulled the hoodie tighter and went upstairs through the dark house and out the sliding door onto the deck. The deck was empty. A wet ring marked the railing where Cliff had left his drink, but the glass itself was gone. Beyond the railing the lake was mostly black, broken only by moonlight and the small slap of water against the dock.

Doug walked to the dock.

---

The stone path was uneven under his feet, the flagstones sunk at different depths from twenty years of Minnesota freeze-thaw. Doug walked it from memory in the dark, through pine and birch, the trees close enough on both sides to block the house lights after the first stretch. Before the dock came into view he could hear the water under it, a steady slap against the posts.

The moon was three-quarters full and high enough to reach the path in broken pieces, the light cut by the tree canopy into bright shapes on the stone. September night in northern Minnesota. The warmth of the afternoon was gone and what replaced it was a damp cold off the lake that worked through the hoodie to his skin. Doug shoved his hands in the front pocket. Pine needles underfoot, brittle and sharp. Their smell, turpentine and sugar, was the smell of every September he'd spent at this house, and for a moment it was Thursday again, the arrival, the gravel driveway, Martin's voice from the deck: _Whitfield! You look like shit. Get up here._

Martin had noticed right away. One of the few, maybe the only one, who'd looked at Doug at the airport on Thursday and seen what was underneath the hoodie and the forced smile. On the deck that first evening Martin had asked, privately, _You eating?_ and Doug had said _yeah, I'm fine_ and Martin had looked at him the way you look at someone who is not fine and both of you know it and let the lie stand because pushing it would make it real.

He'd done it in July too. Doug had answered on the fifth ring from his kitchen and Martin, hearing the echo in the apartment or the drag in Doug's voice, had started asking what was in the fridge. Doug had lied once, then stopped. Half a lime. Mustard. One beer. Martin had stayed on the phone until Doug ordered takeout. That was how Doug ended up here, three months later, walking to the dock because the one man who'd noticed was now the first dead man.

Martin was dead now. The man who noticed was dead.

The trees thinned and the dock appeared, the swim platform at the end a dark rectangle against the water. The bench at the far end sat crooked to the boards, one leg hanging half off the edge. Near the swim ladder, a tumbler had shattered into moonlit grit, amber still slick on the planks, and two pale gouges cut through the weathered gray where rubber soles had skidded.

Then Doug saw the shape in the water.

Doug stopped where the stone path met the dock's first planks. The wood was pale in the moonlight, the grain visible, and the water on either side was dark, shifting in short ripples under the moon. In the water to the left of the dock, six or seven feet from the edge, there was a shape that did not belong to the lake.

His first thought was deadfall from the north shore, bark-stripped and waterlogged, hung at the surface. Martin had complained about those. They fouled the swim ladder. They bumped against the dock at night and made sounds that woke people up. Doug's brain offered log because it always offered the explanation that hurt less. _Contraindicated_, the word arrived before the water turned the shape enough to show him a shoulder.

It was a person. Face-down. Arms extended at wrong angles, resting on the surface with a terrible limpness beyond sleep or relaxation or any other state a living body can produce. The legs were extended at a slight downward angle, the feet nearly touching the bottom. The back of the pale shirt sat just above the waterline, the fabric dark with water and clinging to a back and shoulder blades with the awful stillness only a dead body has.

Doug took three steps onto the dock. The wood creaked under his weight. The sound felt obscene.

The silver hair. The width of the shoulders. The pale shirt. It was Cliff. Doug knew it before he assembled the evidence, the way you know someone from behind by the set of their body, the particular proportion of shoulder to waist, the details you've absorbed across twenty-five years of draft weekends without ever deciding to learn them. Cliff Haywood was floating face-down in three feet of water beside the dock at Martin Kessler's lake house, and the water kept slapping softly against his sides.

Doug knelt on the dock. The planks were damp and cold through his sweatpants. He leaned over the edge and reached down until his hand found Cliff's shoulder. The shoulder was rigid, flesh stiff and heavy under his grip, the meat and bone of a body without a person in it. The water was cold. Minnesota-lake cold, September cold, a cold that went past his wrist and seized his forearm and held it while he pulled.

Cliff's body moved sluggishly in the water, heavier than Doug expected or Doug weaker than he remembered, and the pull rolled Cliff toward him until the right side of his face broke the surface. The gash on Cliff's right temple was the first thing Doug saw. It was dark in the moonlight. Blood reads black under moonlight, not red. The wound was wide, skin split around one deep impact, the kind metal or dock hardware would leave. The right eye was open. The other eye was still in the water. One hand came with him, palm up, two knuckles flayed raw and a crescent of black dock grit packed under the thumbnail. Cliff's mouth was open too, slightly, the jaw slack, and there was lake water in it.

Doug let go. His hand came back wet and smelling of lake rot and the sweet-sour beginning underneath it, a smell with names he did not want in his head here on this dock in the dark with his hand dripping.

Doug looked at Cliff's feet. Leather loafers, already taking on water. Indoor shoes. The ones that had made that soft careful sound on the hardwood an hour and a half ago. The canvas deck shoes Cliff kept by the slider on the mat all weekend were somewhere else. He didn't walk the stone path and this wet dock in these. Cliff cared too much about shoes.

Doug stood. His hands were shaking. The tremor had a quality he recognized from the first week on the Lexapro, before his body had agreed to the new chemistry, a vibration that seemed to come from somewhere deeper than his muscles, from the wiring underneath. His knees were wet from the dock planks. His right hand was wet from the lake. Cliff was in the water. The water was three feet deep. There was a gash on Cliff's right temple. His shoes were wrong. His body understood it before his mind would.

When Doug opened his mouth, a sound came out that he did not recognize. Raw and enormous, it came from somewhere below his diaphragm, behind his ribs, bigger than any voice he actually used in the world, and it crossed the water and hit the far shore and came back as an echo that sounded like someone else screaming from across the lake. It came again. And again. Three times Doug screamed on the dock beside Cliff Haywood's body, and by the third time his voice was breaking and the scream thinned into a hoarse, wrecked noise closer to the sound he actually made in the world, which was barely a sound at all.

The echo thinned out over the water. The loons, if they were out there, stayed quiet. Somewhere across the lake a dog barked twice and stopped. After that Doug could hear only the small slap under the dock and his own breathing. It wasn't the clean empty quiet he'd come down here for. Cliff was in it now.

Doug stood on the dock and his legs felt wrong beneath him, loose and unreliable. He sat down on the wet planks. Cold went through his sweatpants immediately. Doug sat and looked at Cliff in the water and waited for footsteps on the stone path, because they would come. They had to come. The scream had carried across the lake and into the trees, and somewhere in the house four men had just heard the quietest person they knew make a noise that did not belong to him.

Water slapped lightly against the dock. Cliff's silver hair shifted a little with each slap. From a distance it could have looked gentle. Up close there was no missing what it was.

---

Nate reached the dock first. Doug heard him before he saw him, bare feet on stone, slap and hiss and another slap as the flagstones bit him.

"Doug? Doug, what happened?"

Nate's phone light hit the dock and swung across the water and found Cliff and stopped.

"Oh, shit." Nate's voice dropped from his chest to his stomach. "Oh, shit. Oh—"

"He's dead," Doug said. His voice was back to its normal register, barely a sound, and the words came out flat and colorless after the screams that had produced them. Doug was still sitting on the dock. His sweatpants were soaked and his hands were in his lap, the right one still damp from the lake.

Nate stood at the foot of the dock with his phone light on the water. The beam caught the silver of Cliff's hair and the fabric of his polo and the stillness of his arms, which were out at angles that suggested reaching, as if Cliff had been trying to grab hold before he stopped trying. Nate's mouth stayed open, but nothing came after the first oh, shit. Usually he could fill any silence with stats, bullshit, a whole panicked halftime show. Barefoot on the cold stone, he had nothing. His light hand trembled slightly and the beam wavered on the water, making Cliff's shape shift and blur.

"Is he—" Nate started, and didn't finish.

Paul arrived next, at a run, breathing hard, carrying a real flashlight because Paul knew where the flashlight lived. The beam was brighter than Nate's and when it found the water and found Cliff and the shattered tumbler on the planks it stopped wavering, pinned there the way Paul's hands always went still around broken things. Insurance adjusters look at wrecked things for a living.

"Jesus Christ," Paul said.

Paul didn't wait. He stepped off the dock's edge and into the lake up to his shins, the water soaking his khakis to the knee, and he got a hand under Cliff's shoulder and turned him. Cliff's body rotated slowly in the shallow water. The face came up, white and slack, the eyes unevenly open, the gash on the right temple visible now in Paul's flashlight beam, wider and more ragged than it had looked from above. The same light found the hand Doug had dragged up with him, knuckles stripped, and the collar twisted hard around Cliff's throat. Paul put two fingers against the side of Cliff's neck and held them there for what felt like a long time and was probably ten seconds. He moved his fingers. He tried again, a quarter inch to the left. Then he took his hand away.

"He's cold," Paul said. His voice was careful, calibrated, the voice he used for information that mattered. "He's been in the water for..." He stopped. Doug watched Paul's insurance brain start the calculation: water temperature, body temperature, rate of cooling, duration. Then grief overrode the math and left him standing in the lake with one hand on his dead friend's arm and no idea what the number was or whether the number mattered. "A while. He's been in here a while."

"Paul, come out of the water." Joel's voice came from the foot of the dock. Doug hadn't heard him arrive. He'd come down the path with careful, placed steps, fast enough to matter but not fast enough to slip. His phone light found the water and held on it with the same steadiness as Paul's flashlight.

"I was checking."

"I know. Come out of the water. Don't move him any more." Joel said it with the clipped authority Doug had heard from him only a few times in twenty-five years, the voice that belonged to the courthouse, not the lake house. His light moved from Cliff to the shoved bench, the broken tumbler, the fresh skid marks near the ladder, then back to the body. "This is a crime scene."

The word settled over the dock. Nate looked at Joel. Paul, standing in the water, looked at Joel. Doug, sitting on the wet planks, looked at Joel.

"How do you know that?" Nate said. His voice was raw and smaller than usual and the question had no performance in it.

"Look at his shoes." Joel crouched at the edge of the dock and aimed his light at Cliff's feet. The leather loafers were visible beneath the surface, the dark leather taking on water, the thin soles entirely wrong for the stone path and the dock wood and every surface between the house and the lake. "Those are his indoor shoes. He didn't walk down here in those."

Nobody spoke. Joel's light stayed on the shoes. Water moved around them in short black ripples.

"And the water's three feet deep," Joel continued. His voice was precise and quiet, the voice that assembled facts the way Paul assessed damage, lining them up so panic couldn't outrun them. "A conscious person doesn't drown face-down in three feet of water. He was either unconscious when he went in, or he was held."

"There's a wound." Paul was still in the lake. He looked down at Cliff's face, half-lit by the flashlight beam. "Right temple. It's a gash. He hit metal, or got driven into it."

"Either way, this wasn't a fall," Joel said.

The sentence ended and nobody added to it. Joel stood up from his crouch. Paul waded out of the water and onto the dock, his khakis dripping, his shoes ruined. He stood there breathing through his nose in short, counted pulls, like he was trying to keep a gauge needle from slipping into the red.

Tommy came down the path last.

His footsteps were heavier than the others, each one landing hard on the flagstone. At the foot of the dock he stopped with his phone in his hand and the light still off. No question came out of him. He just stood there, a dark shape against the tree line, breathing through his nose like he'd run harder than the short walk from the house required.

Then Tommy turned on the light and found Cliff.

"No," Tommy said.

Low. Stripped of the coaching bark. Just the word. Tommy came as far as the first wet plank and no farther. His free hand flattened once against his thigh and stayed there. When Joel said _held_, the muscle in Tommy's jaw jumped hard enough for Doug to see it in the bad light.

"Tommy," Nate said, and the name had the tone of someone reaching for a person who might not be reachable.

Tommy didn't answer. His light dropped from Cliff to the broken glass and the boards and then went dark again. Everybody else had asked some version of what happened. Tommy hadn't asked anything. Doug didn't know what that meant. It only sat wrong.

"We need to call Mallick," Paul said. He was out of the water now, standing on the dock, his flashlight pointed at the planks rather than the body, giving Cliff the privacy Cliff no longer needed. "Right now."

"It's midnight," Nate said, and the absurdity of the objection, as if there were a polite hour to report a body, registered on his own face the moment the words left him. "Yeah. Obviously. I know."

"I'll call," Joel said. He already had his phone out, his thumb on the number before the sentence was finished. He stepped off the dock and walked a few paces down the path and put the phone to his ear.

Doug sat on the dock and looked at Cliff. The moonlight had shifted or his eyes had adjusted, and the skin was already a different color from living skin, a pallor underneath the permanent tan that had always made Cliff look like he'd just come back from somewhere expensive. Cliff's watch was still on his wrist, the crystal intact, catching the moonlight. His hands were open. The polo was a pale blue, the same shirt Cliff had been wearing earlier that evening when he'd stood in the great room talking about the house and the estate and the process and said _he'd have won the league this year_ before taking his drink outside.

Two hours ago. Maybe less. Cliff had been alive in the great room two hours ago, wearing those leather shoes on the hardwood floor, making plans for a future that now included neither Martin nor himself.

Behind him, Joel's voice, flat and clear: "Detective Mallick. It's Joel Beckman, at the Kessler house. We've got a second body."

The words carried across the dock. _A second body._ Martin had died. Cliff had been put here. Wrong shoes. Shallow water. Broken glass. Skid marks. Gash on the right temple. Somebody had done this, and the somebody was one of the men breathing in the dark with him.

Doug looked up from the water. The four men had sorted themselves without discussing it. Paul on the dock with the flashlight angled down, as if he could keep doing one useful thing. Joel at the foot of the dock, phone still in his hand. Nate lowering himself beside Doug because sitting beside the body-finder was the only help he had. Tommy one step back from the wet boards, his phone dark again, his face turned toward the lake instead of Cliff. Even the way they were standing had started to matter.

Joel came back to them. "She's on her way. She said not to touch anything." His glance went to Paul's soaked khakis and Doug's wet right hand. "I told her we already touched things."

"I was trying to help him," Paul said, and his voice cracked on the last word. It wasn't a sob. It was the sound of a measured man's measurement giving way for a second.

"I know," Joel said.

Nate sat down on the dock next to Doug. Three feet between them, but on the dock because Doug was on the dock and that was the point. Nate's bare feet dangled over the edge, inches above the water where Cliff was floating. He realized it, muttered "shit," and pulled them back under him.

The wait settled over them in separate pieces. Paul kept the flashlight on the planks. Joel stood at the path, listening for tires. Tommy stayed where he'd stopped, too still, his hands opening and closing at his sides only when he thought nobody was looking. Doug kept seeing Cliff as he'd been in the great room two hours earlier, dry shoes on hardwood, a glass in his hand, talking about taxes and process like the future still existed.

Headlights appeared at the far end of the gravel driveway, distant at first, then the crunch of tires reached the house. Joel straightened. Paul's flashlight dropped to his side. Nate stayed seated for another second before pushing himself up.

Doug stood. His sweatpants were soaked, his knees ached from the dock, and his right hand still smelled like lake water and Cliff's skin. One last look at the water: hair drifting in the moonlight, dark shoes below the surface, the gash on the right temple a black line. Then Doug turned away and walked up the dock toward the house and the headlights.


# Chapter 15

At 11:47 PM dispatch sent Mallick back to Martin Kessler's lake house on Elbow Lake, the second call to the same property since Saturday morning. She was on the couch watching the ten o'clock weather, a front pushing in from the Dakotas, rain by Wednesday, and thinking about whether the tomato cages would hold. She picked up on the second ring.

She wrote it down anyway. She always wrote it down.

She changed out of sweats and into a blazer, polo, slacks, and low boots. She clipped her reading glasses to the chain around her neck, took her leather notebook from the kitchen counter, and left through the garage. The casserole dish sat in the cabinet above the stove. She left it there.

The drive from Detroit Lakes took twenty-six minutes. County Road 6 was empty. Deer eyes flashed at the tree line and vanished. She drove with the window cracked. The cold had that September edge that told you October was already practicing. She didn't turn on the radio.

Saturday kept replaying anyway. There had been the green bean casserole, Kessner instead of Kessler, and men lining up to correct her. Paul had the careful insurance cadence. Cliff had the smooth courtesy of a man who sold people the future for a living. There had been thirty-seven corrections across six interviews, thirty-seven small disclosures from men who thought they were fixing her mistakes instead of showing her where they'd been.

Saturday had been a suspicious death with a natural heart under it and five separate panic attacks on top. She had walked away with a contamination map and a case she could close.

Now somebody had put a second man in the lake.

She turned onto the gravel drive at 12:13 AM. Two county cruisers were already there, light bars washing the A-frame blue, then red, then blue again. Every window in the house was lit. Figures moved behind the glass.

The lake was loud from the yard. On Saturday it had been background. Tonight it sounded like pressure.

Deputy Arneson met her at the door. He was the same big, competent deputy she'd dealt with on Saturday, and when a scene got ugly he chewed the inside of his cheek until the muscle jumped at his jaw. It was jumping now.

"Detective. Same house."

"What do we have?"

"Cliff Haywood, forty-eight. Found face-down beside the dock around eleven-thirty. Doug Whitfield pulled him out before we got there. There's a cut on the right side of the head."

"How bad?"

"Hard to say in this light. Good-sized split. Also..." Arneson glanced past her toward the dark out back. "He was wearing tassel loafers."

"On the dock."

"In the water beside it."

"How deep?"

"Three feet. Maybe a little more right off the edge."

That kept accidental drowning off the easy list.

"Who found him?"

"Whitfield. He's in shock. Other four are in the great room. Kelleher's with them."

"Any phone on Haywood?"

"Not that we saw. Wallet, yes. Keys, yes. No phone."

She filed that and walked in.

The room she'd worked on Saturday was still crowded with men and light. No one in it looked in charge of anything.

Doug Whitfield sat on the couch with a fleece blanket around his shoulders, face drained gray by shock and overhead light. Paul Decker stood near the island with his hands folded, already arranged into usefulness. Joel Beckman leaned against the mantel and watched her come in, all the lawyer sarcasm gone. Nate Griggs paced in one narrow lane across the rug, cap still on, words building in his throat. Tommy Renaldi sat in the Adirondack chair by the deck door with his hands between his knees.

Saturday Tommy had been all movement. Tonight he looked bolted down.

Mallick stopped where all five could see her.

"I'm Detective Mallick. I was here Saturday investigating Martin Kessler's death." She let the correct name sit there. "I understand Mr. Haywood was found in the lake."

They heard it. Paul looked at Joel. Nate stopped walking in mid-step. Doug raised his eyes for a second, long enough to register the missing casserole, the missing apologies, the missing act. Tommy didn't move at all.

"Nobody leaves this property," she said. "Nobody touches anything. I want each of you separated for the rest of the night."

Paul took a step toward her. "Detective, if there's anything we can do..."

"Stay available, Mr. Decker."

She turned to Arneson. "Mr. Beckman in the study. Mr. Griggs in the basement. Coach Renaldi stays here. Mr. Whitfield stays on that couch. Keep Mr. Decker in the kitchen."

Nate lifted both hands. "I can give you the whole night, start to finish. I can walk you through dinner, who went where, who was outside, all of it."

"I'll come get you."

There was no warmth in it, no neighborly flutter. Nate's mouth shut.

Mallick looked back to Arneson. "Call dispatch. I want the BCA crime scene unit headed this way. Tell them possible homicide and a compromised water scene. Nobody goes near the dock until I see it."

Arneson nodded and stepped out.

Kelleher started moving the men. Doug got up slowly, blanket still around him. Joel went toward the study without complaint. Nate muttered once under his breath and headed for the stairs. Paul stayed near the island, hands still folded, waiting for instructions that weren't coming.

Kelleher touched Tommy's shoulder. Tommy lifted his head, looked at Mallick, and gave her a flat stare, mouth set hard.

She went out the back door.

---

The route to the dock vanished into the trees ten feet off the deck. Her flashlight found wet gravel, pine needles, roots, the dark shine of cedar rail at the bottom. It was good enough for a person who knew the property. It was good enough to carry a man down without wanting to be seen. She would need the BCA to photograph the ground before daylight traffic ruined whatever it was holding.

The air by the water was colder than the yard. Her breath showed. Lake smell came up strong: mud, weeds, cold mineral water, churned-up silt.

Arneson had left a battery spot at the end of the dock. Cliff Haywood lay on his back in that hard white circle, arranged into more order than death usually allowed. Deputies had straightened him after pulling him out. She could see it in the hands, in the shoulders, in the shirt twisted across his chest.

Froth had dried from one nostril and the corner of the mouth. His eyes were closed. She would have preferred them open.

She crouched, pulled on gloves, and started with the shoes.

They were leather loafers, tasseled and expensive. Cliff wore them in the house. She remembered the click of them on Martin's hardwood on Saturday. Even his worst weekends had a dress code. Sunday afternoon she had seen him cross the deck in beat-up boat shoes with wet-darkened seams and soft rubber soles.

These were not dock shoes.

She lifted one foot. The sole was clean through the arch. No grit clung to it, no wet sand worked into the edge, and no pine needles stuck to the leather. There was only a scuff at the toe and the drag-smear of somebody else's handling.

Either Cliff hadn't walked down here in those shoes or somebody had changed them after he was dead.

She lowered the foot and moved to the wound.

The split sat on the right temple, a little above and behind the ear, about two inches long. It was clean enough to be hard-edge impact, not rock and not branch. She looked at the railing. The wood showed old splinters, weathered gray, nothing fresh. She looked at the bench at the end of the dock. The bench was smooth, with no blood, no hair, and no scrape she trusted.

If he had fallen on his own, the wound should have argued for it: forehead, chin, bridge of the nose, maybe a shoulder. This one sat too far back, on the side and behind, not where a simple fall would land.

She shined her light over the edge of the dock. The beam reached bottom fast, finding pale sand close in, then a seam of churned silt, then darker water beyond. It was three feet, maybe a little more where the dock dropped off. A sober grown man who wanted to live should have stood up in it. A stunned man could drown there. So could a man with somebody's hand on the back of his neck while the fight ran out of him.

The froth mattered too. It wasn't definitive without the autopsy, but it pushed the same way. He had taken water in alive or close enough to it.

Not proof yet. Still enough to move accident off the easy list.

She stood and backed up a step. In the white light, Cliff's body looked split between two stories. The dock said slip, stumble, bad night, cold water. The loafers said indoors. The wound said help.

She walked the planks to the far end and back again. Nothing looked obvious on the bench, and nothing lay loose on the swim platform. At shore, where gravel gave up and grass started, her light caught half a boot print. It was bigger than Cliff's shoe. It was clear enough to note, not clear enough to own. It could be Doug. It could be a deputy. It could be the man she was out here for.

From the dock, the house was mostly windows and glow through branches. From the house, after dark, the dock disappeared. The trees and water did the rest.

A person could hit a man out here. A person could hold him down. A person could walk back to the house wet to the elbows and never be heard over the lake. Whoever did it was strong enough to manage Cliff Haywood in the water, desperate enough to do it this close to the house, and careless enough to put the wrong shoes back on him.

She took one last look at Cliff's face, then turned back toward the lights.

---

The kitchen table was where she set up. It was the same chair as Saturday, under the same cheap overhead fixture. The old refrigerator kicked on with its forty-minute wheeze as she opened her notebook to the blue-tabbed section marked *Kessler residence, corrections.*

She did not need to reread the whole page. The tags were enough.

Decker had corrected the body position.

Haywood had corrected the study location.

Griggs had exposed the voicemail.

Renaldi had said little, corrected nothing, and kept his hands busy.

Whitfield had shut down.

Beckman had known the timeline clean.

Saturday the map had told her who touched what after Martin died. Tonight it might tell her who had access, who had leverage, and who was stupid enough to panic twice in the same house.

Cliff's correction still mattered most. He had known Martin's study too fast because he had been in it. At the time she had filed it under money. Martin owed Cliff forty thousand dollars. A dead man with an unsecured safe could make an idiot out of anybody.

Money still might explain it.

Paper could explain it better.

She wrote one line on a fresh page. *Haywood was in the study. Reopen that path.*

Then she started with Doug Whitfield.

Doug sat back down with the blanket pulled high and both hands around a mug of water he wasn't drinking. Up close the gauntness in his face looked worse than it had on Saturday. The lake had left a dark line halfway up his sweatpants.

"Mr. Whitfield. Start with when you went down there."

Doug swallowed. "Around eleven-thirty. Maybe a little before."

"You go every night?"

"Most nights."

"Why."

He looked at her over the rim of the mug. "It's quieter."

"To smoke?"

"Sometimes. Mostly just to sit."

"Who knows you do that."

Doug gave a tiny shrug under the blanket. "Everybody, probably. I disappear a lot."

"What did you see?"

"He was in the water." Doug stared at the mug as he said it. "Face down. Left side if you're walking out. I thought maybe he was swimming and then I saw it wasn't that."

"What wasn't."

"The way he was just there." Doug's fingers tightened around the paper cup. "No movement. No sound."

"What did you do?"

"I yelled. I went in. Water wasn't deep. I got him turned over and his face..." Doug stopped, mouth open for a second, then shut it. "I dragged him up. Then I yelled again and ran back."

"Did you notice his shoes?"

"No."

"Anyone on the path? Any voices? Splashing?"

Doug shook his head. "No. Just the lake."

"Did you see Coach Renaldi after dinner?"

Doug thought about it longer than the question deserved. "Inside. On the deck once maybe. I don't know what time."

"Was Haywood with him?"

"Maybe earlier. Not when I saw him." He took a breath and stared at the blanket on his lap. "I don't know. I know that's what everybody says when they know more than they're saying. I really don't."

She believed that part. Shock had narrowed him down to the size of a nail.

"All right," she said.

Doug nodded once and looked back at the mug.

Nate Griggs was downstairs with Kelleher. The basement smelled like detergent, damp carpet, and a house built too close to water. Nate stood as soon as she came in.

"Detective. I've been going through it and I can pretty much give you the whole night if you want the director's cut."

"I don't."

Nate stopped.

"What time did you last see Cliff Haywood alive?"

"Dinner. For sure dinner. Six-thirty to, I don't know, seven-fifteen, seven-twenty. Everybody was there. After that it broke up. Some guys stayed upstairs, some went out back. I saw Cliff on the deck later when I came up to use the bathroom. Maybe nine. Nine-fifteen."

"Who was with him?"

Nate tugged the bill of his cap. "I think he was alone. Maybe Paul had just come in. I can't swear to it."

"How was he acting at dinner."

"Normal Cliff, which means if you hate salesmen he'd make you want to hit him with a chair. Real smooth. Real helpful. He kept doing that thing where he talks low to one guy like he's giving out state secrets and really he's just cornering him."

"One guy being Tommy."

"Yeah." Nate's mouth twisted. "At dinner Cliff leaned in and said something to him—right in his ear—and Tommy didn't move. Didn't tell him to back off, didn't shove his chair, nothing. Just sat there with his fork in his hand looking at his plate. And Tommy always—I mean, you met him Saturday. That's not how he works."

"You were recording dinner."

His face changed at once. The performance pulled back. What remained was the man under it, smaller and more alarmed.

"Yeah."

"How long did you record?"

"Dinner. Waiver wire talk. Mostly bullshit." Nate rubbed his palm on his shorts. "I usually let it run a little after because sometimes somebody says one useful thing when they think we're done."

"Did you stop it yourself?"

"I think so. I mean, I thought so. I'd have to look at the file."

"I'd like the phone."

"The whole thing?"

"The whole thing."

Nate winced, then reached into his pocket and handed it over. "Passcode's 4421."

"Any other audio from tonight?"

"Probably. Maybe. If there is, it's because I forgot to kill the app all the way. That's not me hiding anything. That's me being bad at software and worse at boundaries."

"I'll sort it out."

Nate nodded. "Okay."

"Did you notice any trouble between Cliff and Tommy?"

"Trouble like yelling? No. Trouble like one guy talking too low and the other guy acting like his skin didn't fit? Yeah."

"When?"

"All day. Worse at dinner."

She wrote that down, bagged the phone, and went back upstairs.

Joel Beckman was in the study, sitting in Martin's desk chair with his hands on the chair arms instead of the desk. He looked careful without making a show of being careful.

"Mr. Beckman."

"Detective." Joel stood. "No casserole this time."

"No."

That answer seemed to satisfy him more than silence would have.

"When did you last see Cliff Haywood?"

"Dinner. Roughly six-thirty to seven-fifteen. I went upstairs after that. I came down when Doug screamed."

"What time?"

"Eleven thirty-two. I checked my phone."

The precision sounded native to him, not built for her.

"Saturday Haywood corrected me about this room before I'd named it," she said. "He knew Martin's study because he'd been in it. Did he sound like a man chasing forty thousand, or a man who'd found paper?"

Joel considered that. "Paper."

"Why."

"Because forty thousand makes Cliff loud. This was quiet."

"All day?"

"Since Sunday, really. Tonight it got hard to miss."

"Any tension today between Cliff and anyone else?"

"Tommy."

"How so?"

"Too polite. That's the first part. Cliff gets smooth when he wants something. Tommy gets louder when he wants out of a conversation. Today Cliff was smooth and Tommy was quiet."

"Did you hear an argument?"

"No." Joel shifted his weight. "But yesterday Cliff was bringing up the forty thousand in front of everybody. Today he stopped. Every conversation with Tommy was private."

She wrote that down too.

"Why the shift?"

"Couldn't tell you." Joel's mouth tightened for half a second. "But he found something in that study. Start with Tommy."

"I'm starting with everybody."

"Sure."

He didn't believe her, which was fine. She didn't need belief from Joel Beckman. She needed time stamps and clean observations, and he'd just given both.

Paul Decker was waiting where she had left him, by the sink with his sleeves rolled once and his hands braced on the edge of the counter. He had found something to do while he waited. Four clean glasses sat upside down on a towel by the dish rack, lined into a row that nobody needed.

"Mr. Decker."

"Sure."

"Walk me through dinner and after."

Paul nodded immediately, almost with relief. "Dinner started a little after six-thirty. Doug grilled the steaks. Nate talked almost the whole time. Cliff had two bourbons, maybe two and a half. Tommy drank beer. Joel barely touched anything. By seven-fifteen people were done eating. Seven-twenty, maybe. I was clearing plates."

"You know the time."

"The microwave clock said 7:19 when I started loading the dishwasher." He glanced at the appliance as if it might still back him up. "I remember because I was thinking if I cleaned up right away maybe the house would feel normal for five minutes."

"Did it."

"No."

That was the first blunt sentence he had given her all night.

"Tell me about Haywood and Renaldi."

Paul exhaled through his nose. "Cliff kept finding him. That's the simplest version. By the grill. On the deck. In the hallway by the downstairs bath. He'd come over quiet and Tommy would look like he was bracing for weather."

"Did you hear what was said."

"Not all of it. Money once. Martin's name more than once. I heard Cliff say 'this place' at one point. Tommy told him not now."

"What time was that."

"Around eight on the deck. I think." Paul rubbed a thumb against the counter seam. "Then again later."

"Later meaning."

"Ten twenty-two. Maybe ten twenty-three."

"That's specific."

"I was standing right here deciding whether it was too late to make coffee." He gave the ghost of a humorless smile. "That sounds ridiculous, but that's what I was doing. Tommy went out the slider with no jacket. Empty hands. A minute or two later Cliff followed with his drink and his phone."

"You saw the phone."

"Yes. He had it at dinner too. Set it next to his plate. The screen kept lighting up and he kept turning it facedown."

"Did you see either man come back in."

"I saw Tommy later in the great room. I can't give you the minute. Cliff, no." Paul's eyes moved toward the dark glass of the deck door. "I didn't clock the gap when it mattered. I should have."

"What about disagreement. Yelling, threats, anything more obvious."

"Not obvious." He straightened a little. "But after the deck conversation around eight, Tommy came in and went straight to the slider and checked the lock. Pulled it twice. Then he stood right here in the kitchen with his hands on the counter and didn't do anything for maybe a minute."

"You noticed that."

"I was five feet away." Paul looked at his own hands on the counter edge. "You can't miss a man checking a lock twice in a house where somebody already died."

"You knew Cliff said Martin owed him money."

"Forty thousand from the Roseville duplex. He'd mentioned it before Martin died and after. More after." Paul looked toward the study, then back at her. "He thought the group should treat the debt as real. He wasn't wrong."

"Would Renaldi care about that debt."

"Not enough to kill a man over it." Paul heard how fast he answered and corrected himself. "Not that I know of."

She wrote a few short lines. Decker saw Renaldi go to the deck at 10:22. Haywood followed with his phone. The pressure had stayed quiet all night. The forty-thousand-dollar debt was still live.

"One more thing," she said. "What was Renaldi wearing."

"Dark sweatshirt. Jeans. Work boots, I think. Mud on them earlier from outside."

That matched the half print on the shore closely enough to make the skin across her shoulders tighten.

"Thank you."

Paul nodded. "Do you want the others' phones on the table? I can get that done."

"No. I'll get it done."

He accepted the correction without arguing. That was how frightened he actually was.

Tommy was where she'd left him, in the same chair and the same posture. His hands were still jammed between his knees like he didn't trust them loose. His sleeves were shoved up. The pale scar along the left forearm showed clean under the light.

"Coach Renaldi."

"Yeah."

"Where were you between ten and midnight?"

"In bed."

"What time did you go up?"

"Nine. Nine-thirty."

"Did you leave the room after that?"

"No."

"Did you talk to Cliff Haywood after dinner."

"No."

"Did you hear Cliff go out? Anybody go out?"

"No."

He didn't hedge, didn't add, and didn't perform indifference. Every answer came flat and clean. Tommy was usually louder than this or already leaving the room. This version of him was wrong.

"Saturday Haywood corrected me about Martin's study before I'd finished the question," she said. "He knew that room. What did he find in it that had him on you all day?"

Tommy's head came up. "He didn't show me shit."

"I asked what he found."

"I heard you."

"So he found something."

"No." He looked away again. "I said he didn't show me shit."

That was the most life she'd gotten out of him yet, and it had come on the study question.

"Any disagreement today?"

"No."

Saturday he had been half-motion even standing still. Tonight every muscle was working on containment.

"What shoes were you wearing tonight."

His eyes flicked to hers for the first time. "Boots."

"Where are they."

"Mudroom."

"What kind."

"Brown work boots."

"And what were you wearing when you went upstairs."

"This." He looked down at himself like the answer annoyed him. "Jeans. Sweatshirt. Same thing."

She asked a few more narrow questions and got the same dead-flat answers back: no witnesses, no movement, no conflict, in bed by nine-thirty, nothing to add.

He had the size for it, and he was holding himself so still it looked painful. Neither one was evidence. The case wasn't going to hold on an old scar and a bad feeling. But the stillness was working too hard. He was not calm. He was bracing.

"All right," she said. "I'll speak to you again in a little bit."

That got a reaction. Tommy looked up.

"Thought you said morning."

"I changed my mind."

He stared at her another second, then looked at the floor again. "Yeah."

When she got back to the kitchen, the first BCA van was coming up the drive. Tires crunched on gravel, doors thudded shut, and clipped practical voices carried in from outside. The house heard it too. The rooms seemed to contract around the sound.

The study question kept pressing at her. If Cliff had turned whatever he'd found in Martin's study into something portable, it was on a screen now or gone.

Arneson came in from the porch pulling on fresh gloves, cheek working again. "Techs are here."

"Good. I want all five living phones on this table, logged and bagged. I want Haywood's noted as missing, not overlooked. Nobody goes to a bedroom, a bathroom, or a sink without one of us on them."

"Already told Kelleher no washing up."

"Tell him again."

Arneson nodded.

For the next five minutes the kitchen turned into a collection point. Joel set his phone down without comment. Paul did it gently, like it belonged to someone else. Doug had to be reminded twice before he patted his pocket and produced his. Tommy held his for a second too long before putting it on the wood. Nate's was already in evidence.

Phones did this to people. They turned a room into five separate versions of panic. Paul's care was habit. Doug's lag was shock. Tommy's pause read differently. Not reluctance to hand over a device. Calculation. A man measuring, too late, what lived on it and what lived elsewhere.

Five phones sat on the table. There was no sixth.

Mallick looked at the row of evidence bags.

"Nobody found Haywood's on him."

"No," Arneson said. "I checked again with the deputy who helped pull him out. Wallet and keys in the front pockets. No phone."

"Then somebody handled it."

Paul said, "Cliff always had that thing on him."

Tommy didn't look at anybody.

That locked the study note tighter. Cliff had not just known Martin's study. Either he had copied something out of it or he had made Tommy think he had, and somebody had cared enough to strip the phone off the body.

Mallick took Nate's bag back, opened it, and sat down with the phone and her notebook.

The audio app opened to a list of files. Most of them had Nate all over them: `WEEK3_SLEEPERS`, `MAILBAG_FINAL_v2`, `MON_DINNER_RAW`, `HOT_TUB_IDEAS`, `NO_USE_1`. The dinner file was longer than he'd implied. It ran twenty-nine minutes. Long enough to catch the part after everyone thought the scene had ended. That was usually where men stopped acting for the table and started making their real asks.

She tapped it.

At first it was exactly what he'd promised: beer bottles on wood, somebody arguing about waiver priority, Nate laughing too hard at his own line, someone calling someone else an idiot with real affection under it. It was the group before the second death, the group when it could still pass for itself.

Then plates started moving. Chairs scraped back. Voices spread out, farther from the phone.

Cliff's voice came in close and low, not meant for the whole table but caught anyway.

"Ten-thirty. Dock. You and me."

There was a pause. Three seconds of silverware and chair legs.

Tommy answered from farther off, rough enough that she would have known him without the weekend of interviews.

"Not in here."

Cliff again, just as low. "Ten-thirty."

There was another pause. Then Tommy, flat and final.

"Fine."

Mallick hit pause.

It wasn't a confession. It was more than a hunch. Tommy had lied about the disagreement. Paul had placed both men moving toward the deck. Cliff's phone was gone. The night had finally done her the courtesy of starting to line up.

"Mr. Griggs," she said without looking up.

Nate had drifted as far into the doorway as Kelleher would let him. "Yeah."
